The words "global" and "art market" perform the same fusing/confusing operation which produces a series of intellectual and experiential conundrums for anyone trying to understand—"get the measure of"—the varied phenomena this world entails. Phrases such as "African art" and "art from Africa," like "emergent" and "peripheral," seem to be simultaneously factual and partisan, truthful and yet troubling in some fundamental ways. Would a magazine or art fair such as *Frieze* ever present contemporary "European art" or "American art" in the same grossly generalizing way? Would not these exoticizing terms be mocked as ridiculously indefensible—intellectually, if not as marketing devices? That seemingly innocent preposition "from" subsumes an alarming set of related interpretative problems, for example when "artists from Africa" who've ostensibly managed to join the main stage in London or New York, may have already spent many years living in the United States or France, and make their work there.55

Equally troubling is the statement by Tate's then International Curator, Elvira Dyangani Ose, who professed an interest in telling, through her exhibitions, what she calls "the story of a universal art history." The quantity/quality dilemma surges again. What Tate is doing, she claims, "is increasing the presence and visibility of African art within the Tate collection." This implies both more and different objects, and more and different ways in which these may be presented and understood. At one level this ambition is of course not contentious but laudable. Tate, and other major European and US museums, clearly should radically widen the scope of their concerns and attempt to reach new, different publics—for example, the vast postcolonial diaspora of Africans and their descendants living across the northern hemisphere.

But "universal" here suggests both integration and totalization—an impossible quantitative task that, in all previous attempts in art and cultural history. has always actually been founded on drastic, partial, if often undeclared, qualitative selection. "World histories of art" narratives, for instance, emerged in the nineteenth-century colonial era espoused by the Western national empires, sponsored by leading European and US museum institutions and establishment academic representatives. Their purpose was to make colonized peoples and their artefacts meaningful and valued within the taxonomies of Art and Culture invented by Western intellectual history and its related disciplines.<sup>56</sup>

Tate is aware of these dilemmas. Tate Modern's then director, Chris Dercon, stated: "We are becoming aware that there are different forms of modernities." How this recognition is squared with an ambition to promote "universality" remains, however, unclear. Ose herself indicated recognition of the quantity/quality problem (and its related other dimension, fact/value) in her awkward but nevertheless revealing comment that "Africa is not the only conceptual context in which artists there operate." (my italics) "Africa" and "there," it is clear, are both ideas and assumptions as much as terms that refer to an actual place or places. Given the legacies of colonial history and the present order/disorder of globalizing capitalism, how indeed could what Kodwo Eshun called the "complexities" of "African contemporaneity" be made "visible, audible and 'speakable'"?<sup>57</sup>

These are the same problems and questions that confronted me in writing this book. They cannot, however, be "solved" or "resolved" in a study or an exhibition because they actually entail altering-indeed eventually endingthe power relations that operate now, dynamically but asymmetrically, in the world and in the global contemporary art world. The conclusions and answers I've formed here are provisional, "trial-and-error," rough ones, necessarily limited by the interests that I can both declare and others which may operate still without my conscious knowledge or wishes.

Contemporary art has already been subject to an enormous quantity of writing—theoretical, critical, descriptive and anecdotal, I make reference to some of this discourse where necessary and supply further reading suggestions throughout what follows. In Globalization and Contemporary Art, as I've explained, I explicitly brought seven theoretical categories into critical alignment with groups of essays based on empirical cases. My idea was that these categories and empirical studies could start to inform, test and critique each other. Phenomena in the world are never simply present to us in some transparent or direct manner. Experientially, and in subsequent attempts at explanation, we need to use and create mediating words and concepts in order to make sense of things—although we can still be rendered speechless and disoriented by experience, and further experience can again transform the ways in which we try to understand the world. The Global Contemporary Art World has a similar objective: I have organized its contents partly in order to articulate, but also possibly to destabilize, a set of interrelated and interdependent explanatory concepts.

Several of the most important of these derive from a footnote Karl Marx added to the first volume of his study Capital, published in 1863. Human society's transformations, Marx observed, were based on the complex interdevelopmental relations of six elements (both ideas and things in the world). These are: technology, human relation to nature, processes of production, the production and reproduction of daily life, social relations and mental conceptions. These components have always existed in dynamic interaction and are not to be thought of hierarchically. Notice Marx doesn't mention economy or class, two ideas usually held to be at the determining or "causal" center of Marxism's explanation of human history. The relationships between these six elements are open and change historically, but as a dynamic ensemble they have driven the development of the social order of capitalism, its globalization and will drive its future.<sup>58</sup>The global contemporary art world, a component of this wider world order/disorder, yet active within it in specific ways, may be understood to be similarly composed and recomposed. I have used Marx's insight to clarify and articulate my sense of this art world as it exists in its specific forms in Hong Kong, South Korea, India, the PRC and Palestine.

It should become clear, too, that the development of something called "Asian contemporary art" is as necessary and yet problematic a formulation as "African contemporary art." This art is bound into a system of dynamic power relations with the "core" Western art institutions. Some of these I have started to outline here. Along with Marx's six interacting elements, I pursue the related analytic categories put forward in the first book in my trilogy. The global contemporary art world, to reiterate, comprises art forms, created by their makers using specific means and forces of production, though in a wider context of active and collectively engaged producers. A range of historical and new institutions exists within which artists and their works are produced, exhibited, disseminated, sold, curated and validated. Validation through institutional collection (thus creating "museum quality," an art fair sales patter cliché) is part of a broader process of critical assessment or identification—discourse which includes everything I say in this book. Often produced with other scholars and students in mind, this discourse on art takes a part in the broad social reproduction necessary to enable the global contemporary art world to carry on, as part of our planet's now systemic organization.

Despite my habitual tendency to put "material" or "economic" before "social" or "aesthetic," I don't intend by it to smuggle in any hierarchy. Words, sentences, paragraphs and chapters have to follow meaningful sequence but this should not be interpreted to mean that the most important always comes first. Sometimes it comes last! The global contemporary art world is a matter, first and last, of systemically interrelated and interdependent elements. Though we may particularly treasure certain artworks, or revile them, they really exist only as an indissoluble part and as evidence of a total human and social order. The Art Basel Hong Kong fair held in 2013, to which I turn now, is my first example.

1

## Doing the Business: Producing Consumption in the Hong Kong Art World

My "premium economy" class flight to Hong Kong is delayed due to engine trouble and I spend a mostly sleepless "economy economy" night in a Helsinki airport hotel waiting for the jet's repairs to be completed. I finally arrive at my budget hotel in Soho, near the western tip of Hong Kong island, only about eight hours later than scheduled, though it feels like I've lost a whole day...

#### Hong Kong Gets the Art Basel Treatment

On that first morning in Hong Kong I encountered a big demonstration in the downtown area protesting against the PRC's plans to vet all candidates wishing to stand for election to the Hong Kong government in 2017. The privately owned, stock market-listed *South China Morning Post* newspaper—once owned by Rupert Murdoch's News International group—noted that this was the "biggest march for a decade," held on the seventeenth anniversary of the British handover of Hong Kong to mainland Chinese rule in 1997. A local version of the global "Occupy" movement had emerged recently and taken a lead in the protests. I skimmed the pages of the *A.M. Post*, a free monthly magazine on Hong Kong contemporary visual arts. Many stories in the June 2014 number were concerned with the second "edition" of Art Basel Hong Kong held earlier and the numerous related art world events that had been arranged to take advantage of the potential buyers who had flown in for the fair. The global contemporary art world is, as many commentators note, predominantly "event driven."

A.M. Post reported, for instance, that a planned show of life-size sculptures by Antony Gormley, intended to be sited at various high vantage points around Hong Kong, was cancelled due to the suicide of a bank employee who had jumped from the JP Morgan building offices... the French and Hong Kong governments had collaborated on the just-opened exhibition "Paris Chinese Painting: Legacy of the Twentieth-Century Chinese Masters" at the Hong Kong

Museum of Art... Para/Site, a chic alternative exhibition and meeting space founded by artists in 1996, had paired up with the library, research and education agency Asia Art Archive, founded in 2000, to produce a show about the "colonial legacy" of "sexual conservatism" in the territory ("Sex in Hong Kong: Ten Million Years of Yearning")... Ai Wei Wei's Berlin show 'Evidence' had dealt with the artist's recent imprisonment in the PRC ostensibly for tax evasion and the revelations of official corruption... a show at the newly opened government-funded arts and "cultural engagement" center, enigmatically called "Oi!"—developed on the site of the former Hong Kong Yacht Club included a nostalgic film installation by Kingslev Ng based on 1950s home movies of the colony's Lunar Park fairground.<sup>3</sup>

This snapshot indicates something of the range of activities within Hong Kong's contemporary art world in the first half of 2014, which was enlivened and considerably enlarged by the takeover of the Hong Kong Art Fair by Art Basel three years earlier. This move helped to encourage several globally active key New York/London dealing galleries to open venues in the city, confirming the centrality of Hong Kong in the Asian contemporary art market. A.M. Post's editorial fairly sang Art Basel's praises. The business in Hong Kong had initiated, it noted, some philanthropic as well as financial partnerships with, for instance, Art Basel's support for the planned "visual culture" mega-museum M+, Asia Art Archive, the Hong Kong Tourist Board, as well as "main sponsor" Deutsche Bank (Germany's largest financial group, holding assets worth more than 1 trillion dollars), whose corporate art collection was foregrounded at Art Basel Hong Kong's first edition in 2013. These organizations constitute something of the usual range of "bedfellows"—some combinations perhaps appearing unlikely—which cohabit or partner-up in the global contemporary art world. Multinational banks come to rub their shoulders here with "anticolonial" and even explicitly dissident arts organizations. Sounding only mildly sarcastic, A.M. Post's editors reflected in Warholian terms that it was perhaps "time for artists to [...] enjoy the wealth and quality in life that business can bring, so that art can tread its path towards business—towards a better place."4

Art Basel Hong Kong's debut fair in 2013 consolidated the premium significance of the territory within Asia's contemporary art world, although the focus of activity there is overwhelmingly on sales rather than on art production or museum and gallery shows. The SAR is geographically contiguous with mainland China—from which much art is brought to be sold to an effective world market—and relatively close to a number of other significant eastern Asian and southeast Asian contemporary art centers, including Taiwan, Japan and Singapore. Hong Kong's key role is in the production and promotion of art consumption: in terms of art buying, selling and branding—these are the defining features of the global art fair business. Setting aside sales of artworks shown

at the fair, Art Basel Hong Kong, along with several satellite fairs and auctions in the city, generates and stimulates economic, social and cultural activity at many interrelated levels and points within the system that comprises the Hong Kong art world. The glossy opening pages of the main catalogue published for Art Basel Hong Kong in 2013, a third-tier world art fair now, alongside original Art Basel (first edition 1970) and Art Basel Miami Beach (first edition 2002), emphasize the continuum of "luxury" commodities associated with the art market. These include gold watches, Cuban cigars, vintage French champagne and residential properties in central London and Manhattan. The catalogue also showcased BMW high performance cars painted in the style ("brands") of postmodern and contemporary artists, or their representatives, prepared to be linked to this wider luxury market: from "Andy Warhol BMW M1 1979" to "Jeff Koons BMW M3 GT2 2010."

The Art Basel businesses themselves are marketed as luxury goods, services and experiences—three complementary sets of products central now to the operation and character of the global contemporary art world. If the small city of Basel in Switzerland offered what is described as "limitless culture without going very far," including access to what is claimed to be the "world's oldest public art collection," (the city's municipal collection of art, founded in 1661), then the US city of Miami Beach boasted "premier international art and a high-caliber audience." Part of the unique service offered by Art Basel, then, is the delivery to participating gallery owners of a dense population of serious

![](_page_41_Picture_4.jpeg)

**Figure 3** BMW art car: *Jeff Koons BMW M3 GT2 2010* (BMW car, painted in style of Jeff Koons, 2010). *Source*: © Frank Stella. ARS, NY and DACS, London 2016.

potential buyers. Meanwhile, the throng of mere onlookers, though vital to the spectacle of the fair, are kept at a distance from the VIPs.<sup>6</sup>

Time Out Hong Kong's 'Hong Kong Art Guide' to the 2013 Art Basel fair in the SAR also began with multiple pages parading Western luxury brands and fashion models. Then it announced a Jean-Michel Basquiat show at the new Gagosian gallery in town. A few pages later the Chinese performance artist duo Cai Yuan and Jian Jun Xi – based in Britain, famous mostly for jumping on Tracey Emin's My Bed installation at Tate Modern in 1999—made an appearance advertising new works at the Shanghai Tang gallery venue in Hong Kong. Yuan and Xi's "interventions," the ad claimed, "reflect upon the phenomenon of globalization and the role of modern China in the 21st century." Celebrity in the contemporary art world is an inevitable adjunct of this kind of marketing activity. The Art Newspaper International Edition, commemorating the first Art Basel Hong Kong, presented its own photo-gallery of the most internationally famous Chinese artists: Zhang Peili-reputedly the creator of the first video artwork made in the PRC in 1988—Ding Yi, Cai Guo-Qiang, Zeng Fanzhi, Yu Hong and Ai Weiwei (the latter named as the most influential player in the contemporary art world by Art Review magazine in 2011).

Art Basel Hong Kong, following on from the Miami Beach operation, articulates the related worlds of business, city tourism and regeneration, luxury consumption and celebrity media culture. The art fair model has become a global template, encouraging both mutually beneficial and potentially competitive contemporary art world markets. For instance, the main catalogue contains an advert for the 2014 India Art Fair in Delhi, sponsored by "Yes Bank." "Sponsoring," "collaboration" and "partnership" activities have extended deeply into the productive activity of some globally successful artists who've become key players in the world market. These include Jake and Dinos Chapman, represented by White Cube gallery in London, São Paolo and Hong Kong, whose branch opened in the territory in 2012. Time Out Hong Kong's article on the Chapman Brothers' exhibition there, co-timed with Art Basel Hong Kong 2013, partly concerned the "pyjamas project" they had done with fashion house Louis Vuitton's head designer, Clint Iones. This was another mutually beneficial spreading of cash rewards associated with the luxury goods field, with a bit of the Chapman brand's social comment thrown in. "We don't really have a wider interest in fashion [but] it's quite nice to see the work bleed into other areas," averred Jake Chapman, then aged 47 but still billed, and branded, as one of "Les Enfants Terrible." But while diverse luxury commodities, services and experiences proliferate around and in the art shown at Art Basel events, the heavy lifting of "sponsorship" is done by the finance banks, whose corporate collections of modern and now contemporary art have become integrated into the representational pitch for Art Basel's exhibitions.

At Miami Beach, the Swiss bank UBS has been the main sponsor for the art fair since 2002, and at Basel itself since 1994. The bank described its involvement in the 2012 edition in Miami Beach as "an integral part of our long history of supporting the arts through our global sponsorship programs and the UBS Art Collection." UBS presented this involvement tacitly as a form of public penance for the crimes and malpractice within its global business activities. The multinational bank was castigated after the 2008 Crash, investigated and fined several times by the US government for illegal trading and tax evasion practices that were reported widely in the media. In 2011 its former chief executive Oswald Grübel resigned in the wake of the latest scandal worth about US\$2.3 billion. At the 2012 fair the catalogue statement from UBS extolled the corporation's extensive charitable and education works in the United States, the diverse public accessibility of its art collection and the copious amounts of work done by its employees—on UBS time—in non-profit public service.

#### Offshore Art Business in the Global Neoliberal **Capitalist Economy**

Art Basel decided to contract the main sponsorship of Deutsche Bank for its fledgling Hong Kong fair. The bank's art collections are based at Deutsche Bank Towers in Frankfurt, the Stadel Museum, also in Frankfurt, and in Hong Kong. Other sponsors for the 2013 edition spanned the usual range of luxury goods and "media partner" services—high-end vodka and brandy, five-star hotels, watches, the *Financial Times*—along with, somewhat jarringly perhaps, Para/Site and Asia Art Archive, named as "cultural partners." Deutsche Bank's explanation in the 2013 catalogue of the reasons for its involvement in Art Basel Hong Kong raises the question of some of the "borders" central to this chapter and the book as a whole. The bank's high-minded self-justifications are exemplary of much official art fair rhetoric that constitutes part of what might be called the "global text" of the contemporary art world.

Deutsche Bank's corporate collection, the institution's Art Basel Hong Kong literature explains, consists of over 56,000 artworks; more than 900 branch offices worldwide exhibit examples from it. We are transported once more into the enigmatic world of art quantity/quality issues again. Ninety five percent of the Deutsche Bank collection is "accessible to the public"—but what if this excludes the best 5% of the art? Or does quantity trump quality here in an inversion of usual art critical values? The meat of the matter, however, is in what Deutsche Bank characterizes as its "deep and enduring connection with art globally [which] stems from our belief that creativity transcends borders, challenges the status quo and is a source of innovation and new perspectives."11 Deutsche Bank's motivation to "invest" in modern and contemporary art has been rooted ostensibly in both economic and public relations concerns. Its collections date from 1978, at the time when fine art was first beginning to be marketed as a viable, or potentially viable, "asset class."

The pioneers of this business development in the mid-1970s claimed that artwork purchases could prove to be a reliable way to "park" capital, in a time of high inflation, with an expectation that their financial value, over time, would at least not decrease—or on the basis that art's volatility or risk as a carrier of durable value was significantly less than that of other kinds of material assets, such as luxury boats or cars. 12 Deutsche Bank's sponsorship of Art Basel Hong Kong enables it (so, one imagines, the bank's chief executives think or hope) to appear cultivated and high-principled in terms of corporate social and community commitments, especially given the 2008 Crash with which it, along with UBS, was directly associated—interestingly, in the Miami area itself.<sup>13</sup> Deutsche Bank's sponsorship role also enables the bank to have its global business activities associated positively, if tenuously, with contemporary art's apparent ayant-gardist utopianism and societal radicalism, through the bank's abstracted, ambiguous business-speak of "innovation" and "creativity." This, then, is part of the reason why at Art Basel Hong Kong in 2013 Deutsche Bank came to rub shoulders with local visual arts organizations such as Asia Art Archive and Para/Site—indigenous and initially "grass roots" institutions with considerable connections to post- and anti-colonial traditions in the arts, scholarship and activism.

Deutsche Bank is also interested in the prospect of financial profit through its involvement in the art fairs' cumulative sales in Hong Kong, Miami Beach and Basel which work, directly and indirectly, to enhance the value of the many thousands of artworks in the Bank's own collection. But although the Asian contemporary art market (and "emerging markets" in the Persian Gulf and Brazil) has grown dramatically since 2000, concomitant with a rise in Asia-Pacific based millionaires by 9.7% to 3.3 million in 2010, the total value of art sales in 2011 in European and US markets, despite the 2008 Crash, remained much higher, according to Marc Spiegler, co-director of Art Basel Miami Beach. <sup>14</sup> Another operative border, then, and a veritable economic "iron curtain" operating everywhere in the world, as well as in the global contemporary art world, is that between the rich and the rest. Between, that is, those (the "High Net Worth" and "Ultra-High Net Worth" groups) who can afford to buy art as a form of "investment"—the medieval sense of which, interestingly, meant to besiege or blockade—and the multitudes without that power.

Corporations, galleries and individual collectors made up the bulk of those buying artworks at Art Basel Hong Kong in 2013 (at which 245 selected galleries were represented, attracting more than 65,000 visitors over the four days of the event), though the quality and quantity of information on art market sales remains inadequate in many respects, despite the ever-growing economic significance of world trade in art and antiques. 15 Part of the reason for this is that the contemporary art market is divided between the highly competitive socalled "secondary" public sales done at auctions and at art fairs and the private purchases agreed between dealers, artists and collectors, traditionally termed

the "primary" sales forum. "Secondary," however, is a misleading name for an increasing quantity of sales happening at art fairs, where artists in growing numbers have begun directly to send new works—labeled "direct from the studio"—for first time sale. In an overall growing market all competing sectors might benefit from this change; in the time of harsh volatility and slowdown after 2008, however, the auction houses and art fairs sometimes came into conflict over preserving what they considered the legitimate borders of their selling territories.

For instance, in an effort to maintain its profits during this period. Christie's auctioneers took over a dealing gallery of their own, Haunch of Venison, with branches in London and New York, which it operated from 2007 to 2013. After that point the decision was taken that the gallery's resources were to be integrated into Christie's traditional auction-based sales activities. Art Basel decided to exclude Haunch of Venison from taking a booth in any of its fairs during this period as it enabled Christie's to attempt to peach some of the VIP buyers that Art Basel regarded as belonging to its territory – in its fairs at Basel, Miami Beach and Hong Kong. Art Basel also became wary of rival auctioneers and dealers' clandestinely acquiring Art Basel's VIP "passes," allowing continued access to the sales floor, in order to approach potential buyers, within an infrastructure set up and paid for by Art Basel. (My own attempt—finally successful—to gain a VIP pass for Hong Kong in 2013 involved a bizarre telephone interrogation by Art Basel's UK agent who seemed suspicious of my motives and self-declaration as a "critic." Might I be secretly acting for a dealer or auctioneer?) It's difficult to feel too sorry for Art Basel, however, in their attempts to maintain control over what they see as "their" market and having to suffer auctioneers' agents steering potential buyers away from certain gallery booths. organizing, according to one dealer, "dinners around the fair and [taking] the best tables at the best restaurants," where they "exhaust the clients with their dinners and exhibitions "16

Art Basel, once it had taken over the Hong Kong Art Fair, was able to bring into play the work and expertise of its global "VIP managers" service, totaling 22 agents based around the world. This number was a massive increase upon the single specialist employed by the Fair's previous owners to tout for potential business. "Globalization" in the contemporary art market partly means this kind of intense and extensive—and continually extending—intelligencegathering activity. The imperative is to attract and retain the interest of potential buyers, and, especially in hard times, eliminate what is regarded as unfair competition from other sectors in the business. 17 A public debate organized at Art Basel Hong Kong in 2013 drew explicit attention to the business when it considered the motion "The market is the best judge of art's quality." Participants included Amy Cappallezzo, chair of Christie's "post-war & contemporary" art sales and Charles Guarino, US publisher of Artforum journal. These influential art world professionals were invited to join the discussion, partly in order to give the fair some intellectual and critical kudos. Such "collateral" seminars lend an element of the "biennial experience"—shows more apparently akin to serious museum exhibitions than to the hype-driven selling fairs—and thus cross the border between the two paradigmatic "main events" dominating the contemporary art world calendar.

Guarino encapsulated a prevalent fetishizing of the art market in his preparatory statement for the debate when he claimed that artists "are ever mindful of their audience, of which the market is a logical extension—some might say synonymous [...] Selling work may not be the primary motivation, but it's surely there before brush touches canvas. The real question is 'do good artists ever ignore the market?"<sup>18</sup> In the context of Hong Kong, rather than, say, London or New York, the primary homes of the global contemporary art market but also of art production and its museum validation, this debate had particular significance. (Guarino's revealing phrase, "before brush touches canvas," also underlined the predominance of paintings in the objects put up for sale at art fairs: many more pictures get sold than all other kinds of objects put together.) Another border, then, is that between the market's activities on the one hand and the museum and related academic bases of contemporary art on the other. In Hong Kong and Asia generally, the former—though still dominated by Western interests—is much stronger and more influential than the latter, a situation creating serious asymmetries that further skew the power relations in the global contemporary art world.<sup>19</sup> Astute commentators in Hong Kong were aware at the time of the 2013 fair that the SAR, despite enjoying "an enviable degree of artistic freedom [...] compared to the Mainland," had little historical or recently developed institutional infrastructure needed to sustain art production and the related curatorial/critical processes of museum validation.<sup>20</sup>

The two most recent ambitious exhibitions of new work by Hong Kongbased artists, for example, took place well outside the city. These were the "Horizons" show at the Shanghai Museum of Contemporary Art in 2007 and "Hong Kong Eye," held at the Saatchi Gallery in London, that attracted more than 200,000 visitors in 2012. The historic diaspora of Chinese around the world means that there cannot be just one single location or authentic home for either these people or the works made by Chinese artists where ever they happen to live, or where these artworks might be shown or sold. The development, however, of new high-profile art exhibition venues in Hong Kong has certainly been celebrated by their supporters. They cite the arrival of globally powerful galleries such as Gagosian, White Cube, Perrotin and Lehmann Maupin (from New York and London), and Pearl Jam, Platform China and Peking Fine Arts (from the PRC). A growing number of "alternative" spaces and resource centers such as Para/Site, Asia Art Archive, Osage, Cattle Depot Artists' Village—home to Videotage, a moving image archive and research unit active since 1984—and Spring Artists' Residencies all suggest an indigenous, diverse visual arts culture constituting what has been called Hong Kong's

"new amazing art hub." 21 Sceptics point out, however, that the economic success of the SAR has led to continuous rent rises, squeezing artists out of the city, that the PRC's gradual encroachment on the relative political and cultural independence of Hong Kong is a worrying matter, and that most of the new international galleries ("at the moneyed end of the spectrum") have no interest in showing or selling works by local artists. Their sense of the market is predominantly determined by the taste of the rich of mainland China and Asia broadly. Related to this focus, Hong Kong is also home to a local art public in the main intolerant of the kinds of conceptualist interests characteristic of much contemporary art since the 1990s with the rise of the "Young British Artists" Damien Hirst, Tracey Emin and Sarah Lucas. 22

One exception to the predominance of Western corporate interests in the art market in the territory was the return to the city of Ben Brown Fine Arts in 2009 from London, where the firm's eponymous owner maintains another gallery. Born in Hong Kong, Ben Brown is regarded as one of the first "world class" local dealers to have moved his gallery into the historic Pedder Building in the Admiralty district. Brown claimed in 2013 that the city's art world had moved beyond "pure investment [and] speculation" and was now about "genuine interest [...] a time of great cultural effervescence." His Hong Kong gallery shows a mixture of Chinese and international artists. 23 Both A.M. Post magazine and Time Out Hong Kong's special edition on Art Basel Hong Kong in 2013 recognized the local art world created, though still under consolidation, there. According to an essay in A.M. Post, it constitutes a kind of "art world factory" of key players—the artist, the auction house, the curator, the gallery, the critic, the collector. The author's tone accepted that a necessary system or protoecology was at work, but that this can also be "played" or rigged by those with particular interests and may be skewed by those who enjoy more power (for instance, he noted that prospects "for employment remain bleak for visual arts graduates in Hong Kong"). There was a recognition that the contemporary art system in the SAR was vulnerable, even "pitifully weak," at many levels. It was vulnerable to global business pressures, to the economic and political regimes dividing but also connecting Hong Kong and the PRC, and poor in terms of the prospects for the development of good local conditions for educating and employing home-grown arts professionals. It was very difficult for Hong Kong's own artists and their artworks—especially given the lack of a "culture of professional art criticism"—to rise above the status of being a "sideshow" to the city's art market burgeoning under Art Basel's control.<sup>24</sup>

The vulnerability of Hong Kong's art world, though also its specific qualities, is underlined by the character and local reputation of the Sigg Collection of contemporary works. This was gifted to the mega-museum M+, planned for completion in 2019 on a vast "regeneration" site in West Kowloon across from Hong Kong Island. The collection includes many important pieces by mainland artists, such as Ai Weiwei, Fang Lijun and Zhang Pelli, whose video, photographic, painting and installation works have been censored for many years by the Chinese Communist Party government because of their sociopolitical and sexual content. Time Out Hong Kong magazine wondered what future for "artistic freedom" there might be in the SAR when the PRC had recently prevented Shanghai's Power Art Station from showing Andy Warhol's silkscreen prints of Chairman Mao in the exhibition "Andy Warhol: 15 Minutes Eternal" that then travelled on to Beijing. If that was not bad enough, the complaint was that some influential local art pundits, including renowned traditional artists such as the oil painter Lin Minggang (chairman of the Hong Kong Oil Painting Research Society) had attacked works in the Sigg Collection as "rubbish." Such criticism, though still occasionally seen in the Western contemporary art world's homelands several decades after the notorious Carl Andre "bricks" media frenzy in Britain in the late 1970s, has largely been banished to a marginal "flat earth" position. This achievement was due in some large degree to the efforts of Tate's Nicholas Serota who relentlessly promoted Britain-based contemporary art as a sign of vigorous national creative health through the gallery's annual "Turner Prize," begun in 1984.<sup>25</sup>

Could a "mature," well-rounded and indigenous art world infrastructure or ecology be established in Hong Kong, given these particular vulnerabilities? The same question could be asked of the prospects for contemporary art in Ramallah in the State of Palestine, a multiply-fractured proto-territory frozen into pernicious dependency on Israel, the subject of this book's final chapter. What, then, are the conditions necessary for the authentic flourishing of contemporary art and the range of agents, institutions and networks needed to support it in such "emerging" centers? That modifier suggests something of the difficulties in a global art world dominated by pre-existing Western interests and resources. Perhaps, one way or another, such a question is inevitably what might be called "occidental essentialist." Based, that is, on a stated or tacit partisan perspective: a set of presuppositions generated by Western art institutions for over two hundred years and embodied in the canonical artworks made, bought, sold, exhibited and critically validated within its core territories in Europe and the USA during this epoch.

Isn't the global contemporary art world system created from, and working to reproduce, a set of asymmetrical power relations and nodes highly differentially placed geopolitically? Isn't this system linked decisively, in economic and cultural terms, to the dominant Western powers, for example, through art market businesses such as Art Basel Hong Kong? This global system also manifests the historical legacies of imperialism, colonialism, neocolonialism and Cold War division that still act to determine (forming and limiting) the kinds of regional and local art worlds that have developed, and can, in places such as Hong Kong—now a part of mainland China and yet, for the moment, still relatively autonomous from it. It is to the territory's divisions and borders, products of this history, that I turn now.

#### Combined and Uneven Development in the Hong Kong Art World

The "handover" of Hong Kong by the British authorities to China in 1997 meant that the people of the former colony became citizens of the PRC, and ruled by a government for which they had not, ever, voted. This outcome actually replicated the historical disposition of the British regime that had always denied Hong Kong residents general elections and which only introduced very limited popular representational democracy through the Legislative Council in 1985—a cynical late-Cold War gambit—with the knowledge that return to mainland Chinese control was imminent. (This measure enabled the British to warn with a smidgeon of truth that the Hong Kong people might now be deprived by the Beijing government of "democratic rights" that they had previously enjoyed, albeit very briefly, under their former rulers.) Hong Kong had grown apart from China in all ways since the beginnings of British rule though especially during the 1966-1976 "Cultural Revolution"—and many residents thought of the territory's delivery to the PRC as a second colonization. But the PRC was itself, by 1997, in a process of dramatic change as the capitalist reforms of Mao's successor, Deng Xiaoping, the "modernizer," had radically transformed the country over the previous 20 years. The PRC was invited to join the World Trade Organization four years later—conditional on agreeing to a range of international treaty obligations that regulate global capitalism—and began huge growth that has continued, if after 2014 at a slower rate, making China's the world's second biggest economy.<sup>26</sup>

Art Basel's corporate owners wanted control of the Hong Kong Art Fair because it brought the Swiss business very close to what is now also the second biggest market for art of all kinds in the world (up massively from 6% in 2006 to 25% by 2012, due to a huge rise in the size of a Chinese middle class prepared to spend some disposable income on artworks). It remains unclear, though, how many committed, systematic collectors of contemporary art there are in the PRC—one estimate put it as low as 30. What evidence there is quite firmly demonstrates that they mostly buy contemporary "traditional" ink paintings and calligraphic works made by Chinese and other Asian artists. Not all newly made art is, then, equally contemporary, or contemporary in the same ways: choice of media and themes are key to the varied and partisan definitions of contemporaneity that new works attract. (This is a question that I pursue further in Chapter 4, concerned with the passage from modern to contemporary art in the PRC.)

While Hong Kong by the 1990s had become a highly significant marketplace globally—especially for intercontinental financial dealing—development of the trade in material commodities such as contemporary art via enterprises such as Art Basel Hong Kong and recently arrived London/New York-based global galleries such as White Cube and Gagosian remains a speculative, risky venture. Western art businesses hope that the cosmopolitan mainland Chinese buyers' tastes will gradually become more what might be termed "global contemporary"—that is, derived from the Western European and US modern/ postmodern art era of c. 1860-1990. Christies, for instance, decided to move squarely into the PRC market, establishing a permanent auction house base in Shanghai in 2007 and thus began to compete directly with "state backed" local auction businesses (although the government imposed restrictions on Christie's sales of certain kinds of works).

Contemporary Chinese artists renowned in the west, such as Zhang Xiaogang and Fang Lijun, mostly still sell their works outside of the PRC, where they can achieve much higher prices: 211% higher on average, in fact, for works sold abroad in a period studied recently.<sup>27</sup> In contrast, Sotheby's, along with PRCbased auction houses Poly and China Guardian (which both opened branches in Hong Kong in 2012), sold the vast majority of their lots of Chinese calligraphic works at 2013 auctions there, further indicating Chinese buyers' relative aesthetic conservatism. Calligraphic works accounted for a huge 88% of all Chinese fine art sales at all auctions in PRC in 2011. According to one report, over 90% of all contemporary art pieces—though acknowledging the serious ambiguities of that term—sold at auction by PRC businesses in Beijing in the last few years went to Western buyers.<sup>28</sup>

Though Hong Kong's assimilation into the PRC has been partial in all respects, China, the British and Hong Kong's own elite indigenous ruling groups have sought since 1997 to maintain the benefits of the SAR's economic and financial arrangements for attracting global business. Hong Kong had the world's sixth-highest GDP per capita in 2004 and supported 33% of all foreign capital flows into the PRC. <sup>29</sup> The territory's continuing attractiveness in these terms is obvious. Hong Kong's free trade policy includes no taxes on art imports or exports, though PRC cross-border trade in art and antiques is regulated. By contrast, the PRC operated a 12% import tax for artworks until 2011, which was then halved for a trial period if the work's country of origin had a preferential trading agreement with China. Value Added Tax (VAT) remains at 17%, so the overall import tax is 23% compared to zero in Hong Kong, although the SAR does apply a 15–16.5% profit tax range on businesses. It has been very difficult, therefore, for mainland auctioneers to compete with Hong Kong's contemporary art business given China's comparatively high tax regime. Many PRC collectors prefer to keep their artworks in the SAR in order to avoid these payments. Not surprisingly, art insurance, storage, transport and conservation businesses have also thrived in Hong Kong.<sup>30</sup>

Nevertheless, developments indicate that a kind of globalization of contemporary art sales within the PRC has taken place since the mid-2000s, in the sense that the disparities in price between works sold at auction there and externally, including in Hong Kong, have decreased, though the reasons are complex and the quality of the evidence is patchy. Business professional

opinion, reported in *The Art Newspaper* in 2013 at the time of Art Basel Hong Kong confirmed, for instance, that New York and London no longer maintained an iron-grip "duopoly" on contemporary art sales. It suggested that the move of Art Basel and major dealing galleries into Hong Kong is a kind of recognition of this, as well as of the rising power of mainland China's own art market. 31 However, too much focus on the art market runs the risk of ignoring and underplaying significant cultural and social developments bound up with the transformation in relations between the PRC and Hong Kong, but also within these two interconnected but still separate entities.

For instance, according to Philip Tinari, director of the Ullens Center for Contemporary Art at "798" in Beijing, much more varied and distinct senses of "Chineseness" are now experienced and expressed by the Chinese people in the PRC. These are clearly distinguishable from those of people in Hong Kong. Taiwan and in the greater Chinese global diaspora. Multivalent and mutating identities have been produced out of China's own profound and rapid globalization since the 1990s. This transformation comprises an emerging contemporary generational identity that has grown, Tinari observes

out of a shared citizenship, education system, consumer culture and set of historical memories filtered through their parents [...] rather than some immanent notion of a cultural identity [...] this "China" is itself an extremely wide category [...] that today, in an era of atomised subjectivities [...] contains multitudes"

If the Beijing central government rather unintentionally legitimized contemporary art precisely by treating it "as a market," and as a small part of the nation's economic modernization, then it also helped enable it to become an available and potent form of representation and self-representation.<sup>32</sup> (Chapter 4 explores this development further.)

Art Basel is not the only Swiss business operating in Hong Kong. International Social Services Hong Kong (ISS-HK) is a "global aid" company and non-governmental organization (NGO) specializing in providing contract welfare services and payments to refugees, or "asylum-seeking clients," living in the SAR. The Refugee Union—an asylum seekers supporters' organization—was involved in the street protests in Hong Kong in 2014. This group launched a campaign attacking ISS-HK for allegedly taking \$280 m in its contract with the Hong Kong government but paying welfare support 25% below the SAR's legal poverty line and then colluding in a series of corrupt deals with food suppliers and landlords.<sup>33</sup> Neoliberalization measures within the global economy have centrally involved nations privatizing such government services and cutting social and welfare provision budgets—especially since the 2008 Crash. In Hong Kong this policy is part and parcel of the same "investment strategy" that attracts contemporary art market business by charging low profit taxes and no

import/export taxes. (Only relatively small or zero reductions in national debts and deficits in the European states have actually been achieved by making large programmatic cuts to state spending—"austerity"—rather than by raising progressive taxes, especially on corporations and the super-wealthy, in order to maintain welfare provision for their citizens.)<sup>34</sup>

Magnus Renfrew, then the director of Art Basel Hong Kong, in his "welcome" statement in the main catalogue to the 2013 edition, claimed that the European-US domination of contemporary art, manifesting what he called a "broadly unified aesthetic," had actually given way to a "plurality" of art in Asia. He then observed that Hong Kong, Asia's finance capital, had an "opportunity to become a defining force in the international art world." The regional significance of Hong Kong included its status as the center for the Asia and Asia-Pacific "collector base" for modern and contemporary art. In addition, Renfrew said that the SAR's own emerging visual arts organizations would help to "cement" Hong Kong's position "as a cultural hub of global stature." Of the 245 galleries from "35 territories" present that year at the fair (selected from over 600 applications) over 50%, he noted, had "active gallery spaces in Asia and the Asia Pacific region." This was actually a tacit acknowledgement, however, that a majority of this "over 50%" were, in fact, European and US-owned, despite the "Asian Tiger" rhetoric. "

Ben Brown Fine Arts' arrival in the SAR in 2009 represented one of the most practical means through which support could be provided directly to Hong Kong's indigenous artists. Brown's gallery and, later, the Lehmann Maupin Gallery began to show the work of local artists—such as Simon Birch, Tsang Kin-wah and Pak Sheung Chuen—as well as North American and European artists, including Tracey Emin. Lehmann Maupin also began to offer internships for Hong Kong arts graduates in New York City, the global contemporary art world's "mother ship." This development was part of a scheme by the gallery intended to promote what it called "dialogue between East and West." The other Western galleries' move to the city around the time of Art Basel Hong Kong in 2013 has clearly helped to promote the growth of "cultural industries" infrastructural support companies of varying kinds, as previously noted. The long drawn-out plans for the mega-museum M+ have been very ambitious—a US\$2.1billion development intended to rival Tate Modern and the Museum of Modern Art in New York.<sup>36</sup>

Art production in Hong Kong, due to the high and perpetually rising rents since 2000, has substantially turned, of necessity, into a "post-studio," "post-medium" practice, favoring digital forms, and often requiring an outsourcing of production as a consequence of the space limitations, frequently with the use of modest quantities of materials, typical of the SAR's general "just-in-time" economy. This mode is sharply contrary (sometimes intentionally so) to the eruption of contemporary art production in Beijing since 2000, based on the use of generously sized converted factory spaces available in the outer suburbs,

especially those along the city's eastern corridor to the Capital Airport. The Hong Kong conditions arguably neither guarantee nor foreclose anything in terms of the potential quality of work produced though, as one commentator has suggested, they may lead to assimilations of some "fine art" production to wider commercial design activities in the city. This consequence, in turn, may have provoked, or could provoke, a backlash where artists come to favor working in a traditional studio context, seen precisely as a kind of retreat from Hong Kong's advertising and graphics business culture.<sup>37</sup>

Space and money issues do not trouble Wang Wei Lui's collection of modern and contemporary art. Along with her husband, luxury handbag manufacturer Yiqian, she owns two private exhibition venues—together comprising the Long Museum—located in Shanghai, spaces open since 2011 and 2014 respectively. These institutions exemplify a trend in super-rich Asian wives' cultural philanthropy (enjoying favorable state land purchase and tax benefits) which also includes the private Kiran Nadar Museum in Delhi, India, discussed in Chapter 3. In 2009 Wang Wei Lui spent over US \$58 million buying Western and Asian art while the two buildings, the Long Museums in Pudong and West Bund, cost over US \$48 million dollars to construct. Lui has bought installations by Elafur Eliasson and Mona Hatoum and, earlier on in her collecting career, so-called Chinese "red classic" paintings with post-1949 socialist-realist revolutionary themes. These works, she claims, are really early "pioneers of Chinese contemporary art," existing in attenuated, traumatic continuity with the art world of Art Basel Hong Kong and the SAR's complex, symbiotic relationship with the PRC. 38 In 1949, according to one source, there were only 21 museums in the whole of mainland China, 348 by 1978, but more than 4,000 in 2013. Of these, 175 are visual arts specialist institutions funded by the government. The PRC. like Western nation-states, now sees its "cultural industries" as a significant sector of the country's economic expansion. In 2010 this sector had an output of US \$178.9 billion, up 221% from 2004, and comprising 2.75% of China's GDP. The PRC's five-year plan begun that year had the target for the nation's cultural industries sector increasing to 5% of GDP by 2015.<sup>39</sup>

One of the problems this extraordinarily rapid growth has created, however, is a huge amount of exhibition space with often little to fill it. Many new venues lack permanent collections of artworks, trained staff of all kinds (paid a decent salary) and exhibition program budgets able to turn these museums into permanent functioning institutions. The Power Station of Art, Shanghai, for example, was converted into a museum in 2012 at a cost of US \$64 million, paid by the city government. Like Tate Modern, it is a renovated energy-generating plant. The building was converted in order to host the 9th Shanghai Biennale that year, but since this event finished the museum has had long periods without any exhibitions at all. In addition to budget, staffing and planning problems, policy and political controversies recur. The "15 Minutes Eternal" show of Andy Warhol works, as noted earlier, took place at the Power Station of Art

in April-July 2013 but the authorities removed the silkscreen prints of Mao which they regarded as "unsuitable." 40

The privately owned Shanghai Museum of Glass (or SHMOG, an unfortunate acronym given the chronic environmental problems in the PRC) opened in 2011 with a specialist focus on this construction, decoration and expressive material. It is housed in a former factory renovated in spectacular fashion by corporate designers Coordination Asia, a German design and architecture firm based in the city. SHMOG exemplifies a trend in businesses opening museums effectively as "fronts" for public relations efforts. SHMOG is one of the planned 100 museums to be launched in a decade as part of the city authorities' campaign to turn Shanghai into what it calls a "global cultural and creativity center." Despite these ambitions, the museum's "Keep It Glassy" exhibition held in the spring of 2013 appears to have been the last show the institution managed to organise.41

While many new permanent exhibition spaces in mainland China have emerged rapidly from within a generally poor visual arts infrastructure, some much more innovative shows have been produced by the organizations running biennales and annual art fairs across the whole of Asia, and beyond, in recent years. 42 Commentators also claim that these events now offer some of the most important and stimulating shows in the global contemporary art world calendar, compared with the exhibitions organized by the historic museums of modern art based in the "core" Western countries—especially when those still funded significantly by governments, such as Tate in Britain, have faced deep "austerity" budget cuts of their own over recent years. 43

In particular, Manifesta 3 (2000), Documenta 11 (2002) and some biennial exhibitions (e.g., the Havana Biennials of 1984, 1986 and 1991; the Venice Biennials of 1999 and 2001) received high critical acclaim for mounting shows that challenged and helped transform understanding of the meanings of "contemporary," "postcolonial" and "global" in relation to art made around the world since the 1980s. 44 Partly in emulation, the art fairs organized by Art Basel began to more explicitly "thematize" their displays in the early 2000s and organize "collateral" discussion events. The intention was to add intellectual and experiential texture to, and heighten the cultural status of, these essentially sales-driven events. In a wealthy city such as Miami Beach, able to attract many of the richest and most influential players in the global contemporary art world, and boasting many nearby high-quality museums, galleries and publicly accessible private collections, this was not a difficult task to undertake successfully—in the richest country on earth supporting a very well-developed visual arts infrastructure. Accordingly, there was a considerable "curatorial accent" to Art Basel Miami Beach held in 2011, through 30 sponsored "special exhibitions" organized in the fair as a whole dedicated to themes such as "The Two of Us" (artist couples "working together and apart") and "Repetitions—Art Works in Plural," along with focus on conceptual and performance practices. 45

Art Basel Hong Kong in 2013 organized a section of its show called "Encounters." This consisted of large-scale sculptures, installations and photographs placed at various places in the exhibition arena—a decision which included siting 17 works whose scale worked, it was claimed, to "transcend" the usual art fair size stand for commercial galleries. Art Basel's marketing for the Hong Kong event was based on the idea that the fair was no longer simply a narrow opportunity to buy and sell art, but rather a bigger "event of cultural importance." The "Encounters" show was curated by Yuko Hasegawa from the acclaimed 21st Century Museum of Contemporary Art in Kanazawa, Japan, In addition, Art Basel Hong Kong made the decision to allow some politically sensitive issues to be broached at the fair. One of the "Encounters" works, for example, was provided by the Hong Kong-based gallery Osage (which also possesses exhibition spaces in Beijing and Shanghai). Osage displayed large-scale photographs taken anonymously by Filipino domestic workers, in a 2009 show by artists Sun Yuan and Peng Yu called *Hong Kong Intervention*. The works depicted these Filipinos in their employers" homes in Hong Kong and in other locations in the SAR.

These photographs offered a commentary on employer–servant relations in a globalized world where both legal and illegal migrant workers along with asylum refugees move (and are moved on) from state to state, across real vet sometimes relatively porous borders, seeking employment and personal safety. Given UBS's and Deutsche Bank's longstanding sponsorship of Art Basel, and its Miami Beach and Hong Kong events, it seems that contemporary art in a globalized social order has come to depend upon key players within the financial system—in terms of both the fairs' subvention and their occasional presentation of "cutting edge" social commentary artworks. This is a very fine line to

![](_page_55_Picture_4.jpeg)

![](_page_55_Picture_5.jpeg)

Figure 4 Sun Yuan and Peng Yu Hong Kong Intervention (anonymous photograph of Filipino workers in Hong Kong, 2009).

walk, however, given the structural asymmetries, inequalities and iniquities of the system evidenced by both these banks' recent criminal prosecutions and fines for persistent wrong-doing.46

#### Hong Kong's Art World Inside and Outside China

Was the art exhibited for sale at Art Basel Hong Kong in 2013 somehow actually globalized, or made "global," in and through its presentation at this event. where hundreds of galleries based in Europe, North America and Asia presented several thousands of works made in many different places? The visitor's first, though in my case recurring, experience of the sheer overwhelming mass of works on display at art fairs, in the giant sheds of space provided at these industrial scale venues, prompts this and related questions. "Global" can stand simply as a synonym for absolute volume or totality. In what ways does the art fair event create and mediate the presentation and intelligibility of contemporary art? Once again, the quantity/quality issues raised earlier resurface. All the catalogues and related booklets published for Art Basel's fairs offer a literal written text and furnish a presuppositional context for making some provisional sense of the mass of works on sale. The physical size and density of the fairs along with their discursive representations constitute a key component of the global contemporary art world's marketing and branding system.<sup>47</sup>

The main catalogue's introductions, welcomes, information sections and adverts for luxury goods finally give way to short sections on the hundreds of galleries taking part written by gallery staff themselves. Some of the works for sale are illustrated, accompanied by short texts of differing kinds, such as an artist's biography, or a note on the work's content and other works for sale, or background information on the gallery itself. As this advertising space in the catalogue is bought by galleries there is leeway in what their managers decide they want to include. But the galleries are highly selectively chosen, Art Basel constantly emphasizes, on the basis of their "quality" and "representativeness" in terms of what it claims are the highest global standards of creativity and innovation.48

This mediation of the artworks on sale through the catalogue format accompanies the "experiential" tours organized for buyers of the galleries' booths by both legitimate art fair guides and, as mentioned earlier, the occasional unofficial ("hostile") strikes carried out by undercover auction house and dealing gallery professionals trying to poach potential buyers. The highest-profile works are often bought ("pre-sold") before the fair even opens. Galleries send "jpeg" illustrations of them to buyers, partly in an attempt to forestall their money going to other artefacts for sale by other galleries at the fair. In a shady practice castigated by some, these might be works reserved for what Marc Spiegler, co-director of Art Basel Miami Beach in 2011, called the "top clients."

But there is also an attempt to keep other works available for what is called "building relationships with new clients"—those, that is, who might become regular buyers.49

Some of the "collateral events" held in Hong Kong in 2013 debated the merits of these practices and the wider geopolitics of the art fair form under globalization. The Asia Art Archive had run a three-year project on the nature of "the global." Several of its sessions in Hong Kong with speakers such as the Indian critical theorist Geeta Kapur, artist Vivan Sundaram and Iranian filmmaker Abbas Kiarostami looked at contemporary art and visual cultural production from a broader Asian perspective. Might the inclusion of these powerful voices be seen as a form of "repressive tolerance," enabling the corporate owners of Art Basel Hong Kong to neutralize potential criticism by drawing those who are actually likely to articulate it into the event itself? Or can such an event, given its evident and tacit imbrications within global capitalism, genuinely respond, for instance, to neo-Marxist postcolonial critiques?<sup>50</sup> How would we know if this had been achieved?

This question raises the matter of the diverse and possibly antagonistic interests present at Art Basel Hong Kong in 2013, including the perspectives of academics and the institutions for which they work. A group of Art Baselfunded sociologists from the University of St Gallen in Switzerland have studied the social impact of all the fairs. I encountered them at the "collateral event" I was speaking at in Hong Kong. Professor Franz Schultheis, their leader, was understandably diplomatic, given their benefactors, in the remarks he'd previously made about the Miami Beach fair to *The Art Newspaper*. "There is cultural globalization here," he observed, not giving much away. With a nowobligatory genuflection to the French sociologist Pierre Bourdieu's research in the 1960s and 1970s. Schultheis talked about contemporary art's "cultural capital" (rather than its financial value, though he claimed art and money are "antagonistic"), identifying the art fair, anthropologically, as a form of life and an instance of a "habitus," with the implication that, presumably, it deserved to live just like any other life-form.<sup>51</sup>

What Art Basel's business and organizational expertise brought to the Hong Kong fair was really a "technics," a hyper-rationalized means of operation designed radically to increase its quantity and quality in terms of branding, marketing, sales and profitability. This expertise included three innovations. Firstly, the "24 hours art night," held the day before the fair opened in order to generate interest and excitement in and about Hong Kong (sometimes portrayed as "the New York of the East"), when visiting potential buyers were bussed to as many openings and shows as they could physically stand. Secondly, the attraction to Hong Kong of numerous serious collectors from Europe and the USA along with a much higher number of core Western galleries to sell them artworks. Thirdly, super-efficient "VIP dining arrangements" that meant much better networking amongst potential buyers, artists and dealers. In short, Art Basel brought accumulated professionalism from the west (all those VIP agents allocated countries to "work") and offered a commitment to professionalize Hong Kong's part of the global contemporary art world via the growing, if admittedly still very weak, infrastructure stimulated by the Swiss corporation's arrival.<sup>52</sup>

Leap, the self-billed "international art magazine of contemporary China" produced an intelligent, partly satirical commentary on this transformation process, or at least the promise of it. It recognized, as did *The Art Newspaper*, that Art Basel Hong Kong, despite its own rhetoric, was "less an Eastern fair, more an international brand in an international, free-trading city" able to attract some of the wealthy, the powerful and sundry celebrities from Europe and other parts of Asia, as well as mainland China. "People loosen up when speaking Chinese in Hong Kong," Leap noted:

This first Art Basel Hong Kong has all the feeling of an Asian celebration. Once they get a few Laphroaig 18 [whiskies] inside their belly, the Mainland [Chinese] gallerists slip raucously into their native dialects. With a good day's trade behind him, you can hear the pride of the rich man in his language. There haven't been so many Chinese and international big name galleries in one place for a long time [...] the tycoons like to buy from power galleries, not even knowing the artists [...] Artprice rankings are what drive most people. Basquiat is first, Fangzhi Ceng second. Liu Wei is showing at White Cube.<sup>53</sup>

Within the globalization of visual arts "creative industries" businesses in the contemporary art world, Art Basel Hong Kong is an instance of the kind of cross-continental socioeconomic event that neoliberal public/private liaison organizations such as the Asia-Europe Foundation (ASEF), underpinned by EU and the Association of South East Asian Nations (ASEAN) intergovernment services, exist to sponsor. ASEF's "Creative Encounters" program, for instance, is designed to bring together cultural organizations in Asia and Europe by supporting artistic exchange between the 49 countries of the "Asia— Europe Meeting" group (ASEM). Its declared aim is to reinforce "dialogue between cultural professionals from the two regions," initiate "capacity building workshops" and develop long-term cooperative projects. 54 But might these forms of "NGO-type" developments effectively constitute another form of insidious Western neocolonization, given the structural asymmetries in the power relations between the Euro-American "core" and "peripheral" Asian nations? Can there be a mutually, equally beneficial result?

The Hong Kong-born and -based curator Johnson Chang, founder of the territory's Hanart TZ Gallery in 1983, and co-founder of Asia Art Archive, saw the task of those primarily interested in indigenous visual arts development as that of understanding the degree of "self-colonizations" that Asian countries

have generated and to which they have succumbed. Early twentieth-century Chinese nationalists, he has noted, were dazzled by an image of Western modernization powering the "May Fourth" movement that sought to bring in Western culture and values, as a reaction against Japanese imperialism after the First World War. A similar bedazzlement by Western capitalism and its promise characterized the "modernizing" ambitions of leading factions inside the Chinese Communist Party after the death of Mao with the leadership of Deng Xiaoping.

Art Basel Hong Kong may be seen, then, as a by-product of this longer history and of the ties and divisions that characterize the complex historical relationship between the SAR and the PRC. For Chang, a way for Asians to be liberated from this obsession with the West is to work to build closer ties with other countries in the broader region, as part of a continuing construction of practical opposition "to Western cultural colonialism." India's contemporary postcolonial social theory. Chang suggested, was China's "most relevant object for reference and cultural exchange." This was because its scholars, such as Geeta Kapur, Homi Bhabha and Dipesh Chakrabarty had made the most complex study of "modern Western thought," which was a necessary precondition to understanding "contemporary Western thought," (India's contemporary art world and its relation to both Western modernity and the country's own recent socioeconomic neoliberalization since the 1990s is the subject of Chapter 3.)

Chang curated the influential exhibition "China's New Art Post-1989," first shown at the Hong Kong Arts Festival in 1993, which then toured in the USA. It seems that Hong Kong's global success has always been and remains intimately tied to both the West and the PRC. Divisions of many kinds—economic, social, cultural and political—are as constitutive of its identity as its links to these places, states and peoples. The work of Chinese artists in the SAR and on the mainland is shaped now by global market forces (within and beyond art) that have dominated the PRC's development since the 1990s. Ai Weiwei went so far as to claim in a review of London's Hayward Gallery's 2012 retrospective show of performance and installation works, "Art of Change: New Directions from China," that "the Chinese art world does not exist [...] Chinese art is merely a product." Though this is polemic overstatement, a lot of art produced has been for and about the market. For instance, Henry Chu's 2007 interactive installation work, The Sound of Market, converted Hong Kong stock market data into music tunes programmable by the user. In what other revealing or enjoyable senses can the art world be reduced to or represented as a market? The artist Li Liao, for his 2012 piece, Consumption, famously went underground at an Apple computers assembly line in China in order to document in photographs and text how long it would take him to buy an I Pad on the wages he earned there.

But the longer-standing "alternative" visual arts organizations in Hong Kong—long pre-dating Art Basel Hong Kong, with a sometimes explicitly

dissident attitude to the establishment culture of British colonial power and postcolonial influence there—have, for many years now, been attempting to build a sophisticated critical art culture and material infrastructure to support indigenous art and artists. Along with Asia Art Archive, the gallery Osage (founded in 2004) and its associated design businesses operating worldwide has a mission to promote contemporary Asian art and culture. Its galleries in Hong Kong, Beijing and Shanghai have shown some of the most challenging and thought-provoking Asian conceptual/installation-based contemporary art. Osage was the first Hong Kong gallery to be selected to take part in Art Basel in 2011 and Frieze Masters, London, in 2012. Osage also runs a range of visual arts service businesses, as well as producing fashion and accessories marketed and sold across the globe. At the same time it operates some "notfor-profit" cultural programs—"Creative Communities," "Cultural Cooperation" and "Creative Capacities"—intended to improve regional co-operation within Asian countries and the world beyond, some co-run with the City University of Hong Kong.<sup>57</sup>

Osage's 2014 two-site exhibition "Market Forces—Erasure: From Conceptualism to Abstraction," curated by Charles Merewether, was held at its East Kowloon base and at the Hong Kong City University's temporary gallery in the north of the SAR territory. This show also dealt with the powers of the contemporary art market, the kinds of use of materials commercial factors tend to impose on the way artists produce "for" a market, and questions to do with the continued domination of Asian contemporary art by Western values. The exhibition included works by artists from Hong Kong, the PRC, Japan, South Korea, Thailand, the Philippines, Singapore and Indonesia. Merewether born in Scotland but a long-term Asian resident—has long had a particular interest in forms of materiality present in contemporary "Eastern conceptualism" that are not reducible to market commodification. This was demonstrated by another of his recent exhibitions held at Osage, "Poetics of Materiality," featuring new works by four Asian artists: Ringo Bunoan (the Philippines), Young Rim Lee (South Korea), Ng Joon Kiat (Singapore) and Yu Ji (PRC).<sup>58</sup> While Osage gallery maintains a strong interest in the active legacies of materiality and materialism in contemporary Asian painting and sculpture forms exploring their evolution into recent "globalized" mixed media and installation art configurations—Hong Kong's Videotage moving image collective, in contrast, has sustained a commitment to supporting new technologies for critical representation and documentation practices in contemporary art.

Videotage is a not-for-profit video collective that has documented the territory's transformations through processes of globalization, the end of British rule and Hong Kong's return to mainland Chinese control since 1997. It's both an archive resource center and a cultural agency that supports individual artists and groups based in the SAR. Its original home, at what became known as the Oil Street Artists' Village (an abandoned government supplies depot at

North Point, on the eastern side of Hong Kong island) was closed down to make way for high-value residential development in 1999, after an occupation to resist this move enforced by the initial SAR government. This was the same year that Hong Kong resident artist Tammy Cheung made her first documentary film about the lives of three Indian women working in the city, Invisible Women, which explored gender inequalities and the lot there of ethnic minority groups. Many other video works have since dealt with what has been described as Hong Kong's "constant identity crisis" brought on by its location, attraction as a destination for both legal and illegal Asian migrants and its complex relationship to the PRC.

These works include Linda Lai's Voice Seen, Images Heard (2009), a collage of film clips about Hong Kong's past and Eric Siu's witty three-part video film critique of global consumerism *Made in China* (2011).<sup>59</sup> The territory's shifting identity has been characterized by one of the founders of Videotage, artist Ellen Pau, maker of the 1999 film *Recycling Cinema* which micro-focused on the activities she documented along an expressway close to the Hong Kong seashore, as moving from an "insecure postcolonial status to the humbling embrace of the great motherland [but] society seems ever confused at how to react to cross-border connections "60

Videotage curators, in statements written for its "One World Exposition" video screening event in Hong Kong in 2012, declared a particular interest in engaging, via media art practices, with the PRC from what they called the "strategic location" of the SAR-which they characterized as always both "inside" and "outside" China

![](_page_61_Picture_5.jpeg)

Figure 5 Ellen Pau Recycling Cinema (still from video, 1999). Source: © Ellen Pau/Videotage.

Videotage offered what they claimed was an important "attempt to rethink our identities through different concepts of Chinese culture." With the looming presence of the first edition of Art Basel Hong Kong in mind, Hong Kong-born curator Isaac Leung wondered if his city could be imagined "not only as an offshore location of the "national Chinese art market," but also as a complex "mediator" proposing, via media art, "a mode of thinking that is different from other forms [enabling us potentially to] rethink our history, memory, subjectivity and citizenship." The PRC-born co-curator Li Zhenhua developed this theme of the "Parallel Worlds" that Hong Kong and the PRC constitute in his review of the development, in both territories, of film, video and "integrated media art." He noted that the years immediately before the 1997 handover had been a critical moment in the development of video art in mainland China. In this period a sizeable group of artists using video for experimental short films and animation projects had emerged and the PRC's "independent film circle" began to receive increased international attention. What understanding of Hong Kong before the 1997 "return" to China could PRC citizens have had of the territory, only seeing images of it in archive film, asked Zhenhua.<sup>61</sup>

One answer to this question would be that the people in the PRC and Hong Kong share a common experience of very rapid and continuing urbanization since the 1980s. Ou Ning's and Cao Fei's 2003 film San Yuan Li, commissioned for the 50th Venice Biennale, presents a multicamera portrait of a mainland Chinese village on the edge of the urban sprawl from the mega-city of Guangzhou, home to 12 million people. Another shifting border, then, is that between such urbanization processes and the transformations in what was once a rural life where subsistence farming and craft culture had been able to survive. Within these ongoing global developments lie the contradictions, tensions and traumas that have accompanied PRC and Hong Kong Chinese working population concentrations and related sociopolitical marginalization. Ellen Pau's explicitly political film, For Some Reasons, made in 2002 as a response to the PRC's attempt to impose new "anti-subversion" laws in Hong Kong—against the Sino-British agreement at handover—helped to mobilize opposition to the bill which eventually was abandoned because the elected Legislative Council would not support it. 62

Twelve years later, the demonstrations that I encountered when I visited Hong Kong in July 2014 also concerned the governing powers that the PRC wished to extend to the ex-colony. The British, remember, had only introduced elected representation in 1985, when it was clear that handover to mainland China was imminent. This late-Cold War act—though highly cynical in one respect, but now vital to the actual popular freedoms of Hong Kong's citizens in another—provides a telling link to the next chapter. This is concerned with the state of the global contemporary art world in South Korea, a country divided in 1953 from its northern region and people by the civil war and then Cold War superpower conflict fought between the Soviet Union and China on one side and the United States and its allies on the other.
