2

## New Media Art and Cultural Globalism in South Korea

Consulting the inflight map on the screen on the back of the seat in front of me as the plane finally dips down into Incheon airport I am alarmed to see quite how close Seoul is to the border—the "Demilitarized Zone" or "DMZ"—with North Korea. In the dark, however, it is all one Korea to me. Respite on the ground is short. Seoul is only an hour's flying from Dalian, a city in northeast China where my university maintains a campus at which we teach 800 Chinese students in partnership with a local university. Four hours later I fly on to Dalian for two days of meetings with our staff and students then back to Seoul to give a lecture at the "Video Art Archive Network Forum" conference. After Dalian—an international city, by the PRC's standards—Seoul feels very "Pacific-American," very corporate.

#### The Chic of Global "New Media Art"

The 2013 Video Art Archive Network Forum in Seoul brought together leading organizations from around the world with specialist interests in "new media" and "new media art." Taking part in the conference were representatives from the institutions FACT (Foundation for Art and Creative Technology, Liverpool, UK), ZKM (Zentrum Fur Kunst und Medientechnologie, Kahlsruhe, Germany), Video Data Bank (Chicago, USA), Videotage (Hong Kong, PRC), Inter-Communication (Tokyo, Japan), Minsheng Art Museum (Shanghai, PRC) and the Loop Alternative Space in Seoul, which hosted the conference. I attended the conference with a colleague, Menene Gras, from the visual arts organization Casa Asia—a department of the Spanish government based in Barcelona and Madrid. The Forum in Seoul included, as part of its collateral displays program, an exhibition of recent video works by Spanish artists curated by Gras. The global contemporary art world, to reiterate, is a networked "event driven" entity.<sup>1</sup>

The categories "new media" and "new media art" are simultaneously intellectually vague and rhetorically significant within the global arena of the contemporary art world. In one sense, the term "new media" may appear simply to nominate a technological specialization: photographic, film, video, digital and internet-circulated images and image-production processes. But photography, traced to its origins, emerged nearly two hundred years ago and film much more than one hundred years ago. The modifying prefixes and suffixes "new" and "art" provide important clues to the complexities and confusions surrounding these terms. In another sense, then, "new" isn't—indeed is never—a neutral, adjectival term: "new" virtually always suggests an evaluative judgement, although it may be tacit. This surreptitious, and often ideological, usage is indicated, for example, by the way that "new" is routinely used as a synonym for "modern" meaning "advanced" or "up to date"—as in the phrase "economic modernization" (i.e., changes to industry that end what are held to be old "restrictive labor practices" such as closed-shop trade unions).

The "new" in "new media" is partly a matter, then, of emphasis on the claimed innovative technological and labor processes within and through which images or a representational idiom—e.g., a photograph or film—has been produced. But the modifier "new" also affects our understanding of the product of these processes, which is claimed, by virtue of its ostensive novel character, to be somehow better, or more truthful, or more interesting, or a significant breakthrough (and a decisive break from the "old" or the past) in some other way. In another direction, the suffix "art" works as a specializing proper noun, wherein certain "new media" works are selected and assimilated into this specific aesthetic and institutional order, which is also held to afford a distinct form of experience and knowledge. Philosophers have called this the "art episteme."<sup>2</sup>

The absorption of some "new media" processes and products to the category of "art" erects another border, through which specific photographic, film and digital-internet representations have been divided from other institutionalized orders of representation and forms of knowledge, such as the "mass media" and, over about the last ten years, "social media." "New media art" organizations, however, though they are necessarily tied to traditional museum practices through their affiliation to the "art episteme," have increasingly wished to manifest a break away from the kinds of establishment institutions that have hitherto collected, exhibited and validated historical art. In this sense, "historical" can be used as a synonym for "traditional" meaning old, reactionary and out-of-date; the emphatically not new.<sup>4</sup>

While new media art organizations since their inception in the 1980s have usually offered public displays of their artefacts, conventional notions and practices of exhibition—connoting a unidirectional process of interpretation dictated by expert curators—always appeared anachronistic and inappropriate for their objectives. Correspondingly, new media art institutions rarely inhabit (or wish to inhabit) buildings or create display spaces that look like traditional

art museums and particularly not like the neoclassical structures of the Louvre in Paris or the national galleries of London, Washington and many other Western capitals.<sup>5</sup> The rhetoric of new media art organizations tends to be what is termed "post-museum" (sometimes even "anti-museum"), despite the important continuities with some kinds of modern—but now also contrastingly "old"—art that these organizations usually claim. Despite the fact also that, increasingly, some of the "traditional" art museums have themselves increasingly adopted a "post-museum" stance in how they have begun to revise and extend their self-definitions, "mission statements" and core activities, often as part of an explicit discourse on the meanings and consequences of globalization.

Seoul Museum of Art (SeMA) is an exemplary case in this regard, to which I shall turn shortly. Since the 1980s South Korea has become one of the leading high-tech "emerging" economies and societies in eastern Asia—associated particularly with automobile and digital communications media innovation and production. Much of the country's contemporary art world, and one-fifth of its population, is based in or near to the capital, Seoul, close to the border with North Korea. One of the world's most explicitly "globalist" biennials takes place in the South Korean city of Gwangju, 200 kilometers to the south of the capital. Both Seoul and the Gwangju Biennial (first held in 1995 and 39th to be founded in a total of 149 surveyed in 2013) have long manifested strengths in "new media art."

These have drawn on the global success of Nam June Paik (1932–2006), by far South Korea's most influential and well-known twentieth-century artist, internationally acknowledged as one of the leading precursors in the development of contemporary and "new media" art. Paik's major retrospective exhibition held at the Guggenheim Museum in New York in 2000 sealed the artist's validation by one of the most powerful of all the Western gatekeeper visual arts organizations. In 2008 the Nam June Paik Art Center was established within the Gyeonggi Cultural Foundation, a complex of museums and exhibition spaces in the city of Suwon close to Seoul. Later, a dedicated building—emphatically "post-museum" in its design, in this case resembling a standard office block—was constructed to house the institution's collection of Paik's artworks.<sup>8</sup>

South Korea (officially The Republic of Korea) was divided from North Korea (The Democratic People's Republic of Korea) in 1953 along the 38th Parallel or Demilitarized Zone—the "DMZ." South Korea endured autocratic and effective military rule from then until 1993 when the first civilian president was elected. The Gwangju Biennial, founded two years later, was partly inaugurated in order to memorialize a civilian protest against the regime in this city in 1980—South Korea's own Tianenmen Square massacre—that was put down by the army, killing about 150 demonstrators. Nam June Paik's family, like many others, had fled what was to become "South Korea" during the civil war.

The artist settled in Japan, then Germany, before moving to New York in 1964 where he began to experiment with the use of portable video equipment. Widespread interest in film and video art developed in South Korea during the 1970s. It emerged partly as a form of resistance amongst artists and activists to both the mass import of US Hollywood commercial film and the authoritarian surveillance carried out by the military regime at home. <sup>10</sup>

Loop Alternative Space in Seoul, founded in 1999, inherited and propagates this belief in video as a practice—whether linked to the "art episteme" or not—that carries inherent "democratic potential," a claimed capacity to encourage what one of its director's has called "participatory [...] autonomous direct democracy." Other early new media centers (all eschewing the title "museum" altogether) also operated, alongside Loop, to support this effort. Art Center Nabi, IlJoo Art House, Ssamzie Space, Art Space Pool, Project Space SARUBIA and Space Beam all appealed to what one commentator has called a "digital aesthetic" opposed to the established, conventional gallery culture in South Korea. <sup>11</sup>

During the 1990s a sophisticated film culture developed in the country linked to forms of leftist and socially liberal political movements. These included the Seoul Women's International Film Festival (founded in 1997) and the Korea Oueer Culture Festival (founded in 2000). The Incheon Women's Biennale of Art first took place in 2004, following social and cultural liberalization policies gradually enacted by the civilian governments that replaced military rule in the country. Since then, video and multimedia works have constructed a revisionist account of the country's historical development. These include Hwayeon Nam's 2007-2010 extended multimedia work Operational Play-a kind of anti-war filmic history of military activity in Korea over the centuries. Other moving-image works have critically investigated the extensive use of documentary film and photography by military regimes themselves in South Korea during the twentieth century. Hyunsuk Seo's The Hanging Garden (2009), for instance, satirizes official state propaganda histories of Korea. Yeondoo Jung's feature-length video work Documentary Nostalgia (2007) offers a witty deconstruction of hackneved representations of the Korean national landscape created by official state media over the decades. 12

Analogous critiques of military-rule documentary film and TV propaganda can be found in video works produced by artists in other eastern Asian countries following the tentative and often reversed beginnings there of less repressive civilian democratic social orders. Works made in Thailand, for instance, have examined the role of military moving-image representations in creating dominant notions and images of Thai (formerly Siamese) national identity. Others include Chieh-Jeh Chen's 2003 film *Factory*, dealing with capitalism, Cold War and neoliberalism in Taiwan, and Michelle Dizon's 2009 film *Empire* that examines recent globalization processes, urban development and corrupt corporate power machinations in the Philippines.<sup>13</sup>

It may be that a coherent "pan-Asian" development is discernible here, despite hugely significant differences between the states, cultures and peoples across the continent's southeast and eastern regions. Artists and arts organizations—new-media-focused and others—have worked together for several decades in an effort to build supportive networks. Do these activities exemplify Johnson Chang's call, discussed in the previous chapter, for Asian states to learn from themselves and each other about the extent of their "self-colonization," as part of a necessary preliminary stage before being able to create sustainable real societal and regional independence from the West? Or should the contemporary art world's presence within a country such as South Korea rather be understood as a form of cultural neocolonization characteristic of the global neoliberal capitalist social order? I turn to this issue now and begin by looking at the international activities of a visual arts organization funded by a Western European government.

#### **Contemporary Art as Global Cultural Diplomacy**

As Casa Asia's name indicates, this Spanish government funded agency operates a "cultural liaison" network linking visual arts organizations in Spain to those in Asian countries such as South Korea through public exhibitions and film screenings, related publications, conferences and other events. Casa Asia's activities contribute to the broader work of implementing what has been called "the cultural cooperation policy of the Spanish Agency for International Development Cooperation." In Seoul, Casa Asia organized the exhibition "Language and Aesthetics of Spanish Video Art: Ten Years of Critical Practices." held at the Songwon Art Center, near the Seoul Museum of Modern and Contemporary Art, which opened in 2013. Casa Asia's exhibition was presented in collaboration with Loop Alternative Space, the Spanish Embassy in Seoul, Cooperacion Espanola, and the Spain-based media and video art distribution agency, HAMACA.14

Why does the Spanish government believe Casa Asia's activities to be worth funding? Before exploring this question it is worth pointing out that for historical reasons—given Spain's central role in colonizing Latin American countries over several centuries—the Spanish government still regards its strategic economic and cultural relationship with the Americas to be primary. In a globalizing world, however, no continent or region can be ignored by nation-states defending their economic power and pursuit of this goal through external sociocultural (and sometimes political and military) activities. Art Basel's penetration into North America at Miami Beach and into eastern Asia through its take-over of the Hong Kong Art Fair embodied a global extension of the "premium" art fair experience that greatly benefits Switzerland economically, directly and indirectly. The ARCO art fair, held annually in Madrid, is another contemporary art world event entailing transcontinental ambitions, this one highly beneficial to Spain. Though ARCO has traditionally sustained its strongest intercontinental links with galleries based in Spain's ex-colonies in Latin America, its actual and potential reach now also includes several Asian centers.

ARCO is the brand name for the Feria Internacional de Arte Contemporáneo (International Contemporary Art Fair). ARCO is backed by the Spanish national government and is also the recipient of Madrid and surrounding region state funding. It operates simultaneously as a commercial art fair and, effectively (along with most art fairs and biennials), as a hybrid quasi-state apparatus designed to promote global economic, social and cultural developments beneficial to the host nation. Through such forms of intervention into the "cultural industries" sphere of the global economy the borders between "public" and "private" interests and activities have been both redefined and radically blurred within the state-capital nexus. 15 Routine involvement in the activities and objectives of this intervention now characterizes the work of many strata of professionals, including museum directors, government "cultural regeneration" advisors, academics and specialist "enterprise" staff in universities, business people as well as art fair managers.

No less a figure than the king, Don Juan Carlos I of Spain, for example, in his welcome address to ARCO visitors, printed in the main catalogue for its February-March 2013 edition, spoke, in his role as honorary president of the art fair, of the significance of what he called the event's "usual vitality and innovative spirit, consolidating our region as a tourist destination and a major focus of creation and cultural diffusion." The president of the regional government of Madrid, throwing his hat into this ever-faster spinning rhetorical ring, raised the stakes beyond the significance of the nation's capital and its environs. "Spain," he noted, "is a leading cultural power, and the prestige that comes with this is also measured by our capacity to renew and strengthen our presence in international contemporary art circuits."

Finally, sounding rather like a director of Art Basel (or perhaps more like one of its global bank sponsors), the mayor of Madrid appeared to equate contemporary art entirely with global business development. "This commitment to art" represented what she called

one of the best targets for investment, as it offers a safe bet of increasing value for individuals and businesses. That is why ARCO Madrid is presented as the best way to boost a sector that complements its pronounced economic nature with other values, such as the promotion of culture and knowledge, by giving priority to the marketing of works of art as well as raising the international profile of art galleries and artists. 16

As noted in the previous chapter, some of the bedfellows in the world of global contemporary art would appear sometimes, ostensibly at least, to be at crossed purposes. Politically dissident or simply "edgy" artists—remember Jake and Dinos Chapman's luxury pyjama designs publicized at the time of Art Basel Hong Kong in 2013—still articulate the language of avant-gardism and political opposition to capitalism, yet find themselves now habitually sharing the art world duvet with billionaire collectors and business-hungry government advisors. A mostly tacit acknowledgement of sufficient (or extensive) shared interests suggests that they all manage—most of the time anyway—to rub along. 17

Though the majority of the video artists included in the Casa Asia show at the Songwon Art Center in Seoul may have been Spanish citizens, their films were partly chosen because they focused on Asian countries. These included Sergi Aguilar's short video on Cambodia, Dionisio Gonzalez's on Vietnam and Marisa Gonzales's film on the Philippines. Aguilar's 2005 From Phnom Penh to Srepok W. Area explores from a helicopter viewpoint the still devastated Cambodian landscape—a country subjected to secret US aerial "carpet" bombardment in the early 1970s. Dionisio Gonzalez's 2008 film short Thinking Hanoi narrates the urban environmental damage in this Vietnamese city caused by a miasma of internal combustion engine fumes and polluted humid air. Marisa Gonzales's 53 minute-long video portrays the life of an estimated 150,000 Filipino women working in exploitative domestic service jobs in Hong Kong and their one day off a week lived out on the streets at weekends before they return to their employers' homes (see the related discussion of this topic in Chapter 1).

Others films in the show examined global terrorism. For instance, Joan Leandre's and Toni Serra's 40 minute *Babylon Archives 2005* (2005) probed, in a fictional mode drawing on video game conventions, terrorist attacks on Western interests including embassies, corporate headquarters and retail businesses in US. European and Asian territories. The critical claims made for films shown in the exhibition echoed those made by South Korean film-makers and commentators that video is a privileged idiom particularly suited for such politico-aesthetic representation. Moving image works were asserted, for example, to be uniquely both global and local ("glocal") in terms of their aesthetic-critical potential. Part of this power lies in their capacity, Gras claimed, for instance, to offer a challenge to "the art system and art market and the economy of society in general." This is restatement of the familiar idea that, distinct from traditional painting and sculpture forms, film and video (often also used as elements within installations) are in some ways quintessentially global contemporary art forms.<sup>18</sup>

Some leading South Korean visual cultural organizations have internalized this language, equating contemporary art (though not just film and video) with a new phase in a globalizing—though also democratizing—modernity to which the nation must be committed. This commitment is evident, for example, in mission statements made by Seoul Museum of Art (SeMA). These include its Nanji Residency project that has been housed in a converted sewage treatment plant and its grounds on the city's outskirts since 2006. Selected artists invited to live and work at the residency, from South Korea and the world beyond, are funded by SeMA to make and regularly exhibit new work in a dedicated collective living/ studio complex and adjacent gallery. An exhibition of Nanji Residency artists' works took place during the Video Art Archive Network Forum conference.

The 2013 7th Residency Program, as the catalogue for the exhibit declared, showed how SeMA had adopted what it called a "globalized management system" as part of its vision as a "post-museum" institution. Within this, these vounger artists—including 16 chosen from outside South Korea—were said to be "incubating" their artistic interests. Use of this term, with its strong biological sense, suggests the organization of a kind of controlled aesthetic experiment with varied, and globally inclusive, cultures of production. Every month in the residency's exhibitions and discussion sessions the artists would reveal and discuss how their work was developing. They also took part in pan-Asian events hosted in South Korea, such as the 2nd "Asian Arts Space Network (Hub City of Asian Culture, affiliated to Korean Ministry of Culture and Tourism)" and collaborated with Alternative Gallery Loop and the Video Art Archive Network Forum conference itself. 19

SeMA's Nanji Residency initiative is intrinsically globalist in outlook. It announced (sounding a bit crypto-imperialist in the process) what it called an "overseas expansion" in its activities. While partly competing with other such schemes around the world, Nanji, through elaborating a "network with international residency programs," was trying to offer the best facilities to lure the most promising young artists to South Korea. Rather like the global landscape of fairs and biennials, museums and artists' residency centers form part of the "ecology" of the contemporary art world system, wherein benignly cooperative relations appear overall to hold sway. But these symbiotic relationships may enter phases of antagonism, if the interests of these interacting components or those of their funding patrons—begin to clash. This potentiality was exposed in the volatile relationships that sometimes come to exist between art fairs and auction houses (discussed in the previous chapter) when their respective sales territories are perceived to have become threatened or are actually impinged upon for one reason or another.<sup>20</sup>

Seoul has a wealth of museums and galleries, along with the Nanji Residency program and several university-level institutions offering visual arts provision. This relative plenitude is evident especially in comparison with Hong Kong's acknowledged imbalance, where market institutions strongly overshadow other parts of the SAR's generally weak visual arts infrastructure. Nevertheless, more than three-quarters of the artists in the 2013 Nanji Residency program had actually studied for their MFA or Masters degrees in universities or art schools outside South Korea. This suggests an underlying systemic weakness that could explain SeMA's attitude of defensive competitiveness about the country's future visual arts development and profile in the global arena.<sup>21</sup>

SeMA's rebranding exercise in 2012 also entailed a revealing introspection (reminiscent of twentieth-century Communist Party political self-critiques, those both voluntary and forced) expressed in its new mission statement. As a self-designated "post-museum," for example, the institution, its leaders asserted, "strives to become a humanistic space [a] living space [...it will] do its best to communicate with citizens of Seoul, endeavoring to become a world-class art museum." The institution was now working to build a "global network;" it was "strengthening curating power" and "increasing community activities." The post-museum will be reorganized in order to respond to its users "various preferences" and will need to shift from a focus on "past art and classic works" to "developing timely projects, anchored in the contemporary and modern."

Central to this endeavor was the task of "promoting the globalization of regional and local art." Diagrams accompanying this key statement indicated the relations between the different parts, functions and building styles of the SeMA "post-museum" institutional ensemble as a whole (SeMA has five centers in Seoul). "Communication" with its user-groups is central to the success of all its ventures, the statement asserted, along with the "drive to build an international network." Like Tate in the United Kingdom (which pioneered strategies for collecting and displaying international contemporary art), SeMA wished to lower the hitherto disabling borders existing between curation and the permanent collection on the one hand and the role of education and societal engagement on the other. "There is an endeavor," the statement proceeded, "to explore global symbiosis as a study place for international exchange, advocating non-mainstream art as an alternative space displaying a variety of experimental works." Central to this project would be Seoul's hosting of the 8th Media Art Biennale—Mediacity Seoul 2014, focused on "cutting edge media art." These forms, idioms and artefacts were identified to be essentially "cross-genre" and declared to embody a necessary "hybrid culture."22

#### "Glocal Video": Conventions and Critiques

Complementing and contextualizing this focus on contemporary media art in Seoul was the exhibition of "historic" video works that accompanied the 2013 Video Art Archive Network Forum hosted by Alternative Gallery Loop. This consisted of works selected by the eight participating international organizations from their respective archives. The conference was held, in part, in order to discuss the problems these leading organizations have faced in attempting to create coherent, standardized global archiving conventions which would enable video works to be conserved and made accessible through a unified, singular system for international—indeed, truly global—use.

Intellectual Property Right laws vary considerably from country to country, as do technical and other practical systems that make such potentially universal, or "arbitrary," use impossible. Getting basic international cooperation has proved difficult, making it hard to introduce what have been termed "sustainable archive sharing projects." The "post-museum" conundrum preoccupying Seoul Museum of Art also raises key questions concerning the public exhibition role of new media art organizations. Digitally preserved "paleo" video artworks (those made in the 1960s, 1970s and even early 1980s) must still be shown through analogue systems in the main, requiring a whole period technology of antique reception devices to be maintained against succeeding decades of digital innovation.<sup>23</sup>

Such practical and related policy issues and problems challenge the characteristic globalist perspective of new media art organizations committed to the universalism of these representational practices and their concomitant sociopolitical radicalism: video art's claimed "democratic," "participatory" and "autonomous" capacities. 24 It was this belief that helped power the emergence of a formation of "alternative visual culture" in South Korea, able, it has been claimed, aesthetically and ideologically to deconstruct the dominant "modes and basic framework of fiction film, documentaries, commercial media art, and mainstream art." New media organizations could thus project themselves as representing what one commentator calls a "microscopic sociopolitical alternative to the sociopolitically macroscopic ideologies of capitalism, patriarchy, heterosexism, and nationalism,"25 In terms of mainstream South Korean media output, for example, the term "foreigner" is usually reserved to refer to white Westerners, and TV commercials endlessly present whiteness (and whitening) as the key to success and accumulating wealth. In contrast, the term "immigrant laborer" is used to refer to a manual worker from a poor third world country.<sup>26</sup>

The simple "alienation effects" used in some of the early video works may be thought to create a clear contrast with—and offer a kind of blocking of—such mainstream narrative film and commercial TV conventions (not just in South Korea). Included in the Alternative Gallery Loop exhibition, for example, was Zhang Peili's 1988 piece  $30 \times 30$ , reputed to be the first video artwork made in the PRC. It shows a mirror being smashed, repeatedly mended and smashed again over a three-hour period. Such works, at one level, thus starkly, if also enigmatically, draw viewers' attention to the passage of real, as opposed to fictive, time through such emphatically repetitive, "non-narrativized" actions. Peili's 9½-minute video Water-Standard Version from the Dictionary Cihai, made three years later, displays a different tactic. Peili wholly recreated the CCTV studio newsreading environment but employed the announcer—Xing Zhibin, "the voice of China's media broadcasts"—in the task of simply reading words out from a dictionary. This ambiguous action highlighted the announcer's usual role in providing the government's selection and account of the news as well as provoking questions about the authority and meaning of public communication.<sup>27</sup>

![](_page_79_Picture_2.jpeg)

Figure 6 Tamura Yuichiro Nightless (ver.5) (still from video, 2010).

Some video works shown in this exhibition appropriated, sampled or "remediated" conventional narrative moving-image techniques, "bending" or drawing out from them nonstandard or anomalous effects. These alienation devices also act to produce elements of ideological and social critique. For example, Japanese artist Tamura Yuichiro's *Nightless (ver. 5)*, made in 2010, is a kind of "road movie," but it is made entirely from still images taken from the "Google Street View" facility. The audio track is taken from Youtube sources, so the artist himself has actually neither filmed nor recorded any pictures or sounds. The transcontinental journey of scenes in *Nightless (ver. 5)* takes the viewer from Nebraska to Chiba, from Alaska to Portugal, Marseilles and beyond. All the scenes occur in daytime, creating an unsettling continuity which defies usual moving-image depictions of "fictional" and "historical" narrative, installing instead a kind of frozen global contemporaneity/simultaneity of action and location.<sup>28</sup>

This effect offers parallels with the experience of using actual web sites which live-stream data, creating virtual images of the whole world for various purposes. These include "live tracker" visualization systems representing aircraft movements around the world, with "3D," "cockpit view" and other display options that also show an endless virtual day of real-time landings, take-offs and flights through cloudless blue skies.

Other works premised on the globalist, democratic role of "video-as-critique" have scrutinized predatory global neoliberal capitalism across Asia, where migrant labor, corporate outsourcing, downsizing and asset-stripping practices are characteristic of the social—economic order. Chieh-Jen Chen's 2003 film *Factory*, for example, deals with the impact on Taiwan of US corporate power, the Cold War and "glocal capitalism" since the 1990s. Chien-Jen Chen asked the workers he had met in 2002 to return to the abandoned Lien Fu Garment factory at which they had worked for years until sacked, and to perform a re-enactment of their labor there. Corporate investors in the factory had closed it down in 1996, withholding severance pay and pension contributions while maintaining ownership of the company's assets. In Taiwan's lowwage economy based on offering corporations "attractive" conditions to set up

their businesses—including few laws protecting workers—these laborers were regularly left unemployed when industries simply moved away to find more advantageous conditions. The video work concentrates on the gestures and expressions of the women workers re-situated in the long-empty factory, while juxtaposing these with clips from earlier black and white Taiwan government promotional films extolling the virtues of business relocation to the island.<sup>29</sup>

Singapore-American artist Ana Prvacki's 2007 film Money Laundering is an effective satire on financial and political corruption. Her 1 minute 38 second single-channel video work literally shows wads of US dollar bills being scrubbed clean in real time—demonstrating another critical "anti-narrative" technique. Filipino-American Michelle Dizon's short video Empire (ABS-CBN), made in 2009, deals with corporate power and socioeconomic globalization in Manilla, referencing Andy Warhol's iconic eight hour 1964 film of the Empire State Building in New York. Dizon's *Empire*, however, substitutes Manilla's communications tower, described by a critic as "surrounded by haphazard development and a makeshift democracy stunted by the rule of the rent-seeking elite. It is a tower of a media conglomerate that controls water, light, broadcast and cinema" in the city. South Korean film-maker Soyoung Kim's Two Doors is a "transmedia" documentary made in 2010. Through combination of a range of media sources (including video film, public TV footage and newspaper articles) Two Doors portrays a sit-in protest over rent rises on a factory roof in Yongsan, South Korea, the previous year that ended in violence when a government paramilitary squad entered the building and shot dead several of the protesters. The title refers to a fatal flaw in the planning of the raid (which door to break down?) that led to the deaths of both protesters and police officers sent into the building with erroneous information about the actual location of the demonstrators.<sup>30</sup>

#### Culture, Contemporaneity and the Postcolonial Artwork

The claim that such video art practices are quintessentially globalist rests on a particular characterization of their material-technological features and lack of traditional origin in or from any one particular location or culture in the world. That is, they emerged and grew up with, as part of, recent globalization processes themselves. They authentically "belong" both everywhere and nowhere at the same time and attain—through what might be called this radical geographically dispersed ontology—a kind of heterogeneous purity of potential meaning and reference. Wherever in Asia video work as a formal-aesthetic means and practice of representation has been developed it has seemingly become implicated within a radical sociopolitical critique of these postcolonial societies and the globalized neoliberal capitalist social orders that have developed, but which remain contested, within them, since the 1990s.<sup>31</sup>

A similar case has been made for installation art. The two idioms have also been combined, with video being incorporated into installation works, as in the example of Mark Boulos's "video sculpture" practice. Boulos's works have focused on postcolonial situations in Asian and African countries, examining socioeconomic structures of exploitation linking Western corporate capitalism to the extraction of raw materials such as oil, for export from poor southern hemisphere rentier states such as Nigeria. For example, his 2008 video installation *All That is Solid Melts into Air* dramatically set two large video screens off each other—one depicting the lives of brokers on the floor of the commodities exchange in Chicago and the other local people fighting the Shell corporate multinational in the Niger Delta. Boulos has also made work, with an anthropological dimension long present in Western documentary film history, on the social life of the revolutionary Marxists groups living, with *No Permanent Address* (the title of his 2010 video installation work) in the jungles of the Philippines.<sup>32</sup>

A different sensibility characterizes the work of South Korean artist Jewyo Rhii, who was born in Seoul in 1971. Her installation works are concerned with the quotidian materials and domestic spaces of contemporary Seoul and South Korean society, explored through what might be called Rhii's "systemic itinerancy" which is core to all her activities. She disavows the idea of a permanent home and moves from location to location—sometimes in receipt of arts grant income to fund these living projects. Rhii collects, fabricates and assembles objects which are then presented in more or less obviously orchestrated ways for public viewing and consideration, both in declared conventional exhibition locations, but also in her abodes and other less conventional venues and receptacles for display. For her piece Ten Years Please (2006, ongoing), for example, she carried round a group of disparate objects (her "works") in a shopping trolley, unwilling to destroy them after an earlier exhibition. After finally deciding to display them all at once, she then invited friends to "look after" them for ten years. Themes of anomie, city life and alienation arguably dominate her installations, in, for example, Night Studio, exhibited at the Artsonie Center, Seoul in November 2013. This installation was a partial recreation of the contents and layout of her apartment in the Itaewon district of the city which is home to thousands of immigrants, "at the end of a cramped market street of shops dealing in bags, socks, Korean souvenirs, old US army paraphernalia, and imitation designer goods." Her 2009-13 installation Moving Floor, built for an exhibition in Germany, was another recreation of part of her apartment in Itaewon.<sup>33</sup>

In a way these installations of things Rhii has made or collected stand for (represent) her living space and her actual living or life. Their "readability" or significance, however, is inherently unstable, volatile—viewers can make of them what they will and there is certainly no clearly or even loosely intended authorial meaning. These objects, their selection and arrangement for display are signs of specific and generalized condensations and displacements of

![](_page_82_Picture_2.jpeg)

**Figure 7** Jewyo Rhii *Night Studio* (mixed media installation, 2013). *Source*: Courtesy Wilkinson Gallery, London.

possible meanings and affects (feelings)—in Rhii's own life, the life of the district where she happens to be living, Seoul, South Korea, the world beyond. "The works," she notes, "are each displaced in divergent times and spaces." Copious notes are presented alongside the myriad objects in the installations in order to aid, but also to make more complex their understanding. There is a kind of quiet, unannounced historical and archival investigation going on in Rhii's works. She has discussed the development of Seoul after the Korean War and its impact on the older city and its inhabitants (for instance, in her 2008 mixed media work *Lie on the Han River*, a compendium of drawings and photographs exhibited at the Bildmuseet, Umea, Sweden). But her installations are caught somewhere between being analytic and fantastic constructions, containing elements of both which, in their interaction, further confute simple or singular readings. This is a defining feature, one is tempted to say, of cultural displacement as contemporary experiential reality—that of people, and of objects, places and relationships within our globalizing social order.

This, of course, is not only an eastern Asian reality. Displacing and disrupting borders like the one dividing the two Koreas continue to exist all over the postcolonial, post-Cold War Asian world following the last century's imperialist and civil wars, along with the neo-imperialist and internecine conflicts of this one. These include, as I showed in the last chapter, the border that still both divides but now increasingly connects Hong Kong's SAR and the PRC.

The borders separating but also joining India, Pakistan and Bangladesh (the three former components of "British India") are discussed in the next chapter. A third is what the Israeli authorities prefer to call the "security fence" or "security barrier"—actually a monstrously ugly concrete wall—dividing what they have mostly arbitrarily designated as the Palestinian areas from legally or illegally held State of Israel territory (see Chapter 5).<sup>35</sup> Highly dangerous, virtually closed borders and "no-go" buffer zones also characterize Israel's contiguous relations to its northern neighbors, Lebanon and Syria. These countries, like the Koreas, have been traumatized and torn apart by civil wars and Cold War politics, regional fighting over several decades and now by the broader religious and ethnic violence engulfing Iraq, the Kurdish region, Iran and Afghanistan. The old Cold War regional and global superpowers—Russia, Turkey, the United States and the European Union—once more intervene from the skies.

Artists in Lebanon, including Walid Raad, Akram Zaatar and Walid Sadek, have produced works in photographic, moving-image and installation modes that underline what Charles Esche, in his discussion of Rhii's art, called the "unreliability of the past to shape the present and the possibility to open up both to artistic invention." Sadek's writings, in addition, have dealt with major filmic accounts of the 1975–1991 Civil War in Lebanon—for instance, Ghassan Salhab's 1998 Beyruth Fantome and Ziad Doueiri's West Beirut (1998). These feature-length films establish the immanent personal and societal chaos of the conflict and yet also work to create some stabilizing narratives of the war's meaningfulness to its survivors. However, displacement and disjunction are mass as well as minority artistic experiences and materials under the conditions of globalization—in the violent and relatively peaceful, or just pacified, parts of the world—when rapid social, political, economic and geo-spatial change/movement potentially disorients everyone and everything.

Rhii, for one, doesn't appear to think of herself as ever "having" a home or being "at home" and never stays in one place for very long. Cosmopolitanism used to be the positive name for this incessant mobility. Her 2008 Airport Romance installation is a witty commentary on an aspect of this globalized nomadism. This installation work was a simulation inside a Dusseldorf gallery of some of the material props of moving and living between countries drawn from the liminal spaces of airports. Rhii impressionistically recreated check-in, immigration and security areas in the gallery, suggested through provisional arrangements of roped-off areas, desks, piles of luggage and seating. Airport Romance succinctly evoked the quotidian alienations but also perhaps the fleeting pleasures of people-moving and people-processing systems and spaces.36

At the Gallery Hyundai in Seoul the Iranian artist Farhad Moshari exhibited some extraordinary beaded knives. These were formed into patterns to create words made up of menacing blades and multi-colored handles attached to the gallery walls. As such, Moshari's assemblages equally inhabit an at least

metaphorically violently disjunctive world. These artefacts lie somewhere between an approximation (or even kitsch recreation) of a traditional "Persian aesthetic" on the one hand, and a version of Iranian-inflected contemporary pop culture, on the other. The knives comprising, for example, Moshari's My Flower (2012) are at once apparently practical and decorative objects that simultaneously belong to both and neither of these realms. Questions and doubts about what an artwork is "of" and where an artist might be said to be "from" (and what "traditional" itself signifies) are raised and radically problematized by the objects, assemblages and installations created by Moshari and Rhii. An analogous fundamental questioning has gone on, I've argued, within many of the "new media" works made by eastern Asian artists discussed earlier in this chapter, setting aside the question of whether these practices have any special claim to "global" or globalized" status. Issues and senses of identity, history, meaning and violence are bound up together in this wide range of contemporary artistic products and practices.<sup>37</sup>

#### The "Real DMZ Project": Conflict Art in the Korean Peninsula

Within the context of the formation of South Korea the identity of the country's people, and the divided history of the peninsula they cohabit, requires scrutiny. The Artsonje Center in Seoul supported the "Real DMZ Project" that brought artists from South Korea and other parts of the world (though not from North Korea) in 2011-2013 to the border zone to make artworks about the circumstances, experiences and meanings of the division. This project, also known as the Gangwon-do Art Project (after the nearby town), is located in the border area of Cheorwon province, in central Korea. This province is close to the 38th Parallel where the states later to become known as North Korea and South Korea were truncated in the 1953 Armistice that established a ceasefire—a temporary suspension of fighting, but no formal end to the civil war.

Several other de-militarized zones have existed, and some continue to exist, within Asia (as well as in other continents). DMZs are a material and societal residue, though with still powerful continuing effects, of twentieth-century colonialism and Cold War superpower conflicts. The DMZ dividing West and East Berlin, for example, ended with formal German reunification in 1990 part of the process that saw the termination of the USSR and the "Soviet Bloc" states, including what had been the German Democratic Republic ("East Germany"), the following year. Persisting, highly volatile militarized and demilitarized border divisions characterize much of western Asia (or the "Middle East," in received imperial parlance). These include, for example, Lebanon's border with Israel, and the longstanding conflict between the Palestinians and Israel, operating inside the State of Israel and between Israel and State of Palestine areas, the illegally occupied West Bank and besieged Gaza territories.38

War, consequential societal chaos and general penury now characterize life across several Middle East states. This has followed US and European interventions in the Gulf Wars in the early 1990s and during the last decade, the occupation of Iraq in 2003, the eruption of Islamic State's attempt to carve out a regional caliphate (Islamic religious state) since 2012, along with civil conflict and ethno-religious fighting within the so-called "failed" or "failing states" of Syria, Lebanon, Egypt, Libva, Afghanistan and Yemen, Terrorist attacks linked to Islamic fundamentalism have taken place at an increasing level in Europe, Africa and the United States over the past twenty years.<sup>39</sup> Closer to South Korea, the fading British Empire's hasty, arrogant and ignorant division of India from Pakistan (West and East) in 1947 and the later declaration of Bangladeshi independence from Pakistan in 1971, produced one of the bloodiest social traumas of twentieth -century decolonization.<sup>40</sup>

The Delhi-based Rags Media Collective was invited to take part in the Gangwon-do art project, given the parallels between the divisions within the subcontinent in the late 1940s and the Koreas a few years later. The collective exhibited a short video work made in 2005 called Door to the Sky. This depicted an aircraft door, torn from a crashed/bombed plane and starkly shot against clouds—an image, they declared, of a "frontier between worlds," resilient vet fragile. Despite the abstract symbolism, the door is something through which humans routinely have to move. The India-Pakistan and Korean divisions, by implication, may be national, international, and "super-power political" in significance, but the physical fact and direct personal experience of a closed border (and door) starts, and, in human terms, ends, with the planned blocking of an actual road, an intended sealing and preconceived prohibition of people on the ground from being able to meet, pass by each other, and live together. 41 For the same reasons, the Irish government and British Northern Irish arts organizations also supported the South Korean DMZ Project. recognizing some familiarity with the situation people on the western edge of Europe faced until the eventual fruition of the "peace process" and putatively permanent cease-fire called by the terrorist organization, the Provisional IRA, in 2005.42

The anti-imperial war in Vietnam waged by the indigenous population against the colonizing French, like the Korean conflict, became another Cold War civil conflict, this time in which the United States and the Soviet Union provided military resources for their proxy allies and fought directly on Vietnamese territory. When this war ended in 1975 the consequences of the victory for the nationalist Vietnamese (led by "the Vietcong," or communist guerrilla soldiers) would be global isolation and impoverishment until "independent" Vietnam eventual re-entered the global capitalist system in 2007, when it was permitted to join the World Trade Organization. The journalist John Pilger noted that in this year:

Vietnam was allowed to join the WTO and qualify for World Bank loans provided it embraced the "free market," destroyed its free public services and paid off the bad debts of the defunct Saigon regime: money which had helped bankroll the American war. The Vietnamese were financially being granted membership of the "international community" as long as they created a society based on inequity and exploited labor, and abandoned the health service that was the envy of the developed world [...] along with a free education system that produced one of the world's highest literacy rates. Today, ordinary people pay for health care and schools, and the elite send their children to expensive schools in Hanoi's "international city" and poach scholarships at American universities. 43

South Korea became a major beneficiary of US and other Western "development aid" in the form of gifts, direct loans, preferential government contracts and corporate capitalist investment during the Cold War and in the period since 1991. It attained membership of the international Monetary Fund in 1955 (shortly after the end of the Korean War), the General Agreement on Tariffs and Trade (GATT) in 1967, and the WTO in 1995.

The contrast with North Korea—heavily supported by the USSR in the past but now, practically, only in large measure by the PRC—is extreme. North Korea remains a closed and self-enclosed social order, divided from most forms of relationship with most other nation-states, and from most world trade, travel and tourism, which have become the dominant forms of globalizing socioeconomic interaction that now shape, in both profound and small ways. every country's culture, peoples and societal development. North Korea's dictatorial political system, self-identified as autarkic "state-socialism," and its profoundly entrenched cult of personality centered on a dynastic, ruthless authoritarian power base, marks it out as probably now the most systemically draconian social order in the world. Its officially self-declared "socialist-realist" painting, print, sculptural, photographic and filmic modes form a public propagandistic visual arts realm developed at the inception of the state in the mid-1950s. These practices and products remain interesting in what now appears as their archaic historical quirkiness but they represent the morally blank obverse of a highly puritanical and literally murderous state-censoriousness towards apparently all other kinds of art and its producers—as far as this can be known, given the secrecy of its social order.<sup>44</sup>

The artworks commissioned or exhibited by the DMZ Project in 2013, along with related discussion sessions, were selected in relation to a set of themes based on five key words set by the organizers. These were "Heart," "Earth," "Iron," "Rice" and "Water." These were chosen in order to enable a socio-aesthetic

excavation of the origins of the divided Koreas—without, however, the involvement of any artists or critics from the North-and a concomitant critical examination of the social order built in the South since 1953. The basic material survival of the two Korean states established in 1953 depended on food production and its distribution, along with very basic infrastructure development.

This rival state building phase followed a civil war, killing up to 5 million people, which came soon after the Jeiu Uprising in 1948-9 that led to the deaths of about one million people who'd fought against both US occupation and pro-Japanese Korean collaborators (Jeju is an island to the south of the peninsula). The two Koreas of the post-1953 era were—and remain—societies built from these violent depopulation events, setting aside the further traumas of personal and societal displacement that the creation and maintenance of the DMZ itself has caused. It has been argued, however, that these founding traumatic events have been displaced—officially, if partially—within the dominant positive narratives of the South's national development since the war. These accounts continually celebrated industrialization, material infrastructure and, later, high-tech development, marginalizing the highly destructive societal legacies of state repression of popular dissent, civil war and territorial division 45

For example, tongil rice, a special variety of carbohydrate-rich grain, was created in South Korea, with US expertise, as a staple crop to feed the growing population and ward off, it was believed, growth or resurgence of popular support there for Communism. A similar project was initiated in North Korea at the same time, for the same reasons, sponsored by the Soviet Union. This development required a rapid intensification of industrial-agricultural production and the extended use of chemical fertilizers. Artworks made for the Real DMZ Project 2013 alluded to this process: Sunkyong Kim's 2008–2011 photographic project Rice Fields in Cheorwon explored the meanings of the idea of people "living in harmony with nature"—in the DMZ itself, where the artist's home town is located—given the historical imperatives of technology-driven food production. Water provision and dam-building are closely related issues that both unite and divide the interests of the two Korean states. (Water security is an issue that also profoundly affects the day-to-day life of Palestinians, whose supplies are controlled entirely by the State of Israel.)<sup>46</sup>

At earlier moments in the post-separation history, ties between the two Koreas had been closer and some mutually beneficial co-developments occurred. For instance, the Kaesong Industrial Park development, in the northern part of the DMZ, set up in 2002, involved economic and social cooperation by both governments. This stalled, however, and led to an entrenchment of separate and further separating destinies, influenced increasingly by the competing regional interests of the US and the PRC. Other artworks created in the 2011-2013 period for the project in mixed media, architectural and installation forms have addressed a variety of related themes. These included works on the material structures of the DMZ buildings as well as the homes and shops in the town of Cheorwon. South Korean architect and designer Taehyung Kim, for instance, produced a series of detailed drawings exploring residential buildings in Cheorwon, looking to identify what he called a potential "new type of alternative social and cultural space [beyond] occupation and domination, beyond exclusion and conflict, beyond commercialization and development."47

Artists made works for the Project on the social psychology of border conflicts and divisions, about "security tourism" in the DMZ, and reconstructed and filmed Korean War battle scenes. Other pieces were concerned with the day to day existence of soldiers on both sides who police the DMZ, examined life in the surviving, stranded civilian areas, and considered the torture of anticommunists by North Korean state forces. 48 Some of these artworks are witty and seemingly trivial, such as Sean Snyder's photographs and installation pieces concerned with showing North Korean leader Kim Jong Un lower legs and platform heels, revealing a shortness scrupulously hidden in official state media representations. Snyder's 2007 work Korean Central News Agency. Pyongyang, DPRK elides six photographs of the trouser legs of the leader with the name of the organization responsible for propagating his image—thus tellingly illustrating the interdependency of one with the other. Snyder's broader and underlying serious interest, however, is in such globally circulating propaganda imagery and the forms of its stage-management. 49

South Korean Suyeon Yun's 2004–2006 photo-documentary project Incomplete Journey dealt with the lives of North Koreans who had managed to escape to the South. Way to Eden is the title of one work in the series ironically showing North Koreans who have settled in the South standing in front of a giant advertisement showing cartoon Adam and Eve figures. But Suveon Yun critically compares and possibly even equates the different kinds of societal shabbiness and shallowness that exist, according to the artist, in both states. Noh Suntag's Red House # 1-13 photographs from 2005 illustrate the massive set-piece collective gymnastics displays held in North Korea, designed to indicate the cohesiveness and resilience of the nation. But Suntag reminds viewers in his statement for the exhibition that South Korea has also organized largescale sporting events such as the 1988 Olympic Games for similar propagandistic ends. The two states, he claims, are finally "not unlike mirrors reflecting one another." Chien Chi Chang's 2007-2008 film work Escape from North Korea examines the very dangerous journey North Koreans have to make if they wish to get to the South, travelling north and then west, illegally, through the PRC, down to Laos and then back up to South Korea.<sup>50</sup>

Other works have dealt interestingly with aspects of earlier détente—and apparent or failed détente-between the two countries. For instance, Chankyong Park's 2005 hour-long single-track video film Flying depicts the

journey made by South Korean leaders in 2000 for the first peace talks ever held between the two countries. Part of the film shows the jet flying from Seoul to Pyongyang, but Chankyong Park turned the flight scenes upside down, so that the plane appeared to fly under North Korea—a gesture underlying the "creative ambiguity" of the détente mission, according to the artist. <sup>51</sup> Yang Ah Ham's 6-minute 30-second video film *Tourism in Communism* (2005) is about a moment in the late 1990s when South Koreans were given permission to visit a border area in the North regarded as sacred to all Koreans—though this ended in the shooting of a tourist by Northern soldiers, which put paid to the cooperation.

Magnus Bartas's 2009 video film *Madame and Little Boy* (2009), based on bizarre factual events, concerns a South Korean actress kidnapped and abducted to the North where she went on to make films with her husband, who was also kidnapped, before both managed eventually to claim asylum in the USA.<sup>52</sup> Video works not included in the DMZ Project have highlighted the continuous "interrelationality" of the two Koreas and other contiguous territories, such as Japan and the PRC. Yangachi's 2009 film *Middle Corea Trilogy* explores the adventures of a mythic family that comes to symbolize a kind of self-sustaining "pre-national" state collectivity. Its members over three generations were able to move between all these countries, crossing closed borders and creating, in a sense, an effective border between the family itself and these external states. The title name "Middle Korea" suggests neither North nor South Korea, but rather a territory—also neither entirely real nor imagined—hovering somewhere between both.<sup>53</sup>

#### **Contemporary Art Across Global Asia**

The "Real DMZ Project 2013" and the exhibitions I've discussed accompanying the Video Art Archive Network Forum in Seoul all indicate how South Korea's visual arts organizations—those "new media"- and "post-museum"-focused, as well as the conventional galleries—had shared an ambitious pan-Asian emphasis. These shows presented works by artists living and working in a wide range of Asian territories all of which have experienced actual, postcolonial and now neocolonial histories, protracted sociopolitical crises and violent conflicts bound up with the Cold War and subsequent global neoliberal capitalist exploitation. The borders created—those relatively open or closed, peacefully policed, "demilitarized" or mired in state and popular violence—have been literal and physical. The disputes they symbolize, entrench and embody are also deeply rooted in factors of class, gender, nation, ethnic, political and religion affiliation. These, in turn, are bound up with vast regional and global labor migration processes, issues of personal and group safety, and access to resources for people of all kinds, including artists.

The "Asia Curator Network"—a project closely linked to the Moving On Asia publishing initiative—has brought together dozens of curators and artists over a ten-year period through involvement in a series of annual exhibitions and related events. Moving On Asia's plan was explicitly to work on projects "transcending any regional limitations" within the continent, operating a decentralized "horizontal structure" bringing together its members "based on independence and autonomy." Its intention, its spokesman-curator linsuk Suh has claimed, was to "spearhead a new type of moving image confined by no boundaries and signifying the integration and expansion of different media art genres"—building "a new cultural identity in the 'glocal era' of the 21st century and [breaking] down stereotypes of Asian culture."54 Territories such as Singapore and the Philippines, in addition to Hong Kong and South Korea, as we have seen in this chapter, have all produced movingimage works dealing with the complex legacies of their respective colonial pasts, diversely postcolonial histories and presents. These cannot be reduced to a single or simple point of origin, such as the myth of a "Raffles" colonial figure in Singapore, displaced from this founding role as the British colonialist is in Tzu Nyen Ho's 23-minute single-channel video film Utama: Every Name in History in 1 (2000) which explores another figure—Utama—reputed to be the first indigenous "king" of the Malays. Other recent films by Tzu Nyen Ho have excavated broader mythmaking aspects to Singaporean history and identities.55

Singapore, as much as South Korea, has been the site of a kind of hypermodernity of industrial high-tech development in the global neoliberal era emergent since the 1980s. Its video artists, responding to this history, have focused on themes of social alienation, migration and multiculturalism. The city-state has highly complex relationships with contiguous Malaysia, the PRC and the vast greater-Asian diaspora of "sino-culture" prevalent in the region. At the same time, Singapore hosts surviving oral performance traditions amid the territory's swiftly modernizing mass media. Once again, digital video/cable/satellite and internet practices have been used by artists as tools of political critique—in a "remediation" process, as one commentator calls it—in order to assess how the still authoritarian Singapore state used such technologies itself as major propaganda weapons in the period of 1960s–1990s.

Ka Fai Choy's 2010 research-based installation *Lan Fang Chronicles*, for example, used video to survey an area of Indonesia (once West Borneo) involved in a republican experiment for over 100 years by Hakka Chinese living in the area. This work critically illuminated, through implicit contrast, Singapore's own authoritarian political order, as well as its diasporic foundations. To the north, in Vietnam, the DOCLAB center for new media in Hanoi—like Loop Alternative Space in Seoul—has begun to try to encourage local audiences and an alternative political and cultural scene, exhibiting a commitment to "cultural democracy" through use of new media technologies

and innovative forms of social engagement. However, government censorship of artists and visual arts organizations there continues to inhibit this process.<sup>57</sup>

If such moving image and installation works (and those works combining these practices) are the primary "global art forms" then this is perhaps because in some fundamental ways they must embody/signify nowhere and everywhere at once—and by so doing radically problematize inherited, preglobalization notions and myths of "origin," "in-ness," "of-ness" and "from-ness." These arguments have been highlighted recently in a study of Palestinian contemporary art, subject of this book's final chapter.<sup>58</sup> Critic and curator Johan Pijnappel identified the same complex of problems in an essay on contemporary art "in" India, noting that "the whole question 'Who or what comes from where, and what does it mean?' has become much more complicated."

Nationality is not a reliable guide, Pijnappel observed, as many artists—based in Asia and elsewhere—have what he called a "cross-cultural biography" inflected by experiences of immigration, colonization, dislocation, ethnic identity and language negotiation. <sup>59</sup> The artists Nalini Malani and Shilpa Gupta, for instance, often referred to as "contemporary Indian artists," in fact have much more complex social identities. Malini was born in Karachi in 1946 (when it was still part of India, not Pakistan) and Gupta comes from the Agrawal community of cosmopolitan traders in northern India. Both artists have highly differentiated senses of personal and group identity, origins and interests. What do the seemingly once assured prepositions "in" and "from" come to mean, then, given this inter- and intranational complexity, setting aside the many other forms of possible identification and affiliation? I turn to artworks by Malani and Gupta in the following chapter.

What meaningful distinctions between "Western" and "non-Western" art and artists are tenable now, given the deracinating processes and consequences of globalization? Could the differences and oppositions this dyad once clearly represented have been eradicated, even though the world's leading galleries and museums are still generally held to be those in the United States and Europe? But how is this assumed "lead" or "superiority" to be defined, measured and assessed? In what does it consist? Is the claim of Western superiority in the visual arts actually just a prejudicial supposition made by certain critics and commentators (including this one)?

It is factually true that up to 2008 the highest prices for contemporary artworks made and sold by Western artists—those, that is, "from" the United States and Europe—certainly still towered above those made by artists "from" South Korea, India and the PRC. But what is the final or long-term historical value of this quantitative measure in making critical, evaluative judgements? Might it not just enable some influential players in the art world system to reconfirm and have reproduced their own values, backgrounds and interests (the view expressed in the debate about the art market held at Art Basel Hong

Kong, discussed in the previous chapter)?<sup>60</sup> The organizers of the first Kochi Biennale in India, held in 2012–2013, certainly wanted their event—involving 50% of works, they declared proudly, made by artists "from" India—to be seen to rank alongside the best of such exhibitions held in the West. They drew explicit, ambitious parallels with Venice, no less—the home of the first biennial exhibition in 1895, a city with which Kochi, the organizers claimed, shared an historic trading and cultural tradition. Was such a comparison just rhetoric and sales pitch?

But Pijnappel's concerns, however, were not just about cultural and artistic relations between nation-states, regions and continents. They were about all the borders involved in "West-versus-East" thinking—including neo-orientalism and a "reverse-orientalism" (Occidentalism) produced when, for instance, Indian artists have made what Pijnappel called superficial work while on grantaided visits to Western countries. But what then, under globalized conditions, do really "deep" and meaningful artworks look like? How can they be recognized? Publics inside India for contemporary art can be as divided (and venues as divisive) as publics and venues anywhere else. The background to the establishment of the Kochi Biennale in traditionally "communist" Kerala province demonstrates this.

Pijnappel himself chose not to show Malani's 2008 work *Remembering Toba Tek Singh* in the elite space of the then new extension to the National Gallery of Modern Art in Delhi, an institution which mostly attracted Western tourists and the Indian capital city's cultural elite. Malani's video/play installation project was concerned with India's and Pakistan's aggressively competitive nuclear testing programmes in the 1960s. The work's title refers to a town in the Punjab province of Pakistan renowned for its religious and social tolerance (the name of which wasn't changed, as most were after Partition in 1947, to reflect the dominant Muslim population). Instead, in an attempt to reach a much wider public, Malani's installation was displayed in the Coomaraswarmy Hall in Mumbai—key location of India's historic proto-national debates in the 1930s and 1940s—in order to attract a larger number and diverse mix of visitors from all classes, religions, ethnic groups and castes. It is to these wider historical and societal conditions of the global contemporary art world in India, within and out of which the Kochi Biennale has emerged, that I turn now.
