## Globalizing Indian Contemporary Art: The Biennial as Rhetorical Form

"Every morning before facing the world, I face myself. Every morning as I get ready to meet my every day challenges, I look at myself in the mirror. The crystal clear image fills me with confidence. Inspires me to go ahead and take the challenges, head on. In a moment I know that nothing is beyond my reach. And no goal too far. Much like Modiguard mirrors. The face of a confident and vibrant India. Bringing the world's finest technologies to give you unmistakably clear and distortion free mirrors." Peering through my own not so clear glasses and rubbing my sore eyes as the cramped seven hour flight from Heathrow to Mumbai nears its end I notice that the young Indian businesswoman pictured in the ad I am reading for Modiguard ("Honest mirrors") has the pallor of someone who's stayed indoors admiring her own image for too long. The Jetwings inflight magazine I've been reading, in fact, seems to feature a surfeit of less than healthy looking individuals, once I begin to examine the range. These nouveau riche entrepreneurs and their families have certainly made it—evidenced by their private gated-community new-build homes, butler service, private schools offering Cambridge University entrance exams and "state-of-the-art security." But many either have the same pallid look as this Echo of Narcissus or—and this is the obverse problem caused by the affluence now besetting the subcontinent's elite—appear increasingly to suffer from obesity. Thankfully, however, as other adverts attest, professionals are on hand to deal with this eventuality. Jetwings also features India's "First Biennale of Contemporary Art," the opening of which I am travelling to attend in Kochi, in the southern state of Kerala. Interspersed between spreads on gated enclaves in Mumbai, six-star hotels such as the Taj Coromandel in Chennai ("Forever classic. Forever Contemporary") and "ultra luxury homes" in Bangalore "sculpted to suit only the very upper crust," is an ad exhorting me to "Take a holiday in Kerala, God's own country"... I sit back, in so far as this is possible in my economy seat. The plane dips down toward Mumbai and I see that

the slum-dog houses run right alongside the runway, offering a very different kind of "incomparable lifestyle"...

#### The Kochi Biennale Big Sell

On the same day that Shri Oomen Chandy, the Honourable Chief Minister of Kerala opened the first Kochi Biennale in December 2012, he also launched what was called the "Grand Kerala Non-Stop Shopping Surprises" event. This was a 48-day "festival of consumption" in which 10 000 local businesses participated, according to *The Hindu* newspaper. Neoliberal capitalism in India was riding high, The Times of India confirmed ebulliently, in an article with the breezily rhetorical title "Can You Bet on Consumption?" From one perspective encapsulated in the global neoliberal world order mediated by magazines such as *letwings* there is now a pan-Asian continuum of elite consumerism. This comprises the goods, services and experiences of luxury hotels and upperclass travel, private housing, private education, high-performance cars, fashion, accessories and contemporary art.

The magazine Alef, for instance, self-styled as the "Cultural Journal to the Gulf," available free at airports across the region stretching from the oil-rich sultanates in the north to Kochi itself near the southern tip of India, recently celebrated what it called Qatar's "first women's luxury brand" range. The creation of this collection of "bespoke" leather goods, shoes, jewelry and couture had been superintended by an imported, and also luxurious, French "master craftsman." A single pair of shoes destined for the state's fashion elite consumers would, the company responsible reassuringly boasted, take their artisans at least three months to make. Alef also carried a story about Damien Hirst's first one-man show that had just opened at the Alriwag Doha gallery in Qatar's capital city. The equally high-end Italian and French curators imported to run the exhibition program of the Qatar Museums Authority reminded readers that Hirst, with his art's vaunted "complex" philosophical themes of life, death and love, was also "one of today's most commercially successful, as well as enduringly popular artists."<sup>3</sup>

Luxury, however, can be made available it seems to the (relative) masses. Organizers of the first Kochi Biennale announced that it had attracted 10 000 visitors in its first three days, and claimed that by the end of its three-month run in March 2013 it should have hit the target of 800 000. The exhibition would "give a new face to Kerala's cultural tourism," an unnamed member of the "Kochi Biennale foundation" board noted. The "Doubting Thomases" who had opposed government funding for the event on the grounds that international contemporary art was elitist would be proved wrong. (Note the religious reference—there is an association of St Thomas the Apostle with the ancient nearby city of Muziris.) Kochi, however, had no reputation as a center for the

visual arts in India. Moreover, the communist Kerala state government that had originally pledged financial support for the event fell from power in 2011 and this funding had not materialized. Nevertheless, the new biennial, its organizers and supporters declared, would constitute both a local and a national triumph.<sup>4</sup>

Those responsible for articulating the purpose and character of the first edition apparently saw no conflict of interests within this extension of "cultural tourism." Kochi, after all, was close to ancient but now lost Muziris, the "prosperous seaport" of the first century CE. Muziris, primordial nexus of Indo-Roman Empire-Greek trade routes, was redescribed if not reinvented in Biennale literature publicity as a 2000-year-old "business and cultural center," however much now dilapidated and unvisited.<sup>5</sup> Represented as an intrinsic part of "Kerala's cosmopolitanism in antiquity," Muziris features in the biennial's promotional materials as no less than a powerhouse of globalization millennia avant le lettre.

Warming to this theme, the significance of Muziris now, in terms of its inspiration for modern Kochi, was sketched in. Publicity material reported that the lost city's:

goods, ideas, technology and other creative expressions [accompanied] the movement of people across geographical zones, often crossing socio-cultural boundaries [creating] a unique social platform for heterogeneous cultural practices [...] no single strand [was] responsible for the creation of cultures. Each [was] dependent upon another and the other [...] spread great distances.<sup>6</sup>

The inauguration of the Kochi Biennale would enable Kerala to transcend its hitherto limited tourism trade, largely based on "beaches, back waters and forests"—in other words, the standard Goa India beach holiday offer. Instead, the existing "Muziris Heritage" project, involving conservation, restoration, history, environmental projects and "development of crafts and art forms, occupational and other community activities" was depicted as a kind of omnicultural endeavor of which the biennial would become a natural extension. If the world had once gone to and from Muziris—sent its traders and products through its roads and waterways, to be followed by Jewish, Syrian-Christian, Islamic, Buddhist and Roman settlers—then this "long lost cosmopolitanism," re-presented now as a confluence of "multiple cultural streams," was to be reborn, via "a new social platform" in Kochi, as the biennial opened its doors for the first time to the world, and to the global contemporary art world, on December 12, 2012.

The exhibition took place, however, in a series of buildings and spaces that signify the more recent, less palatable history of southern India—that dominated by the serial conquest and occupation of what is now Kerala state by Portuguese, Dutch and British explorers, soldiers, traders and their "settler"

populations in the era from the 16th to the mid-twentieth century. The biennial's opening ceremony took place at Parade Ground, Fort Kochi, an open area along with bordering public spaces where the Portuguese had stored arms and the Dutch and British drilled their troops (Vasco de Gama, who "discovered" this territory and claimed it for Western Europeans, was buried nearby in 1524). Many of the artworks displayed in the first Kochi Biennale were shown in the complex of buildings and open spaces at Aspinwall House—originally the business premises of the English trader Aspinwall and Co., established in 1867. David Hall, a site for more artworks, was a bungalow built by the Dutch East India Company around 1695, named after a Jewish merchant, David Koder, who lived in the house (refurbished recently as an "eco-hotel"). Other artworks were installed in the grounds of the Cochin Club, an early nineteenth-century "exclusive British Gentlemen's Club" which now, though still a private social institution, deigns to admit women.

Durbar Hall was built as a royal court in the 1850s by the Maharaja of Cochin (Kochi's name rendered in English under British rule). It was transformed, exhibition publicity claimed, into a "gallery of international museum quality" for use by the Biennale curators. This institution is run by Lalitha Kala Akademi—India's National Academy of Arts—the organizers of the now defunct "Indian Triennial" exhibition established in the 1960s, whose relation to the Kochi Biennale I consider later. Kerala's chief minister claimed Durbar Hall was "Asia's best gallery." Two further sites for the biennial's artworks and related activities indicated the basis of Kerala's historic economic and trading significance. These were Pepper House, on the waterfront near Kochi, and Mattancherry Bazar Road. The former is a large warehouse facility or "godown," built by the Dutch. The latter thoroughfare was the hub of the trade in tea, spices, cashews and dried fruits, along with other commodities such as rubber, some buildings along which remain used for freight storage. 10

Ninety artists were featured in the first edition of the Biennale, representing over 20 countries, though more than half of the total, the organizers explained, were of Indian origin. This selection was intended to signal that the organizers, and their backers, would not be in the business of simply importing generic "global" artists drawn from some generic "global biennial" template. However, some very well-known Indian, and "globally successful" artists, including Sudarshan Shetty, Subodh Gupta and Vivan Sundaram, did participate. The Kochi Biennale, here matching the global template, also included "collateral" programs of performances by musicians and drama groups, film showings, seminars, guest lectures, conversations and panel discussions—intensively so, at least at the outset of the exhibition. 11

The first edition of the biennial attracted financial support from organizations within the usual spectrum—state/public, private/non-profit and some ambiguously in-between—based in India, the broader Asian region and globally. The Australian government, for instance, was involved, through both its

cultural-diplomatic International Council (supporting its own artists taking part in the show) and its department for foreign affairs and trade. The Goethe Institute Max Mueller Bhavan, a German state cultural-diplomatic agency, and the Staatliche Kunstsammlungen Dresden (a provincial state art museum), also provided subvention to Kochi. BMW cars and Tata Enterprises—the giant Indian global multinational corporation—were prominent commercial "partners," a term key to the language and activities of the global contemporary art world. Hong Kong's Asia Art Archive, along with the international Biennial Foundation, based in Switzerland, helped co-develop the Kochi Biennale's symposia program. Several Gulf states-based arts and culture organizations were also involved. The leading practical organizer and funder of the Biennale in 2012–13 was the global advertising agency Wieden + Kennedy, operating from a base in Delhi. 12

The Biennale attracted support in kind from the Kerala state government and the Cochin Corporation (a Kochi city foundation), along with money from civil society arts and cultural organizations—including the Kerala Lalit Kala Academy, the Kendra Sangeet Natak Academy, the Gurjal Foundation India and Sangita Jindal. No financial contribution, however, was made by the Indian national government. The Kochi Biennale came to be represented as a Kerala state event, set within what the organizers subtly chose to call "the global cultural map"—meaning India as a whole, and the whole world beyond. The previous involvement of Indian central governments in high-profile national arts events, such as the Indian Triennial, defunct since 2008—widely perceived by the turn of the millennium to have become simultaneously lackluster aesthetically and politically propagandistic—inhibited players on both sides in the run up to the first edition of the Biennale. 13 Besides, the proponents and forces of neoliberal India had, since the 1990s, fostered a very different ethos that I turn to later. Nevertheless, the Kochi Biennale organizers certainly understood that sustaining local support for their event required that, as well as emphasizing the important participation of local visual artists, traditional regional cultural practices (here effectively meaning tribal music and ritual performance) had to be foregrounded strongly in the exhibition's "collateral events" program. 14

#### Kochi as a Global Venue

Kochi's mayor in 2012, Tony Chammani, in his supporting address at the inauguration of the Biennale, observed that it had become nothing less than "mandatory for a thriving city like Kochi" to have this event. The exhibition, further, would, he claimed, "elevate Kochi to the elite list of cities holding biennales" and enable it to reap "its economic, cultural and trade benefits." Perhaps it was inevitable that its organizers and supporters would attempt to draw parallels with Venice—home of the first biennial and renowned for its

intercontinental cosmopolitan trading, arts and cultural significance since the Renaissance. Hormis Tharakan, former director-general of police in Kerala and retired secretary to the Indian national government cabinet, wrote effusively, comparing Venice and Kochi, in the biennial's in-house magazine, *Biennale Leaf*, in December 2012. This essay is exemplary of ubiquitous contemporary art world publicity, combining analytic and rhetorical elements—an instance of well-intentioned "boosterism" that suggests its author had become carried away with an attractive though fanciful conceit.

In the fourteenth century, Tharakan claimed, for example, Cochin (as the city was then called) actually did rival Venice, given its monopoly on "black gold" (pepper) and other spices. Merchants from Venice and Constantinople, Alexandria and Arabia, Java and China visited the city to trade, "pay their respects to the powerful local Kings or just to gawk at the grand palaces, avenues and bazaars." Venice was to lose its independence as a republic in 1797, when the city-state was captured by Napoleon Bonaparte's forces. Two years earlier Fort Kochi had been wrested by the British from the Dutch, on the pretext that the latter had sided with the French dictator. Weaving together the history and fate of the two centers through such sleight-of-hand narrative devices, Tharakan finally asks rhetorically if Kochi, like Venice, can revive itself as an international art capital in the manner achieved by its putative historic competitor since the late nineteenth century. "Will Kochi," he muses, "reclaim its rightful place among the metropolises of the world through the medium of art as Venice did over the last century?" 17

The fact that now more than 150 biennials operate across four continents indicates, rather, that the international contemporary art exhibition-as-cultural-tourism format has become a staple vehicle for the socioeconomic "regenerative" ambitions of many cities, regions and nation-states. Each biennial, however, entails specific, irreducible originating features, environmental and historical resources, infrastructural aspects and support organizations. An international body, the "not-for-profit" Biennial Foundation, was created in 2009 to liaise between and provide specialist help for members of this growing constituency—which one commentator has called the "global biennial community." At the same time, the mix of state, NGO and commercial agencies involved in funding particular biennials (and to some degree art fairs too) around the world indicates the existence of a complex web of active and interactive political, diplomatic and "aid"/"development" agencies. These operate both in concert, and sometimes less co-operatively, to attempt to secure a range of different though interlinked economic, social and cultural objectives. <sup>18</sup>

Kochi Biennale's originators were two Mumbai-based artists raised in Kerala: Bose Krishnamacahari and Riyas Komu. This enabled the organization they set up to claim with some credibility that its philosophy and ethos was authentically rooted in the place where the exhibition would be held and steered by producers of art, rather than by what might be presented by hostile

commentators as an elitist transnational "professional curatorship" parachuted in on expense accounts. This argument was to prove especially significant in the biennial's developmental dialogue with suspicious Keralan groups and individuals. Local writers such as Paul Zacharia, and film-maker Adoor Gopalakrishnan (who selected films for the collateral program), defended the Biennale initiative and Kerala state's financial support for it. Importantly, their testimonies were given credence because the event's organizers had assiduously attended to sectoral interests in the local political landscape. Inclusion of Keralan artists and "traditional" cultural activities really mattered, because the region's dominant Malayalis tribe, which provides Kerala with its local language, regularly holds highly popular festivals of theater, music and cinema. This is another example of how the global art world nests together some apparently odd bedfellows—strange, if not openly antagonistic—for symbiotic benefits that can be social, political and aesthetic. 19

In the run-up to the Biennale's opening, promised funding from Kerala state sources was interdicted whilst these sometimes testy debates about the purpose, character and value of the biennial were played out. Who or what was it really for? What would be the actual as opposed to the claimed benefits for local people? Wasn't contemporary visual art intrinsically an alien, elite form irrespective of whether certain Indian artists were themselves now prominent amongst its recognized globally successful practitioners? Arguments about the rights and wrongs of globalization per se, projected as an abstract and imposed process, became implicated in these exchanges. They were also related to historical debates about India's colonization, decolonization, its socialist and nonaligned phases in the 1950s and 1960s, and the rise of neoliberal capitalism after the end of the Cold War in the early 1990s.<sup>20</sup>

In the end, the initiative happened because local government, businesses and landowners, acting through the Greater Cochin Development Authority—an example of the classic late modern socioeconomic composite "regeneration agency"—granted the Biennale free use of all its venues. The "munificence of the corporate world of Kochi" also identified, the organizers claimed, a higher good beyond this effort to enhance the region's "cultural tourism." This was that the Biennale would bring what was called "great glory to Kochi, Kerala and India." The real achievement here was that the first edition, according to Ranjit Hoskote (billed by the Biennale's script writers as the "towering" critic and curator who'd organized India's national pavilion at Venice in 2011) exemplified genuine artistic "pluralism." <sup>21</sup>This achievement was also a diplomatic victory of sorts.

Not in Delhi or Mumbai, then—where together the great mass of professional artists based in India live and work—but in Kochi, more than a thousand kilometers away in the rural south, was it predicted by such experts as Hoskote that a new center of artistic activity would develop. The Biennale would offer what he called "a vibrant space and enduring platform which will become one

of India's primary artistic and cultural contributions to society"—nothing less than a "sustainable platform for contemporary art in India." The "pluralism" that Hoskote referred to meant the Biennale's astute but precarious balancing of competing interests. This was its declared aim, on the one hand, to "pay homage to India's traditions and heritage by embracing its history of poetry and performance," whilst, on the other, "inviting audiences to experience new cultural and artistic expressions." We enter, here, once again, the world of the rhetorically vague if not ineffable. Claims, that is, that can really be neither proved nor disproved, whose effect, though benign and well intentioned, is really in the end to blur boundaries between facts and hopes—reasonable or otherwise. Such claims have become the contemporary art world's default promotional discourse, the lingua franca of the "global biennial community" which Kochi joined on that iconic date, 12.12.12.<sup>22</sup>

#### Art at Kochi: Liberal Pluralism Versus Social **Engagement with Globalization?**

More than forty artists who participated in the Biennale made preliminary visits to the area—ostensibly an indication that they took seriously their stated intention to produce authentic "site-specific" works for the exhibition. These included Subodh Gupta, Nalini Malani, Joseph Semah, Jonas Staal and Amar Kanwar, Some others, such as Vivan Sundaram, chose to use local materials in the fabrication of their commissions. Sundaram took glass, copper and terracotta fragments along with earth from the nearby coastal area at Pattanam and re-assembled them within a large "earthwork" installation entitled Black Gold in a warehouse at Aspinwall House on the waterside, accompanied by a video.

The resulting container resembled an archaeological dig that was both a deposit and a manifest: what was the truth, or assumption about truths about the material and historical past of lands and peoples, that this work was trying to tease out?<sup>23</sup> It is worth recalling critic Johan Pijnappel's doubts about the global contemporary art world's language of geographical—cultural "authenticity" and "origination." Malani, as I pointed out, was born in Karachi (now in Pakistan, but once part of an undivided India under British rule), though she is often identified unambiguously as an "Indian artist." Regional and national identities, perhaps particularly in Asian states wracked by postcolonial borders such as those drawn up to divide India, Pakistan and later Bangladesh, are thus often jarringly at odds.<sup>24</sup>

In what senses, if any, can the art form supposedly global above all others, the video installation (a fair number of which populated the first Kochi Biennale, indicating its intent to showcase what its publicity hand out called "new cultural and artistic expressions") be said to originate "from" or authentically "be of" an actual place? Pijnappel's essay was concerned with the development

![](_page_106_Picture_2.jpeg)

Figure 8 Vivan Sundaram Black Gold (mixed media, installation, 2012).

of what he called twenty-first century "Indian video art"—a term which might itself, given his broad reservations, already seem to presume far too much. Two of the artists he discussed had recently made work specifically on this theme of the apparently essential "in-essential" quality to such globalized contemporary art forms that, in technological-aesthetic terms, are necessarily "from" everywhere and nowhere simultaneously. Sharmila Samant's 1998 short animation video *Global Clones* (not featured in the Kochi Biennale) pictured a Dutch clog morphed from the earlier form of a leather *chappal* (slipper) as it travelled, metaphorically, along the Silk Road that runs through Kochi.

Shilpa Gupta's 2002 video *Kidney Supermarket* envisioned another form of apparently magical transformation—that based on the cash nexus—with which the Asian mass poor have had to engage in ever-increasingly extreme ways in an attempt to make ends meet.<sup>25</sup> In this work Gupta placed a model of a seventeenth-century Dutch East India Company ship symbolizing global capitalism on top of a video monitor showing an advert for how Westerners can go about buying Asian human kidneys. Her subsequent work *Blame* (2003)—labelled "bottles of blame" to be bought and sold by Hindus and Muslims—represented the artist's disgusted reaction to the sectarian ethno-religious riots and mass killings of 2002–2003 in Gujurat, stoked by BJP nationalists and Islamic fundamentalists. Texts on the bottles read: "Blaming you makes me feel so good ... So I blame you for what I can't control: Your religion. Your nationality." This work points to another "border zone" of community/caste/class tensions and

violence inside the Indian nation-state to be set alongside the existing and sometimes intense georegional disputes with the country's neighbors.<sup>26</sup>

Subodh Gupta's 2008 sculpture *Line of Control* is a spectacular atom bomb mushroom cloud form made out of hundreds of steel kitchen utensils invisibly fixed together. It refers to the Hiroshima and Nagasaki airborne detonations over Japan by the United States in 1945 and to the continuing military standoff between India and Pakistan. These domestic implements of daily life and its reproduction are set together in a drastic contrast with the threat of apocalvptic devastation through nuclear war. The form itself seems to be just on the point of an explosion into formlessness. India's and Pakistan's longstanding dispute has been felt most sharply in the border areas of both countries, and principally in the state of Kashmir (the border dividing the two countries was originally named "the Line of Control" by the British). 27 Gupta's *Line of Control* is housed in the Kiran Nadar Museum of Art (KNMA) in Delhi, the first private contemporary art museum in India, named after the wife of the media and telecommunications billionaire Shri Nadar.

For the first edition of the Kochi Biennale, however, Gupta presented a large traditional boat, suspended at a dramatic angle by wires and filled with fishing utensils and tools of varying kinds which he'd found in the area during his preliminary visits. This choice was clearly an attempt to make his work literally "from" local materials, which was then sited in a storage building just off the central Aspinwall House warehouses on the Kochi dockside (next to Vivan Sundaram's Black Gold). Gupta's work, Untitled, was low-key in political and aesthetic terms, in contrast with Line of Control—the former planned and likely, that is, to appeal to a range of publics, tastes and expectations and not aiming to offend or antagonize anyone.

*Untitled* presented a sharp contrast with the work by Nalini Malani shown in Kochi. Exhibition of the two pieces indicated the pluralist intent of the Biennale's organizers and backers. Malani's originally six-channel video piece In Search of Vanished Blood (2012) dealt with the issue of gang rape of women in contemporary India.

For the Biennale it was shown in a simplified form, with one large screen displaying a map of the world centered on the United States, "the eye of the storm" that is, Malani stated in her commentary, late modernity. With an agitated montaged sound track suggesting violence and malevolence, In Search of Vanished Blood attempted an anatomy of global modernization's incitement to sexual abuse and murder of women. It proposed an interrogation of new capitalist commodification of the female body within the geospatial frame of a "traditional" Indian society still rooted in repression and intolerance of sexual, ethnic, religious, class, caste and regional differences. The work's title, indicating Malani's political positioning, was taken from Agha Shadid Ali's translation of the classic Urdu poem Lahu Ka Surag by the left-wing Pakistani intellectual and revolutionary poet Faiz Ahmed Faiz. Malani's feminist position has remained

![](_page_108_Picture_2.jpeg)

Figure 9 Nalini Malani *In Search of Vanished Blood* (mixed media installation, 2012). *Source:*© Nalini Malani

consistent over the several decades of her career and her works—paintings, photographs, films, drawings, prints, videos, sculptures and performances—have always dealt directly with the sexual politics of the subcontinent.<sup>28</sup>

Kochi Biennale's first edition also attempted to broaden its representation of Asian modernities beyond India and its bordering states through the commission of what it called the "BRICS Project." This brought together eight video films made by artists working in the acronymic countries Brazil, Russia, India, China and South Africa. However, two of these producers were German and the curator of the project as a whole was Alfons Hug, a German critic and director of the Goethe Institute in Rio de Janiero. Shown in a number of small, separate rooms in an area off the central spaces of Aspinwall House, these films demonstrated only a loose thematic or symbolic coherence which, it might be concluded, actually reflect the real natures of the BRICS countries themselves.

The alliance between the first four states had started out in 2006 as an intended challenge to the economic dominance of the US and Western European countries within globalization processes (South Africa joined the BRIC countries in 2010). Though all, apart from Russia, newly industrialized, fast-growing economies with growing affluence—though the disparities of wealth within these states are also egregiously wide, especially in India—these states, with 40% of the world's population by 2014, have also been subject to rapid and often powerfully destabilizing forces of societal change, both before and after the Crash in 2008.<sup>30</sup>

One of the two Chinese videos featured in the BRICS project at Kochi was made by Gao Shiqiang. Called Revolution (2007, 42 minutes), it features a group of people discussing the clash of interests always present within the workings of the worldwide capitalist system. Such "antagonistic co-operation" understood as the basis of the global economy was symbolized by the juxtaposition of two different videos shown in the same room on different walls. Here, in his work Castell (2012, 18 minutes), the Brazilian artist Pablo Labato staged a large crowd of men and women attempting, with limited success, to form a human pyramid, while Chen Chieh-Jen's Pushing People (2008, 22 minutes) also dealt with human collective strenuous physical activity. But in this adjoining work we only see fragmented hands, arms and shoulders enlisted in order to attempt to push an unidentified large and heavy object across a dark room. Is the alienated labor of the global masses in the BRICS countries likely to be rewarded with general prosperity and a sense of collective ownership of society, these films seem to ask and to answer immediately in the negative (note the double meaning of the word "pushing" in the title of Chen Chieh-Jen's video). The conditions of economic globalization within neoliberal capitalism in Asia include both maximum exploitation of the weakest and reproduction of the division of the interests of the masses—or "the multitude," as two recent influential commentators have termed them. These objectives help to maximize profits for corporations and the accumulation of substantial private wealth for the comparatively tiny professional elites that have emerged in these societies since the turn of the millennium.<sup>31</sup>

The regenerative "cultural tourism" ethos that the organizers claimed underpinned the Kochi Biennale was a familiar one, based on the hope that the rising tide of corporate and professional elite affluence in India would eventually "raise all boats" in the state of Kerala, with additional "trickle down" benefits for the country as a whole. The event's promotional literature asserted that, for instance, "cultural and heritage visitors spend, on average, 35% more than the non-cultural tourists" and it was expected that 5 million visitors would come to Kochi during the three month period of the exhibition. What the organizers called the new model of "art-tourism and cultural diplomacy" had worked, they observed, as a form of regional regeneration in what they took to be comparable biennial cities such as Singapore, Gwangju in South Korea, Denver, USA and Liverpool in England. Their claim was that locating the biennial event in Kochi would draw "a significant number of national and international" arts projects to the region, creating significant employment and tax revenue for investment in Kerala. The director of the Singapore 2011 Biennial was quoted approvingly, and it was claimed that what was called, without definition or justification, "civic wellbeing" would improve once citizens were exposed to such a "major cutting-edge international exhibition of contemporary art." Kochi would receive what was alleged would be "a huge facelift in terms of glam and glitter cutting across social and cultural spectrums."32

Corporate sponsorship levels for the Biennale were set in order to encourage many different kinds of involvement and resulting benefits. No doubt the organizers were assisted in this calibration by expert advisors at the Biennial Foundation. "Founding Patron," "Friend," and "Official Partner" categories, for example, solicited support in a range of development areas included in the sociocultural remit of the Biennale. These included schools and colleges, learning and resource, arts award, ecological and regeneration, music, film/ documentary, and virtual/digital.<sup>33</sup> As I've noted, partners were identified internationally, across an ambiguous public-private spectrum, including some in the United Arab Emirates in the Gulf. India's market for contemporary art remains comparatively very weak—based almost entirely in the two major cities of Mumbai and Delhi-when set alongside, for example, Dubai. Here, for instance, Alef journal recorded sales by Christie's in January 2014 of modern and contemporary Arab, Iranian and Turkish art worth over US\$8 500 000. The (relatively) socially liberal centers in the Gulf, such as Dubai, Oatar, Abu Dhabi and Kuwait have begun to offer visual arts exhibition spaces and higher education programs for women—as well as developing their own biennial events, such as that at Sharjah, in the UAE, begun in 1993.<sup>34</sup>

The overwhelming basis of the Gulf states' enormous wealth remains the revenue gained from export sales of oil. In India, however, it is tax revenue raised from its working population—in a country where, despite growing affluence for a substantial minority, 400 million people out of a population of 1.21 billion earned less than US\$1.25 dollars per day and where wage inequalities are growing. 35 In the Gulf territories with developing markets for modern and contemporary art, the vast bulk of the profits are still made by and for the US-British auctioneers Christie's and Sotheby's, who maintain a slowly diminishing but still majority control over the global market for art as a whole.<sup>36</sup> India's often-celebrated status as the "world's largest democracy" jars with the authoritarian, monarchic and theocratic Gulf regimes lying to its northwest which are reliant for their growth and reproduction on very low-paid south Asian migrant workers who are allowed very few rights. The "political legitimation" troubles that beset elected leaders in Delhi and Kochi do not generally press on these rulers, such as the royal family of Saudi Arabia, where, for instance, the King Abdulaziz Center for World Culture (badged insouciantly as the Wahabi kingdom's "home for knowledge, creativity and cross-cultural engagement"), offers what has been called a multidisciplinary "platform for creative cultural development in the nation."37

Another artist from South Africa took part in the Kochi Biennale, though not as part of the BRICS video art project. Clifford Charles's also low-key, understated installation work, *Five Cloud Rooms*, housed in a small building in the grounds of Aspinwall House, suggestively recreated aspects of the history of Kochi, including its now fading socialist activism. Charles's first of five interconnected mini-environments, *The Communist Party Reading Room*, modeled

his experience of visiting such dusty spaces in the city where popular affiliation to the local Communist Party was once very strong—its supporters had for several decades controlled the Kerala state assembly. This work implied a broader foregrounding and consideration of India's national development since independence, the fate of its democracy given the embrace of neoliberal capitalism in the 1990s and the record on social development and human rights—in the Indian subcontinent and region as a whole.<sup>38</sup> Can contemporary art have a significant voice within discussion of these matters?

## The Highs and Lows of Art, Media and Kochi Biennale Capital

What might be termed the high theory of the purpose and value of contemporary art world biennials emphasizes their claimed radical "transnational" aesthetic, cultural and political potential. These ideas have been proposed by influential curators such as Nigerian, Okwui Enwesor, and Cuban, Gerardo Mosquera, who have between them developed and "guest edited" many of the "periphery" or "non-Western" recurrent exhibitions now in the contemporary art world calendar. These sociocritical concerns have not usually been set alongside but neither set explicitly against the instrumental "cultural tourism" role of biennials—although this putative socioeconomic regenerative function has certainly been far less foregrounded, to the point of virtual invisibility, in what have become the canonical essays.<sup>39</sup> While the first Kochi Biennale in 2012 foregrounded the latter role in its promotional materials, it also made some space for one of its invited artists, Delhi-born Amar Kanwar, to comment on the revolutionary-emancipatory discourse enunciated by global contemporary art world curator-stars such as Enwesor and Mosquera. This centered on the ability of biennials, Kanwar noted, to "break or redefine the notions of nationalism [...] and democracy" in their choice of artists, themes and exhibition formats.

Kanwar decided, however, mostly to sidestep this issue, especially as it related to Kochi, and instead advocated pragmatism. What he called the utopic "burden of hope" carried by biennials arose, he noted, from the "traps" of "fixed ambition and notion[s] of success." Kanwar's own contribution to the Biennale, a multimedia and interactive work, *The Sovereign Forest* (2012–), dealt with the compulsory acquisition of land in Orissa, India, by the government and mining corporations. The moving image component of this project, "The Scene of the Crime," offered a record of the land and its flora and fauna prior to its planned destruction. Crime, politics, human rights and ecology were thus at the center of Kanwar's work, which directly implicated the interests and actions of Indian national and regional governments, and asked

fundamental questions about the state's commitment to democracy and the real costs of neoliberal modernization. According to Kanwar, India's vaunted regional community diversity, in ethno-religious, caste and class terms, had suffered great strain and antagonism, as the Gujurat riots in 2002-2003 indicated. The sources of these conflicts had intensified and were more likely to recur because of the increasing exploitation of labor, land and environmental resources by the forces of global capitalism with the connivance of government.41

In an earlier historical moment, in the second half of the twentieth century. sociocultural change in India had registered more as a popular, positive advance, toward a national, or even "pan-Asian," identity, away from what had been perceived as constricting traditional regional mores. It is interesting that the term "other" in India can be used to refer both to foreign (that is, externalto-India) influences, but also to national rather than prevailing regional, small "c" conservative, values and habits. An example would be the modernization of women's dress in the Gujurati and Bengali communities that had started to occur in the 1980s and 1990s. I will return briefly to questions of fashion and dress in India later. The 2012 Kochi Biennale's commitment to showing films. both as part of the main exhibition and in its "collateral program," reflected the national and "pan-Asian" progressivism particularly associated with film—of both documentary and fictional kinds—since the days of the Independence movement when Indian democracy had been a force mobilized against British imperial rule. The Netpac organization ("Network for the Promotion of Asian Cinema," founded in 1996), for example, continues to support new filmmaking and celebrates film as a socially progressive force inside India, though it also works for greater Asian cooperation understood as a necessary form of continental cultural-intellectual Decolonization. 42 Screening and discussing films symbolized the pluralism of the Kochi Biennale in its earnest wish to attract many different groups—local, regional, national and international—to the exhibition.

Netpac, like the Kochi Biennale, works with a similar range of national and international organizations, as well as with the Confederation of Indian Industry on health, education, livelihood, "diversity management", development of skills and environment projects. (The neoliberal coinage of "entrepreneurship" and "Business for Livelihood" has steadily crept into advertisements by Netpac's sponsors in its in-house magazine.) The organization's 2013 conference, "Roots, Identity, Modernity: Asian Voices on Culture and Creativity" set out to examine the intra-Asian dimensions to the arts and culture broadly understood. Film itself was conceived, as several of the speakers indicated, to be the developed techno-cultural outcome of a number of premodern Asian "precinematic" visual forms and social rituals, including drawings on paper, shadow play and puppetry.

Film-making across many Asian societies since the 1980s has achieved a social and aesthetic status that the Kochi Biennial organizers desired for contemporary visual art in India. The film critic Latika Padgaonkar remarked that this confidence:

mirrored the resurgence of Asia, or at least of large swathes of the continent, as an economic powerhouse and as a major stakeholder in the emerging world order. Filmmakers showed both the talent and the willingness to exploit new opportunities of globalization without forsaking their engagement with the turmoil in their societies [...] Some make oblique comments on the prevailing moods and social tensions in their countries; others tackle them head-on.<sup>43</sup>

Kanwar's "The Scene of the Crime" could certainly fit into this last category. I've noted in earlier chapters that film and video artists in Hong Kong and South Korea have mobilized the moving image in this direct manner as a means of sociopolitical critique. In a way similar to the call by contemporary art curator Johnson Chang for people in Asian countries to understand and share the extent of their "self-colonization," veteran Netpac activists Aruna Vasudev and Wong Tuck-Cheong explain that film is a popular and effective means through which to articulate and understand the colonial experiences of India's and China's Asian neighbors. This recognition of a "common heritage" was delayed or blocked as the people in these postcolonial countries turned their attention to "urgent internal reconstruction" and consolidating the "gains" from their "colonial masters." Through film, Vasudev and Tuck-Cheong observe, a facility exists to "examine Orientalism and National Identity as well as the cinema's evolving identity in the age of globalism and the development of new technologies."44 This process is intimately tied up with all aspects of pan-Asian modernity and modernization as well as with the development of contemporary visual art in India (of which film and video art is a part) and the first edition of the Kochi Biennale.

The "Roots, Identity, Modernity" conference also sought to bring together discussion of film with that of design, fashion, craft, literature and the visual arts. (In a telling contrast with the Kochi Biennale, the Ministry of Culture of the central Indian Government financially supported the conference, indicating that film has a sociocultural significance and popularity in India that contemporary visual art currently lacks.) Had modernity been a "Western-centric" Enlightenment notion and process, Vasudev and Tuck-Cheong ask; an external reality based on the dissemination of supposed "universal ideas" brought to the colonized world in the form of "the streamlined precincts of the mechanised technology modes"? If so, then perhaps this domination, and the worship of Western modernization by some colonized groups that grew up around it, might help to explain why "historical contacts between Asian countries"

themselves have been long overshadowed. Full recognition of these intra-cultural roots had been at the core of anti-imperialist writer Rabindranath Tagore's vision of "an Asian connect in the early twentieth century." Examples of this pan-Asian cultural integration included, according to Vasudev and Tuck-Cheong, the influence of Japanese art on the work of Indian artists such as Nandlal Bose, that of Chinese embroideries on the Parsis in India, the recent "iconic following of Tamil film-superstar Rajneekanth in Japan," and in music collaborations between Pakistan and India 45

These innovations constituted a living legacy of transnational Asian cosmopolitanism, a "commonality" "in the celebration of the multitudes of technologies including the erraticism of the hand-made, in the imagination of the arts to transform a troubled world, and in the exploration of new cultural paradigms as the foundation for new global conversations"46

Such intra-Asian connections also extended beyond Asia—as part of "global Asia"—back and forward into the metropolitan post-imperial centers of the West. Design critic Jahnvi Dameron Nandan has related how Jawaharlal Nehru (India's first prime minister after Independence in 1947), on a trip back from Beijing in the early 1960s, purchased a Mandarin-collared women's jacket and asked his tailor to replicate one for him. Nehru had this jacket made in numerous lengths, added a rose in the buttonhole and paired it with white *Churidaar* pants. The Beatles famously adopted the item and turned it into a day coat. James Bond villains wore versions of it in the films Dr No and Tomorrow Never Dies. It re-emerged in *The Matrix*, worn by hacker Neo, and more recently in the *Harry Potter* films worn by the magician Severus Snape.

Installations, or films, or jackets can thus become influential and powerfully symbolic. The "Nehru jacket" carried with it something of the optimism of 1950s and 1960s India when the country began to become influential globally as the superpowers' stand-off got complicated by the rise of the "Third World" Non-Aligned movement and regional disputes distanced the hitherto allies China, the Soviet Union and India. Like Vasudev and Tuck-Cheong, Nandan points to a number of intra-Asian style innovations in design and fashion, what she calls a "fusion of Indian and non-Indian concepts" that had "always been an Indian strength." These had included Indian *Parsigara* and Tanchoi textiles influenced in their design and manufacture by Chinese embroidery and weaving, Japanese-art inspired tea Calico textiles and Burma teak ceilings.47

The ubiquitous "Ambassador" car was another example of a customized import, subject to what Nandan terms "Indian-isation." This model was introduced into the Indian market in 1957, based on the British 1948 Morris Oxford series. Like other artefacts subject to innumerably varied popular, anonymous adaptations—such as the *lota*, the small handle-less brass or copper vessel in everyday use in India-the Ambassador, Nandan says, "was considered efficient, all terrain, and its roomy interiors carried everything from poultry chicken to up to ten human passengers at a time."48 Subodh Gupta made two oil paintings of the car, both of which he entitled *Doot* ("messenger"), as well as a full-size aluminum cast of the car, in 2003. Many thousands of Ambassadors still choke India's roads and it wasn't until the mid-1970s that smaller, more efficient Japanese cars, such as the 800 cc Maruti Suzuki, became popular. That model's arrival indicated the continuing demise of British influence in India since decolonization and the rise of globalized automobile production that was rendering British-made cars and the workers responsible for them redundant. The "message" had changed, Gupta's use of the car-as-symbol in 2003 suggests, and India's neoliberal turn a decade earlier would be decisive in producing societal transformations at all levels in the country. Emblematic of this turn is the history and role of the biggest corporate supporter of the Kochi Biennale.

The Weiden + Kennedy global advertising agency—key sponsors as well as involved in the practical organization of the exhibition—relocated their Indian offices and purpose-built gallery to a space within a newly opened shopping center in south Delhi in 2010. These buildings were part of a vast development complex owned by the DLF (Delhi, Land and Finance) corporation, and one of the largest retail spaces in the city. DLF's ownership effectively superseded the city government's own control of urban space in the city that had been previously overseen by the DDA (Delhi Development Authority), DLF is owned by Kushal Pal Singh, rated the 98th richest man in the world by Forbes magazine in 2009 and the world's wealthiest property developer. Another weather-beaten Ambassador car, this time a crashed, broken and graffiti-clad specimen, was positioned outside the shopping center entrance as an evidently graphic symbol—not clearly not an "artwork"—of pre-globalized India, suggesting a sharp contrast with the values of neoliberalism, consumerism and advertising culture that the shopping center itself represents.<sup>49</sup>

The South Court of the DLF center also houses KNMA, the first private art museum in India and home to Gupta's mushroom cloud tower of stainless steel pots and pans, *Line of Control*. The shopping center has been called "design generic." According to one commentator, it is "hyper-ordinary in appearance" and one capable of being built anywhere in the globalized world. In the case of Delhi's iteration, however, the poor and homeless of the city began to use its public toilets and other spaces for their daily living activities. The retail complex rapidly became itself a poignant index of the extremes of mass poverty and minority affluence that Delhi and neoliberal India as a whole manifests. The shift from DDA to DLF represents another kind of border or "line of control" crossed. From, that is, a residual post-Independence state-socialist paternalism based on nominal public control of India's environment and resources to an economic and sociocultural neoliberalization—part of an ongoing global process—with its "trickle down" theory of wealth redistribution and actual chaotic societal upheaval.

## Utopian Rhetoric in Contemporary Art at the Kochi Biennale

A report issued in 2008 claimed that 65% of all luxury consumption in India occurred in just five neighborhoods—four located in Mumbai and Delhi, and one in Punjab. Since then, affluence has, it seems, been spread a bit further afield. By 2012, for instance, over half of all India's luxury car dealerships were located outside the country's five biggest cities. <sup>50</sup>An article in *Jetwings* inflight magazine which referred to this report itself comprised a kind of "info-ad" for luxury branding and its role in the economic and social development of Indian society. It raised the interesting questions of (1) whether Indian contemporary visual art is also a kind of luxury brand, or could be; and (2) whether the Kochi Biennale organizers would do better or worse if they characterized their own product and its packaging as such.

The equivocating, "pluralist" rhetoric displayed in this article—aimed at airborne managers of luxury brand designs as much as at their consumers—sounds rather similar to the Biennale line. "Luxury in India," it intoned:

will always celebrate the core values of Indian heritage [...] The challenge for international luxury brands is to create a fine balance that integrates global brand values with Indian meaning. The strategy to incorporate Indianness across all relevant touch points is key to ensuring brand relevance [...] International brands should view the need to instill Indianness as an opportunity to resonate with the Indian sentiment [beyond mere] transactional relationships [...] Montblanc came under intense pressure when it launched a pen containing images of Mahatma Ghandi that was seen as bad taste.<sup>51</sup>

A vision of the apparently incompatible bedfellows now sharing the global contemporary art world duvet floats once more into consciousness. Its constituent groupings, as noted earlier, are divided amongst, sometimes fighting over, the biennial's triple, overlapping social functions. These may be characterized roughly as (1) the marginal-dissident art/criticism role; (2) the "cultural tourism"/regeneration role; and (3) the investment-for-"High Net Worth"-individuals and corporate organizations role. Professional artists in India, of course, as in all countries (those, that is, that are able to make a full living purely from selling works) comprise an infinitesimally tiny proportion of the working population. The private KNMA, along with the "not-for-profit" Devi Art Foundation, also situated close to Delhi, have been created by extremely wealthy individuals following their personal tastes and social-philanthropic hobby horses. They have invented quasi-public missions for these new visual arts organizations, while the resources provided by the state for cultural

funding projects in India declined after the Crash in 2008, although the recession was not as deep as in Europe and the United States.<sup>52</sup>

The Devi Foundation's international historical and contemporary collection includes regional folk and tribal artefacts—thus representing something of the productivity of India's mass of craft producers, past and present. Delhi-based designer and critic Mayank Mansingh Kaul has observed that historical knowledge of the work and lives of the country's artisan masses is indispensable to an adequate understanding of Indian senses of the terms "ancient," "traditional." "modern" and "contemporary"—in the contexts of art (kalo), design and craft (karigari—"workmanship") and in their complex interrelations. It was in the 1980s in Jawaja, in the province of Rajasthan, Mansingh notes, that an "entire community of leather scavengers [were] transformed into skilled craftspeople, giving India its first iconic bag" (the Jawaia bag). This was a real advance upon the simple mass imitation of imported product designs, a typical practice across many Asian countries in earlier decades after the Second World War.

India's Independence and postcolonial development had been bound up with upheaval at all levels, including the cultural. This "kind of destruction and renewal after 1947 belonged to a longer history of European colonization and social transformation," Mansingh explained:

M.K. Ghandhi's struggle for political freedom from colonial rule was linked to the dissolution of feudal divisions in Indian society itself. And even while India's first Prime Minister Jawaharlal Nehru's ideas of development may seem directly opposed [...] they cannot be divorced from those of Ghandhi's, which influenced state policy until much later. This led to institutions like the National Institute of Design ultimately performing towards rural upliftment, and state patronage for craft and design continuing til the 1980s.<sup>53</sup>

The eruption of neoliberal globalizing forces within India in the early 1990s brought back, for Mansingh, the same fundamental questions about societal identity. What does it mean to be "Indian"? What, if anything, is "Indian art and design"? If received senses and embodiments of Indian nationality were in retreat before the forces of globalization, then what interests or affiliations might replace them—resurgent or new regionalisms, alternate globalisms, ultra-nationalisms, ethnic and religious fundamentalisms? In many respects, the artists who took part in the first Kochi Biennale were also asking these questions and offering some kinds of answers. Equally, the publicity materials generated by the organizers sometimes dealt seriously with the same questions and attempted to juggle a range of what they believed or hoped were not irreconcilable answers.

Business entrepreneurship, export/import agencies, new technologies, digital communications and the internet have all become watchwords of global neoliberalism's development framework. They are also key elements now in the familiar mantra of state organizations in India and everywhere else (e.g., see my account of ARCO's role in Spain in Chapter 2) set with the task of "facilitating" these forces for supposed common economic and social good. One of Mansingh's anxieties concerns the ability of Indian designers and craftspeople to forge a truly successful global brand, and he asks what agencies exist in the country to support such a development. Will India remain simply a base for the manufacture of goods of all kinds conceived and branded elsewhere? As an inspiring Indian model of the creative and antiimperialist synthesis of "East" and "West." Mansingh cites Gyanonandini Tagore's traditional dress fashioned in the moments after she was initially refused entry to a British social club in Bombay before Independence in 1947. Indian clothes were not allowed. So Tagore "tweaked it by pushing the pallu [the fold of additional material then worn at the front] at the back, simulating a western dress, and that's how The English Sari came to be!" It's also how it's often worn in India today.<sup>54</sup>

The moral of this story is that political resistance and innovative design may go hand in hand—that, in fact, they might actually sometimes require each other. Knowledge of the brutal social conditions of much design industry in contemporary urban India, however, can help to sabotage the optimistic "high theory" produced by globally influential art critics and curators of the likes of Enwesor and Mosquera. Consider Dharavi, for instance, a coastal area of 1.7 kilometers in Mumbai where over 5000 types of so-called "informal" design production take place. This is a massive manufacturing center, in some accounts the most densely populated place on Earth and commonly referred to as "Asia's biggest slum." Everything appears to be made there, from *papads* (crisp breads) to high-end leather goods. Dharavi, called by one critic a "purgatory of consumerism," distils the rigid social division of labor that characterizes craft and design cycles of production and consumption in India. The actual makers are always the poor, the designers are middle class and the consumers the elite.

Much of the work done in Dharavi encapsulates the squalor of the global economy and its division of labor. According to graphic designer Ishan Khosla, the "brunt of this is borne by the workers of Plot 13, who, in reincarnating "our" junk into diced, reusable plastic pellets, which are then China-bound—unknowingly—breathe in noxious, cancer-causing fumes. The environmental damage is also palpable: the Koli fishermen don't fish near Dharavi since all the fish are poisoned." Nehru had introduced modern design education into India in the early 1960s—an innovative decision for the whole world then—in an attempt to rejuvenate small and cottage industries after Independence, and "rescue India from the shambles of colonialism." Nehru commissioned a report by the US designers Charles and Ray Eames that articulated a vision for design based on developing indigenous Indian practices, not on the Western model of

industrialization that presumed an historical development (originating in Enlightenment modernization) that had never occurred in that manner in the colonized countries.<sup>56</sup>

Arguments proposing the social value of contemporary art in India that the Kochi Biennale organizers couched mostly in terms of projected economic and social betterment for the Kerala region might have hoped to match the tangible success of the educational card games mobilized in the 2006 Campaign To End Violence Against Women. This South Asian anti-violence coalition of groups designed a series of graphic card packs, aimed at illiterate and uneducated men and women, which were later used in 15 countries, along with other media tools including games, models, films and audio tools. The cards were pictorial and interactive, intended to develop knowledge and embed the rights of women and girls to personal freedom and security. Other card sets were developed to inform poor people about family planning provision and to help illiterate urban street children—the shoe shine boys, *coolies* (porters) and rag pickers. One of the tools was a set of 36 brightly colored picture cards showing a series of moments, emotions, choices and milestones in the life of the children. They were used to discuss "actions and their consequences, social problems relevant to the children's lives."

Once created, these cards could be used effectively by the populous ranks of so-called "barefoot facilitators" and "peer educators" rather than relying on a sparse cadre of literate specialists to conduct education and information activities with the rural and urban poor of India. 57 Natasha De Betak's photographs for her project Nightshade, exhibited at the Experimenter gallery in Kolkata in 2012, show some of the urban poor sleeping, in haunting—perhaps even rather aestheticized—images that reveal vet another kind of border. Two Nightshade (from the series) photographs show in close up, for instance, the faces of children, seemingly in a state of peace that might be that of death as much as deep sleep. As the text in the book of these photographs put it, this time is "the thin line between death and dream [...] when the city sleeps, in a state of trance."58 Biennials have nearly always been staged in or identified with a particular city. carrying the implication that the event is, in some meaningful way, specifically and irreducibly "of" that place. (The roving European "Manifesta" event staged every five years was set up in order to challenge the various complacencies thought to be latent in this convention.) Kochi Biennale's arguments for its "cultural tourism" role also relied on acceptance of the necessity of this rootedness. The names of its Board of Trustees confirm the prevalence of government and commercial stakeholders based in the city and contiguous region.<sup>59</sup>

Enwesor has claimed that it is the "global city," with its social, political and cultural "diasporic public sphere," that has inspired the creativity and challenge of the so-called "periphery" biennials. The dualisms (more borders here) and dialectics of globalization compact within the spatial and human density of such cities—"center and periphery," "local and global," "national and regional,"

"enlightened and backward," "mobility and stasis," "citizens and subjects," "the cosmopolitan and the provincial." Enwesor's rhetoric is high-flown. The "diasporic public sphere," he announces

becomes the space where the problems of translation for culture arises. Avid for the critical incarnation of new forms of experimental production, cultural translation in the global present confronts us with a way to begin again, whereby the past is neither a foreign country nor simply the authentic name of origin [...] the recent phenomenon of biennials in the periphery should not be bemoaned as a readymade example of the biennial syndrome [...] there is a need to see in the biennial phenomenon the possibility of a paradigm shift in which we as spectators are able to encounter many experimental cultures without wholly possessing them <sup>160</sup>

While Mumbai and Delhi might both qualify as examples of such a "global city," the Kochi Biennale organizers wanted to project the admittedly geographically distant regional capital of Kerala as a past, and/or incipient, one. The first edition's dual themes of cosmopolitanism and inclusion and its intent to build a "sustainable platform for contemporary art in India" would be the grounds for this. <sup>61</sup>

Ever hedging their bets, the organizers of the Kochi exhibition claimed that its inauguration could also be seen as a practical, almost ad hoc, device offering what they called "a natural gateway for a biennale [...] to get into the Indian subcontinent," especially now that some contemporary Indian artists "have of late wowed international pundits." This assertion begged the question of the fate of what had initially been called the "Triennial of Contemporary World Art," established in 1968 by the Indian National Academy of Art. The Triennial. a Delhi-based, central state-funded operation from the pre-globalization and neoliberal era, had, nevertheless, also been initiated in order to address, though in markedly different (perhaps even opposed) ways, the "peripheral" status of the subcontinent and Asia as a whole within a world dominated by art, artists. markets and art institutions in the West. Dating from its third edition held in 1978 the Triennial's organizers had taken the fateful decision only to invite the participation of artists from countries that had signed "cultural agreements" with the Indian government. This resolution publicly politicized the selection process for the Triennial and terminally sabotaged the event's international artistic credibility with most Western critics and curators. The underlying principle (and shibboleth) was, and remains, that the realm of aesthetic experience and value is held to be in crucial respects autonomous from the social order as a whole, and that exhibitions of all kinds, if they are to be taken seriously by the global art world dominated by Western interests, must always respect this.<sup>63</sup>

Contemporaneity, however, was perhaps as much of an issue for the Indian Triennial organizers in the last third of the twentieth century as it has become

for biennials and art fairs in the twenty-first. The difference, or one of the differences, was that the socialist states and Non-Aligned movements in Asia, Africa and Latin America then were significant forces both within, and, to a degree, against the two superpower Cold War blocs situated to the north. Mulk Raj Anand, chair of the Indian National Academy had noted at the time of the inaugural Triennial:

Many Asian, African, and socialist countries have not been able to establish a platform where the desired images of the oldest and youngest continents (youngest in the sense of secular achievement in the arts) may be seen together with the achievement of the dynamic west."64

As the Triennial organized its exhibitions on the basis of national "delegations," it was inevitable that the widely perceived anachronistic conservatism of this method would eventually lead to or hasten the demise of its already limited credibility—in Western countries and elsewhere, especially as neoliberalism became increasingly powerful in the 1990s, after the end of the Soviet Union. Though the Triennial had only followed historical practice at the longest and then most prestigious established biennales in grouping artists in terms of their putative national belonging—that is, those exhibitions at Venice, Pittsburg and São Paolo—the changes within "post-national" means of selection and presentation of contemporary art at biennials around the world had begun.65

These issues of the origins and representativeness of contemporaneity throughout the whole world, in terms of what is identified and exhibited as "contemporary art" in all biennials—necessarily a quantitative as well as a qualitative matter—remains prominent, and has become, if anything, even more of an urgent historical, critical and philosophical concern. 66 Probing examination of this problem could really test the too easily repeated use of the term "inclusivity" by Kochi Biennale organizers in their promotional material for the event's first edition. The seminar topics discussed at the exhibition's opening symposium could all be construed to have posed acutely this question and the matter of its economic, social, cultural, political and intellectual ramifications: (1) "The Biennale in the Indian Context: Perceptions of Change and Realities of Exposition"; (2) "Artist as Social Commentator: Generating Discourse"; (3) "Knocking Down Roadblocks"; (4) "Biennale as Imaginary: An Artist Prepares"; and (5) "Biennale as a Cultural Exercise." This latter discussion posed the uber-question of "inclusion"—what was or might be the place of Asian and African art and exhibitions within the "Euro-American discourse"?<sup>67</sup> The Kochi Biennale's stated commitment to a radical political questioning of the meanings of "inclusion" was perhaps meant to be symbolized by the inclusion of a work by Netherlands "relational-aesthetic"/"interventionist" artist Jonas Stahl, called the "New World Summit."

This piece consisted of an installation of 44 billboards depicting the flags of banned political organizations: 23 within India and 21 in other countries. These were all ideologically left wing, or claimed to be, which in itself indicated something of the limits of Stahl's own rhetoric. It had been his intention to invite people from five of these organizations to a planned Biennale discussion. His work was designed, he told *The Hindu* newspaper, to "expose the limits of democracy" and to work towards something he called "a limitless democracy." 68 The leaden weight of antagonistic interests (if not contradictions) is easy to see and in one sense afflicted the Kochi Biennale as much as Stahl's installation. The term "progressive," for both, was required to do far too much rhetorical work. Nevertheless it could be argued that, despite the nuts and bolts of developing "cultural tourism" in Kerala and garnering support from global capitalist corporations to extend the economy of the city, the organizers themselves held. at least in spirit, to Stahl's utopian definition of the potential of contemporary art and the biennial form

This constituted a model, a veritable contemporary Tatlin's Tower, of wishful thinking. According to Stahl

The imagination of alternative political spheres starts in the field of progressive art. Not in that of the mass media, which is one of the worst architects of the monopolies of information and representation that we need to deal with [...] Art has evolved, wriggling its way out of the clutches of authority, through individual mediums and bold blending of them, and contemporary art is in an exciting phase.<sup>69</sup>

Has contemporary art, and one of its paradigmatic expository forms, the biennial exhibition, really escaped from the control of the state and the bourgeoisie, as Stahl goes on to claim? (Enwesor seems to share at least some of this optimism): "The international democratisation movement is in need of tools to help shape the horizon of a fundamental democracy, and that's what's next."70 The history and form of the Kochi Biennale's first edition in 2012 suggests not. What follows here is an account of how the "state and the bourgeoisie" are faring in the development of contemporary art and its higher education institutions in one of India's neighboring states—mainland China, the PRC, forty years on since the death of Chairman Mao and "the great leap forward" taken by his successors into the global capitalist neoliberal world order/disorder.
