4

# Social Reproduction of Contemporary Art in the People's Republic of China: Higher Education and the Branding of "Contemporary Chinese Art"

As the plane taxis along the runway in to the terminal at Beijing Capital Airport I stare uncomprehendingly at my diary, trying to force myself into morning's usual wakefulness. It's 8.30 a.m. here but 12.30 a.m. last night back in England... Asia has got ahead of me, again... A note in my diary says "lecture on globalization and contemporary art" at Tsinghua *University*—12 noon today. But surely it is tomorrow? No. It is today ... And tomorrow we push on to Dalian Polytechnic University, five hundred miles to the east, where my university operates a joint campus for art and design students—another lecture to do there, and a faculty meeting to review the development of our programs in fashion and design. English language ability is a concern—always an issue for Chinese students in England and in China too, now, as our 400 students (planned to double in two years) are taught and assessed only in English. "Transnational" higher education has become a big business for UK universities and several now operate campuses in the PRC. How does this education translate? ... I remember my lecture, today, in fact in only 3 hours or so, or 4 a.m. British time—globalization's "time-space compression," they call this... The looming terminal building blots out the light from my little iced-up window on the world: "Welcome to China. HSBC, The Global Bank," say the signs...

#### Art and Design Education as a Globalization Process

Higher education provision for art and design in China has expanded enormously since the early 1960s when 12 art schools were originally designated as the basis for national provision in the PRC. The country's first journal in the area, *Art and Design Magazine*, had been founded in 1958. One of the first 12 schools, the Central Academy of Arts and Design (also founded that year, in Beijing) began to enroll students on four-year undergraduate degree programs in 1977, just after Chairman Mao's death and, with it, the end of the Cultural Revolution (during which college entrance examinations had been banned).

The Central Academy of Arts and Design should not be confused with the Central Academy of Fine Arts (CAFA, also in Beijing), one of the most prestigious specialist universities in the PRC. CAFA was founded in 1950 and its curriculum and research areas include what Western universities usually call the "arts and humanities"—that is, literature, music, historical and cultural studies, along with visual art and design.<sup>1</sup>

In 1999 the Central Academy of Arts and Design merged with Tsinghua University, becoming its Academy of Arts and Design. Important art and design program developments had occurred at the Central Academy of Arts and Design between 1977 and the merger 22 years later. These reflected and embodied national change as the PRC began to open up to the world and the academy assimilated fields of teaching and research already common in Western universities. In 1980 the Central Academy of Arts and Design set up its Design Centre (which merged with Environmental Art Studies five years later). In 1982 it established the PRC's first fashion design undergraduate program. The following year it created its history of arts and crafts department and began to award PhD degrees. In 1984 it inaugurated its industrial design and fashion departments and began to organize partnerships with overseas universities. And, after joining with Tsinghua University, the Academy of Arts and Design began to run MFA (Master of Fine Arts) programs in 2005 and award doctorates the following year.<sup>2</sup>

Early in the twenty-first century the PRC's central government, that of the Chinese Communist Party (CCP), in what is effectively a one-party state, began to instigate developments radically to improve the quality of research and post-graduate training in selected universities—including at Tsinghua in Beijing. In doing this, the government emulated plans carried out in national higher education systems around the world, as it became clear that the forces of globalization were fundamentally changing and challenging national economies, and transforming the character of knowledge production in all areas. By 2000, within a global capitalist neoliberalizing economy, apparently ceaseless technological innovation—particularly in digital, media, internet, bioscientific and material sciences areas—presented nation-states with opportunities for huge new wealth creation and societal development.

At the same time, however, this transforming change threatened the quality of life of their peoples: nation-states were obliged not to fail to interact and compete in this new world order/disorder of production, trade and consumption. The PRC, like Russia in 1991 after the end of the Soviet Union (though there are very significant differences), substantially—but never conclusively—turned its back on the Maoist-era "command economy." China joined the key global capitalist network institutions, the International Monetary Fund and World Bank, in 1980 and started extensive privatization programs within some sectors of the country's economy. In 2001 the PRC negotiated entry to the World Trade Organization. China also began to encourage unprecedented

social, cultural and intellectual contact with Western-though also African and Latin American—countries (China, as I have noted, was one of the founding so-called BRIC nations).4

The ostensible material contradictions of and ideological conflicts between a commitment to "free enterprise" on the one hand and a political state still nominally faithful to a Communist path on the other pervade the official, institutional discourse of China's art and design higher education and research, as in other academic areas. Tsinghua University's department of art history required. for instance, according to a 2011 prospectus, that its students should "build systematic theoretical knowledge of history of Chinese and foreign art and that of Chinese and foreign arts and design [...] with basic theory of Marxism-Leninism as guideline." What this might actually mean (and require in terms of the assessment of essays) was not elaborated. However, a statement in a document produced by another PRC university makes the Tsinghua statement more intelligible. A postgraduate studies and research bulletin entitled *Brands*, published by the "Brands Research and Development Institute" at Dalian Polytechnic University, informs the reader that all teaching and research takes within, and as part of, the Chinese "national economy and social development 12th 5-year plan." This plan is continuous with, that is, the central planning process instituted in China in 1950 just after the revolution inspired by Marxism-Leninism which brought the Communist Party and Chairman Mao to power.<sup>6</sup>

In 2008, however, when the Brands Research and Development Institute was created, led by academic Jingyang Lin, the PRC's national plan included the aim further to privatize certain "soft" areas of the country's economy. The Brands bulletin recognized the significance of the powerful commercial forces at work once such a process got under way. The language here replicates that of Western companies. The onus was placed on what linguang Lin called "brand innovation capability" and the consolidation of "brand asset" following the Crash that year and resulting slump. The rhetoric of "market absolutism" here ironically mirrors the PRC's previous commitment to doctrinal "Marxism-Leninism." For instance, "the market," according to the *Brands* statement, "is always right"—though what the market actually is isn't defined at all. Further, lingvang Lin declares that "the market is the foundation of success" and that constant innovation of new and successful brands is necessary "because the market is cruel." The very broadest definition of the field of design—now including many areas of "services" and "experience-generating products," as well as material goods of all kinds—has facilitated a vast growth area for higher education and research throughout the world. Dalian Polytechnic University's Brands Research and Development Institute carried out work concerned with, for example, cosmetics, building materials and what are called "environmental systems."8

The Institute is part of the School of Art and Design at Dalian Polytechnic University, and perusal of its interests and language suggest that a self-conscious

and systematic "branding" of student artists' and designers' identities and roles has also taken place. This development has occurred in China and throughout the world as the labor of professional artists and designers has become represented as an elite authored/bespoke product made for a niche or luxury market. I showed in Chapter 3 how the organizers of the 2012 Kochi Biennale worked, in effect, to brand their first exhibition and the artists it featured as a particular kind of regional and national collective product. Artworks and curation programs are increasingly presented—by artists and curators or by their dealers and agents—as expert forms of "service" and "experience" product delivered to consumers, especially, for example, in the art fair environment such as at Art Basel Hong Kong. This emphasis on the meaning, purpose and value of contemporary art production and consumption is now solidly embodied in the social forms of the contemporary art biennial and art fair "event." It has also become central to the "relational-aesthetic" dimension of participatory art practices, regarded by many critics and commentators, along with installation art, as quintessential globalized art forms.<sup>9</sup>

Glossy catalogues of work produced by graduate students at Tsinghua University in the areas of ceramics, visual communication, industrial, environmental and information design, as well as in painting and sculpture, indicate efforts to brand students' identities as future professionals. The pages in these catalogues set aside for each student include some English language text and make recurrent reference to Western art, artists and wider cultural figures. Many Chinese students adopt what appear to be English names/identities, but because of the arrangement of page layouts these tend to become conflated dreamily with titles for the illustrations of their design work. Such hybrid author/product brand-names include, for instance, "Soul Taker," "Diffuse Nicotine," "Curve," "Falling Stone," "Hitchcock's Girl," "Call from the Last Day," "The Metamorphosis," and "Seeking Mondrian." These ambiguously meaningful textual elements raise a series of important questions regarding the use of English by Chinese students. These centrally concern the status of the language within the curriculum of higher education art and design programs in the PRC and elsewhere in Asia where English has become a central pedagogic medium. 10

Beyond that issue, however, these questions highlight the much broader one of the status of Western socioeconomic power and cultural influence in "global Asia" and especially in the world of Asian contemporary art. My own involvement with these issues has had a strong institutional element to it. For several years it was connected directly to the matter of the quality of learning experiences facilitated by my then university in educating our Chinese students based both in England and at the shared campus in Dalian. Chinese government openness to foreign education systems has taken a number of forms since the beginnings of the PRC's "Open Door" policy in the 1980s. Allowing Chinese students to travel abroad and study at universities in Europe and North America was a big initial step which essentially—at first anyway—required these

students, if they were to succeed institutionally, to assume as competently as they could the behavior patterns, standards and values of the host institutions, their staff and students. A great deal of accumulated evidence indicates that this was (and remains) a "hit and miss" experience, for all parties involved. 11

Two issues are particularly significant within discussion of this experience of transnational education. These are (1) its role in what's called "independent learning" and (2) its technical or practical uses in specific curriculum areas. Both raise questions about the Western character of globalization processes in general and the specific Asian (including Chinese) experience of decolonization in the twentieth century, along with the continuing legacies of colonialism. Insofar as the vast majority of now practicing professional artists and designers have been through a university education process, and given that since the 1990s many from Asia have travelled to Western countries for this education's postgraduate or research element, the status of English as the world's lingua frança is a critical concern for this book as a whole. Within a formal education process—one of the key mechanisms for social reproduction in all modern societies—the use of English is inevitably tied to specific forms and processes of understanding, specific senses of self and of others, specific conventions of naming and classification, and a deep historical legacy of specific cultural references, priorities, emphases and exclusions. To "learn" all that as what is sometimes breezily called a "second language" in a formal education situation over a three- or four-year period would be a very tall order for the most scholarly, open and able students. To gain a sufficient "working knowledge" of it simply in order to pass assessments remains a challenge for nearly everyone. 12

The PRC's decision to allow some Western universities to operate campuses and programs in China pushed the globalization of higher education an important stage further. On the one hand, this development occurred through an initial (though necessarily continuing) bilateral negotiation between respective governments and universities about many aspects of institutional education processes. This was necessary because these campuses in China, such as the Winchester School of Art-University of Southampton programs run with Dalian Polytechnic University, had to "bed down" with the host organization in terms of teaching faculty and resources, student recruitment and assessment processes, along with the whole gamut of the PRC's universities' regulatory regime. One condition for this to be possible had to be the existence of mutual goodwill and willingness on both sides to look at their own systems and that of their partners and agree to be flexible. The novel program and campus created in Dalian (out of renovated buildings) was a genuine hybrid, drawing on elements from both countries' systems but characteristic of neither's usual higher education institutional arrangement.<sup>13</sup>

In the case of Dalian, one of the senior academic managers was a professional artist and architectural designer who decided to allow an experiment. This was that the art and design program run with Winchester School of Art would substantially reproduce the same degree programs as those conceived, validated, operated and assessed in England. This meant that all the teaching, learning, assessment and "quality assurance" processes had to be conducted in English, although they also had to meet the specifications of Dalian Polytechnic University's own regulatory framework. The Chinese students recruited to the programs would spend half of their first two years on the degree program studying English, after having passed initial entry-level tests. The challenge, for students and staff, became that of using English language learning as an active tool in the students' education, rather than it being imposed as a necessary formality of study, as it is for Chinese students registered in UK-based universities. Was English, taught as the students' education language and medium of communication—as, that is, a resource of forms and processes of understanding, with specific senses of self and of others, specific conventions of naming and classification, and a deep historical legacy of specific references, priorities, emphases and exclusions—likely, then, to be in continuity or contradiction with the Chinese students' first language?<sup>14</sup>

#### Changing Identifications in Art and **Design Education in China**

An important experiential aspect to this complex of issues, especially in relation to contemporary art discourse where particularly heightened assumptions and senses of subjectivity remain pivotal, concerns the differential character and significance of states of individuality and interiority within Western and Chinese cultures. Insofar as languages are commonly held to "reflect" the forms of behavior and norms of their native speakers, it is the case that Chinese Communist-era values created, reproduced and emphasized powerful senses of collectivist, in distinction from, individualist identities. It is also well to remember, though, that Chinese Communism built on earlier, complementary societal traditions and social norms, for instance, those of Confucianism. 15 Prevalence of "ists" and "isms" indicates the active propagation of ideological belief systems in the examples of both Chinese communism and Western individualism.

By noting this I mean to suggest that one cannot separate the experience, and understanding, of what it is to be a person—literally a single individual—from the experience and knowledge of the ways in which a specific society and its practices may work to reinforce particular ways of seeing and experiencing oneself both as an individual and as a member of a group or whole society. Simplistic assertions of the existence of a border or dualism, for example, between being "English" (or "Western") and "Chinese" (or "Eastern") should be avoided. In both cultures—a phrase that already presupposes, of course, some kinds of actual separation and difference, if not a border—there have been

traditions and experiences of being both distinctly individual and distinctly part of a group, and the whole society. These forms of identity and identification have been, for instance, tribal, ethnic, caste, class, gender, regional, religious, civic and politico-ideological in nature, as well as relatively mainstream or marginal at different historical times. But statist discourses and doctrines of individualism and collectivism gathered pace rapidly in the mid-twentieth century and by the late 1950s a dualistic opposition characterized the Cold War ideological standoff between the putative "democratic" (US capitalist) and "egalitarian" (Soviet/Sino communist) world powers. As these terms with their parenthetical referents should indicate, there remains no neutral, non-contested or uniform way to describe these positions and worldviews, or to account for how their proponents characterized their own, and their Cold War opponents, identities and identifications. <sup>16</sup>

In the visual arts this moment in the Cold War coincided closely with what is usually still portrayed as the apogee (for ideological good or bad) of "capitalist individualism" in the West. This was embodied in the "high modernist" works and adduced philosophical perspectives of the American Abstract Expressionist painters. It is worth noting, however, that several of these most celebrated artists—particularly, holding the anarchist views that they did, Mark Rothko and Barnett Newman—were actually as hostile to the US capitalist political—economic system as they were to the authoritarian Eastern communist states. This detail, however, was generally downplayed or omitted altogether in the way their paintings were exhibited and celebrated by major US and European museums at the time, during and because of, the Cold War itself.<sup>17</sup>

The "Open Door" policy and "modernizing" reforms of Mao's successor, Deng Xiaoping, indicated the beginnings of a significant loosening of the key ideological positions which had underpinned communism in the PRC—especially during the Cultural Revolution, against which the CCP turned decisively on Mao's death. This development certainly did not, ever, constitute anything like an official embrace of Western ideological individualism. But one of the consequences of the reform process that began then to extend privatization and encourage business entrepreneurship was the onset of a social transformation in people's experience and experiential knowledge of self and senses of interiority, away from the previous stronghold of Chinese Communist collectivist values. Deng Xiaoping's reforms certainly were meant to embody and represent a profound official reaction against the newly acknowledged horrors and egregious errors of the Cultural Revolution. During the 1980s, for instance, a key Chinese government slogan was "practice is the sole criterion of testing truth." Practice here was code for the extension of "free market" activities and implied a positive evaluative contrast with the barren "theory" of discredited Maoism.18

In this broader historical sense, then, Dalian Polytechnic University's decision to embrace Winchester School of Art's art and design program implied a

preparedness to negotiate the entailed symbolic and sociocultural weight of "English" (and "Western"). For this was a language, culture and social order in which increasingly strong senses of individual, individuality, individuation, as well as individualism, had begun to develop from the late sixteenth century onwards. This was when these novel terms and nuances of meaning began to come into English from Renaissance and Enlightenment Italian, German and French, and from writers on art specifically. Studying art and design "in English" meant, then, that Chinese students would be exposed—intentionally or not—to a set of historically accrued societal values embedded in the language that stressed quite different and sometimes opposing senses of self, of others as also individual and individuated in specific ways, and beyond that, of the social and societal worlds beyond. Notions of artistic "creativity," "invention," and "authorship" would also be closely bound up with and emblematic of these different and sometimes disputed senses of self and individuality.

As the history of the PRC from the latter third of the twentieth century indicates, since the death of Mao and inauguration of the "Open Door" policy China's ruling groups have taken steps themselves that have helped lead to the moment when the country's people (especially the young, born after Mao's death) had begun to experience their individuality and sense of self and others in ways that were becoming closer to that of Westerners. The range of resonant vet ambiguous brand/names adopted by Chinese design students at Tsinghua University—"Soul Taker," "Diffuse Nicotine," "Curve," "Falling Stone," "Hitchcock's Girl," "Call from the Last Day," "The Metamorphosis," and "Seeking Mondrian"—indicates a familiarity with the density if also postmodern eclecticism of contemporary Western and globalizing social, cultural and artistic life and mores. Two obvious objections might be raised against these assertions. Firstly, that this Chinese experience of the West and of Western-ness is in large part a mediated, vicarious one. Secondly, that many of these brand/names are simply facile, insignificant references to Western media forms and figures. But these observations actually underline how Western identity and subjectivity itself has been, and will increasingly be, reshaped within the same now globalizing mediating-technological processes. "Western" and "Eastern" are states, experiences and means of knowing in a dialectical process of mutual transformation.

How, for example, is the acquisition and deployment of a second language linked to behavior in social situations, and specifically to that of students studying art and design in a university? These questions lead to a second issue concerning use of English as the language of learning and assessment at Dalian Polytechnic University. It had become clear early on in the history of the shared program that Chinese students, in comparison with their British counterparts, were used to a different kind of education experience. This was one based largely on so-called "rote learning" in lecture rooms: a one-way process or transmission focused on a teacher whose assumed expertise and authority was

at the center of the institutional definition and reproduction of what counted as significant knowledge. That mode, however, had started to become less significant in British higher education by the 1980s. In particular, art students in Western education institutions, often with the support of some of their teachers, had rebelled against such methods, which had been seen as intrinsically authoritarian and reactionary (and boring) since the late-1960s.<sup>20</sup>

New, "student-centered learning" and "independent learning" forms began to be implemented, along with focus on developing for individual students a research process suited to their different needs. Within theories of studentcentered learning individuals would find specific answers to problems and ways to complete tasks that they themselves asked and set, though still within a largely predefined program, curriculum and assessment format. For instance, the introduction of a "sketchbook diary" for first year students' daily use at Dalian was encouraged in order to engender a valuable habit of systematic selfscrutiny and self-development long used in Western art schools but new to China. Transnational education, "contrastive rhetoric" studies and translation studies—three emergent subgenres within cultural globalization studies assess teaching and learning modes in different institutional contexts worldwide. The topic's significance indicates the various investments at stake in what is now a multi-billion dollar global higher education business.<sup>21</sup> Millions of students, and tens of thousands of academic staff, relocate around the world regularly now as part of what might be called the global "knowledge acquisition, transfer and exchange economy." Its social, cultural and political implications are vast, as this discussion of art and design higher education in the PRC indicates. The transformations in contemporary Chinese society and culture, as its economy, people and institutions become increasingly interconnected with economies, people and institutions (including universities) around the world have been profound, with wide ramifications within the global contemporary art world.<sup>22</sup>

Higher education and research in the design fields in China (and in India as we saw in the previous chapter) is similarly in a process of globalization. Again, there is evidence to suggest that the PRC has started to move towards its own kinds of "independent learning" focus as much as it has been prepared to allow some of its students to experience Western versions—those both offered overseas and at campuses set up with partner institutions in the PRC. The establishment of independent research protocols are discernible, for example, in the organization of the Brands Research and Development Institute at Dalian Polytechnic University. The Institute, for instance, claimed that it "strove to enable students to have a sense of social responsibility with 'confidence and ability to challenge authority"—this as part of a project linked to raising environmental awareness of pollution risks in building materials.<sup>23</sup> The authority of the teacher was, it seems, being partially decentered: "the traditional teaching model was changing. Professors and students formed a center of teaching

together, professor as a director or guider, students as performers." This model had what was called an "experimental" performative element to it. Think of the terms "director" and "performer" here perhaps as developmental roles in a dynamic scenario rather than as part of a necessarily fixed and final order of things. This "teaching system" was developed in partnership with some institutions outside of the PRC—in this case, with Nanyang Technological University in Singapore.<sup>24</sup>

Maintenance of the contemporary art system in globalized Asia requires the production and reproduction of a professionalized workforce, at the apex of which stand the artists. As in the west, the figure of the artist remains an active and potent symbol of creativity and individuality. The global art and design higher education process helps now to fashion the identity or "branding" of proto-artists—a very limited number of whom will eventually make works for the market, sold by dealers and at art fairs like Art Basel Hong Kong. Some of these artists will get a chance to display their work at biennial exhibitions like that at Kochi and some of those perhaps will have their works bought by "global gatekeeper" institutions like Tate or the Guggenheim Museum. The catalogues produced in order to showcase Tsinghua University art and design graduates exemplify the wider significance of social individuation processes in China. PRC students, such as those enrolled at Dalian Polytechnic University on the Winchester School of Art program, have gone through a kind of "knowledge internationalization" process influenced by an English language-based inculcation into Western art and design practices, involving induction into facets of related occidental culture, history and philosophy.

#### **Contemporary Chinese Art Marketed** for Global Consumption

These higher education innovations are active productive elements now within China's global contemporary art world. Critic Huang Du, based in Beijing, has written-in English and Mandarin-reflecting on the nature of the qualitative and quantitative transformations within this world since the late 1970s. 25 According to Huang Du, a pervading ideology of rank "commercialism" came to replace that of "political propaganda art" in China after the death of Mao, and its morbid symptoms dominated contemporary art in the PRC into the beginnings of the twenty-first century. This "commercialization," he observed, had:

crushed the "Utopian" [values] of the avant-garde artists in the past. Everything is commercialized and consumed in the name of art. In this way, art has become a "stock market" and "Vanity Fair." The impact of commercialization of art is reflected in the liberation of human beings [...] the confrontation between the previous avant-garde art and political ideology has been replaced by the alliance of contemporary art and commerce.  $^{26}$ 

This paradigm transformation in the period up to 2000 was marked by what Huang Du calls a shift from "macro narration"—exemplified by the epic propaganda painting stories of the Communist era—to a form of "micro personalization," in works that represented the apparently randomized daily experience and life of artists in post-Mao PRC. The commodification process that began to incorporate Chinese contemporary art into a global art market—a development I turn to in a moment, with discussion of specific artists and artworks—was encoded in a parallel shift from what Huang Du calls "individual creation" to "mass production, from single work to mass products." Art's earlier sacred "halo" under Communism was replaced, he notes, by a profane "daily, popular, graphic visual language."

Huang Du believes, however, that after 2000 this overriding ethos of commercialization began to lose its grip on the production of new artworks in China. In its place a range of varied practices and genuinely heterogeneous interests has come to characterize the field of contemporary art. He identifies within it several broad themes. The first of these is what he calls "the expansion of media and the intervention of new concepts." He cites as an example the subdued color grid paintings in acrylic of Zhang Xuerui (born 1979), whose diminutive recent works, such as 72 *Inclined Lattice* (3) (2014), resume a "cool," formal investigation of abstraction, eschewing any obvious (or commercial) symbolic or narrative content. Huang Du's second theme is "the interaction between photography and painting." For instance, consider Wang Guofeng's large-scale digital photographs of communist-era public buildings in China and Russia, and political rallies in North Korea, such as *North Korea* 2012 no.2 (2039 × 208 cm, 2012).

These images both extend yet disrupt nineteenth century history painting conventions and objectives, provoking questions about the contemporary visual culture of post-revolutionary state power, ideology and political authority in a global capitalist world (Wang Guofeng's photographs are discussed in more detail in my Conclusion).

Huang Du's third theme is "the focused lens of video." Within this category he cites China's preeminent filmmaker Yang Fudong (born 1971), whose many

![](_page_139_Picture_7.jpeg)

**Figure 10** Wang Guofeng *North Korea 2012 no. 2* (DVD video still, 2012). *Source:* © Wang Guofeng, Courtesy de Sarthe Gallery.

black and white films have combined an interest in Western cinema genre (especially film noir) with the portrayal of contemporary life for younger people in China. Yang Fudong's 2014 five-channel digital video work *The Colored Sky: New Women II* represents a new departure, experimenting with moving-image in an installation form and format. This work, alluding to surrealist narrative imagery, is a dream-like depiction of young Chinese women in a now "global Asian" world which is simultaneously both modern and ancient. Qui Zhijie's 2013 *Playground I* was a 1000 square meter interactive "floor map" of the world installed in a gallery in Hong Kong, upon which people were invited to roll actual spheres of different sizes and materials around a conceptual terrain linking China to a global network of place-names, ideas and objects. The artist included in his *Playground II* (the extension of this map on to the walls of the same gallery) 600 different brands of Chinese cigarette drawn from his own everyday world. Huang Du cites Qui Zhijie as one of the artists exemplifying the fourth theme, which he calls "the extension of Chinese heritage and daily life."

Considering the period between the late 1970s and 2013, how might we characterize the globalizing social order within which this "transfiguration" in Chinese contemporary art occurred? As the financial crash in 2008 demonstrated and the previous chapters in this book have illustrated, globalization processes involve the creation of new conditions and systems that are inherently unstable and dangerous. That term, "system," usually understood, implies an ordering process that overrides, or that at least is intended to override, the local significance or weakness of component elements within it. Analysis of the contemporary global art world suggests, however, that this "system" (part of the globalized world order/disorder) is better understood as a kind of skewed power network whose "nodes" (that is, its key pressure points) have been controlled hitherto by a limited number of key players in financial and institutional terms.<sup>29</sup>

These "gatekeeper" organizations have derived their historical and present power within the global art world system, and its subsector in global Asia, from a number of sources that are sociopolitical, ideological and economic in nature. But their effective control of the system is itself limited and vulnerable to many kinds of external pressures. These notably include (1) financial crises in the global capitalist economy as a whole; (2) destabilizing local political and socioeconomic conditions (in this case, those in the PRC); and (3) actions by some of those dissidents who might be called the "worker ants" comprising the art world system itself. By this perhaps provocative term I mean some artists, critics, curators, gallery owners and others who explicitly oppose—in the name of art itself—the function and character of contemporary art in the globalized art world system where they see it reduced to the status of simply another commodity, and spectacular diversion, within the globalized capitalist world-order.<sup>30</sup>

As Chapter 1, focused on Art Basel Hong Kong, demonstrated, over the past 20 years in particular, the market for contemporary art has become a

structurally significant, if still relatively minor, part of global corporate, state and private "wealth investment management" strategies, along with, for instance, trade in commodities such as gold and securities such as US government bonds. The art market's key sites of activity (its most significant auctions, dealing galleries and fairs) have expanded from New York, London, Paris and other North American and European centers, to global Asian venues, primarily Hong Kong, Singapore, Shanghai, Dubai, Beijing and Delhi. These have attached themselves in some cases to urban centers where pre-existing regular local commercial art fairs, galleries and auctioneers help to pump prime the markets and generate related media and public interest.

The art market's very rapid expansion globally in the years after 2000 was tied directly to the growth in the world economy in the early years of that decade. Its size, in fact, doubled between 2003 and 2006.<sup>32</sup> The subsequent rapid decline equally closely mirrored the losses and bank failures that occurred in the second half of 2008. Yet, since then, the art market has recovered quickly, in terms of volumes of sales and record individual sale prices, at a rate far more rapid than in most other parts of the world economy. This happened because art became seen as a kind of gold itself, a secure place to "park" or store capital in times of continuing uncertainty—with the assumption, or hope, that prices will not have fallen when the time comes to sell.<sup>33</sup> In this countervailing sense, the buoyant art market might be understood as a kind of "cracked" mirror of the stalled world economy as a whole: the rich or corporate investors in it see art as a sanctuary of sorts in continuing uncertain times.<sup>34</sup>

Art auctions remained illegal under Communist Party rule in China between 1956 and 1986. Chen Yifei's 1992 sumptuous "academic-style" painting of Chinese women in traditional dress The Four Graces (note the Western art title, referring to European Renaissance painting) was sold by Guardian Auctioneers in Beijing in 1997 for over US\$275 000, then a remarkable price for a contemporary Chinese painting.<sup>35</sup> The sale was a "watershed" moment, indicating significant incipient change of several kinds. It was rumored that the New York and London-based dealer Marlborough Fine Art had bought the work. But Christie's attempts to sell Chinese artworks in London the following year turned out to be a failure—only 25% of the lots went under the hammer.<sup>36</sup> Two key developments, however, were exemplified by these events. The first of these was the intervention of Western dealers into the then just-opening Chinese art world, economy and society as a whole. The second was the marketing of works by living Chinese artists that stressed Western characteristics and references. This development signaled the creation of a branding process aiming these works at potential Western buyers. While these artworks offered an ostensibly authentic "Chinese" theme or narrative, they were simultaneously done in a familiar and unchallenging Western manner.<sup>37</sup>

Yifei has been termed one of the Chinese "Romantic Realist" painters. These two key terms associated with nineteenth-century European academic painting

evoke portraiture by the likes of Ingres, Delacroix and Courbet. Other Chineseborn artists from the mid-twentieth century—such as San Yu and Xu Beihong had done well at auctions overseas but they had generally lived outside China and rarely had works sold anywhere in the country's mainland. 38 Their pictures instantiated for Western buyers "traditional" Chinese styles and subjects of painting from the history of Chinese art-especially the prominence of calligraphic works combining image and text.<sup>39</sup>

In the early twentieth century visual and stylistic affinities developed—or, more importantly, were perceived to have developed—between paintings done by these Chinese artists living abroad and the works of the European masters. such as Picasso and Matisse. As modernist art developed in Europe and then in the United States after 1945, more affinities—assumed visual similarities read as evidence of actually shared themes or values, whatever the actual intentions or knowledge of the Chinese artists—were registered. 40 These claims were generally based on the presuppositions of some Western art critics and historians that the development of abstract painting in the West had meaningful rather than superficial parallels in kinds of work that had been produced in China and other Asian countries for hundreds of years. 41

It is of course possible at one level to identify kinds of "visual similarity" in these works—between, for example, "drip paintings" made by Chinese painter Wu Guanzhong and American Jackson Pollock. But the conclusion that these similarities are evidence of actual thematic or authorial continuities in works produced sometimes many years or decades apart under very different historical and social conditions remains arguable. The matter is even more contentious and complicated because, by the mid-twentieth century, some Chinese and US artists actually were sometimes definitely self-consciously exploring each other's cultures, being, in the strict sense of the term, "contemporaries." 42 The still-typical Western critical assumption, however, that Chinese (and, in another important instance, Japanese) artists were "copying" or had been decisively "influenced" by Western artists, rather than the interrelation being dialectical, underlines the dominance and entailed arrogance of European and US art history and criticism, as well as its "gatekeeper" organizations. 43

Ten years after Yifei's painting had sold for what was then a startlingly high price for a Chinese painting, Zao-Wou Ki's 1959 picture 14.12.59 was sold by Christie's Hong Kong, in May of 2007, for US\$3.32 m-much more than its then estimated value. Zao-Wou Ki is termed in the sales literature a "second generation modernist."44 This term replicates an item in the terminology developed by US art critics in the 1950s and 1960s to describe and sequence works by American Abstract Expressionist artists, who also used dates and numbers rather than conventional titles for their paintings. In the claiming of another kind of affinity or analogy here, Chinese art made in the mid-twentieth century onwards is connected explicitly to Western modernist abstract art, and, by extension, to its critical and historical understanding through aesthetic philosophies within the European tradition derived from Immanuel Kant. 45 Zao-Wou Ki's paintings can fit neatly into this capacious and accommodating terrain of understanding—they may be "abstract" or have "abstract" qualities, but they also evoke less challenging, more familiar works by earlier European landscape painters such as Turner and Caspar David Friedrich.

This question of formal resemblance exemplifies something of the complex processes of interaction that have developed between some Chinese artists and the world (and art world) outside of the PRC. Through these processes they have come to know Western artworks, to know these as emblematic of Western Art, and to associate their own activities and values with this entity, to varying degrees. Alongside the dealers Christie's and Sotheby's, Western art museums became the most powerful "gatekeeper" players in the globalizing art world after 2000. The greatest artworks of the European and American modernist era are owned and exhibited in the Museum of Modern Art, the Metropolitan museum and the Guggenheim museum in New York, at Tate Modern in London, at the Reina Sofia museum in Madrid and in the other great collections (public and private) held in Europe and its satellite centers. The related authority and once wide influence of US critics such as the Americans Clement Greenberg and Michael Fried—both associated very closely with identifying what was thought of as the best painting and sculpture of the late 1940s, 1950s and early 1960s underpinned the museum collections of these canonical works. 46

#### Chinese Art and the World Art System

The globalized Asian art world, of which the PRC is a part, is this "systemic power network" of interlinked economic, institutional and ideological-cultural relationships and interdependencies. This global system has been founded on the economic and discursive power of Western art (historical, modern and contemporary), its host societies' legal systems, art discourses and infrastructures for the buying, selling, authentication and critical validation of artworks. China and Chinese artists have become assimilated to this system within the processes of globalization over the past 20 years. 47 The Chinese government was invited to take part in some of the key events of the annual art world calendar—for example, to first allow and support the exhibition of Chinese artists' works in a national pavilion at the Venice Biennale in 2003 (their involvement was cancelled because of the outbreaks of the disease SARS). In the same year, the first large-scale exhibition of contemporary Chinese art was held in France at the Centre Georges Pompidou in Paris, also organized in association with the Chinese government. The notional guarantee of "museum quality" is a regulatory key to the workings of the art market—it is a recurrent phrase used in the sales rhetoric of auctioneers and dealers. In this respect the museums and auction houses inflect and entrench each other's roles in the globalized art world. 48

In 2000, most market activity for modern and contemporary Chinese art remained "offshore," conducted in Hong Kong and Teipei in Taiwan. But as China began to develop its economic and social reforms, allowing private businesses to exist and grow, new markets for art on the mainland began to emerge-in Beijing, Shanghai, Hangzhou and Guangzhou, and in about six other centers. 49 Centers for art and artists emerged, too, mostly famously at "798" in Beijing, a former factory district turned mixture of galleries, cafes and bars—though this mutating entity has been more tolerated than directly encouraged or supported by the government authorities. This situation is one of the sources of local "system vulnerability" in the global power network. Since the beginnings of the "Open Door" policy, Chinese government regimes have blown hot and cold on the existence and meaning of an indigenous art world, at "798" and elsewhere. 50

Corruption scandals and political crises in the Chinese Communist Party, and in its rule in China as a whole, have had direct and indirect effects on the activities and changing status of its artists and art discourses. A continued attempted blanket censorship of sexually explicit material in art and the wider culture is one instance. 51 The treatment and status of the most famous Chinese contemporary artist in the west. Ai Weiwei, is another—though he has had a long and complicated history of connection to and disconnection from the Communist Party, reaching back to his father's activities as a well-known poet. Ai Oing had been a Cultural Revolution dissident but was then officially rehabilitated and became a celebrated figure.<sup>52</sup> As I've noted, Weiwei was named "the most influential artist in the world" in 2011, by Art Review, a US-based Western journal and online media source. In 2016 he was still in the top ten. He has become, for some powerful agents in the West, a late-Cold War symbol of resistance to communist power in China and this development marks the perennial ambivalence of US and European political interests generally over the place of China in the world economic and sociopolitical order.<sup>53</sup>

China undoubtedly has become the key structural element to continued global economic growth (as part of the BRIC zone of countries), within which the art market is a small part, but there is a potentially catastrophic asymmetry between China's economic and sociopolitical character—at least as far as the US and its NATO alliance members are concerned. 54The term "authoritarian state capitalism" may aptly name the Chinese socioeconomic system. It characterizes a nation-state ruled by a single unmovable (though not unchanging) cadre of elites that is trying to manage and dominate enormous economic forces and related, unpredictable social change more typical of the development of the capitalist-democratic societies in Europe and North America since the late nineteenth century. 55 This development, however, is by no means historically anomalous. Versions of the combination of a "free enterprise" capitalist economy with an authoritarian state occurred many times in Western Europe in the mid-twentieth century. Some of the fascist regimes that erupted

there in the interwar period, in parallel and then opposition to the Soviet Union, aspired precisely to this combination. Francisco Franco's autocratic regime in Spain was, by far, the most successful of these in melding systematic repression of all kinds with "private enterprise" and big business interests through from 1939 to the mid-1970s. This was possible because NATO, led by the US since the mid-1950s, supported Franco's regime as a bulwark against the Soviet Union, and, sometimes, against the PRC too.

Zhang Xiaogang's 1994 painting Bloodlines: Three Comrades sold for US\$2.1 million at Sotheby's in New York in 2007. It represents a form of socalled "political pop art" that developed in China, made by Chinese artists, in the 1990s. The painting, depicting the curiously inert faces of a trio of CCP members, alludes to something of the internal upheavals experienced by the population as the (self) image and significance of the Communist Party itself went through crises and transformation—especially as it tried (and tries) to legitimize itself while overseeing a limited internal capitalist revolution and beginning to act as a key player on the world stage.<sup>57</sup> The Chinese political pop artists did learn from and partly emulate aspects of Western and particularly US recent art and mass culture. Warhol-type ambiguities and paraded banalities characterized much of Chinese political pop's own "content processing." It remained, however, a part of the wholesale commercialization of art that Huang Du attacks. Like Warhol's products, it remains unclear how "critical" rather than celebratory or neutral Chinese "political pop art" actually is of the things it depicts, though the faint/feint smell of satire and coded attack is there in many of these works. It may well be that this ambiguity was a necessity, in order for its producers to avoid censorship, and worse, from the government. (It was a necessity, also, to avoid use of the pictorial rhetoric of orthodox Chinese Communist Party socialist realist painting, except in parodic-satirical terms.)<sup>58</sup>

But, again in market terms, it was also a necessity for Chinese artists to meet the expectations and visual knowledge of buyers from within the recent Western era—for, since the 1960s, the "cool" ambiguities and seductive banalities of mass-cultural imagery, sometimes dressed up as "complexity" and "multi-accentuality," have been requisite elements of "serious" art in the neo-avant-garde tradition.<sup>59</sup> This requirement developed based on a Cold War-era rejection of both the political posturings of the Surrealists, half of whom had joined the Stalinist communist parties of Western Europe in the 1930s, and the reactive asocial individualism of the American Abstract Expressionists, whose works later became tainted by this painting's now well-known role as an insidious propaganda vehicle for US state department interests during the later 1950s and 1960s.<sup>60</sup>

Yue Minjun's 1993 painting *Goldfish* sold at the same New York auction as *Bloodlines* in 2007 for US\$1.38 m in a sale of 300 lots that made, in total, US\$25 m. This work shows a man laughing sardonically as he appears to watch

![](_page_146_Picture_2.jpeg)

**Figure 11** Zhang Xiaogang *Bloodlines: Three Comrades* (oil painting, 1994). *Source:* © 2016 Zhang Xiaogang.

a fish swim below in the moat of a castle. But his eyes are closed and his serially repeated image diminishing into the distance seems to signal a comprehensive inauthenticity of the world, vision, meaning and image itself. By that year, the market for Chinese contemporary art (sometimes branded less politically directly as "East Asian painting") had become "mainstream," with important sales also taking place in London and Hong Kong. Though Christie's and Sotheby's still dominate world sales of Asian art, the number of mainland Chinese auction houses continues to grow. Taiwan-based firms, for instance, developed a specialized role in selling to mainland Chinese buyers works by Chinese artists that they acquired on the international market ten years earlier.

Despite this, the majority of the global players—buyers, dealers and auction houses—are still based in the West. This reflects a continued comparative lack of interest by Chinese people themselves in buying contemporary works by Chinese artists. In 2006 and 2007, for instance, Sotheby's recorded that only 13% and 16%, respectively, of their sales of Chinese art in New York auctions in those years went to Chinese mainland buyers. <sup>63</sup> The massive growth in the rise of Chinese people recorded as "middle class" (upwards of 500 million by 2012) masks the important question of how they seek to express their wealth status in cultural terms, and what signs of "cultural capital" they seek out and why.

Certainly, as a group they are responsible for huge purchase of worldwide "luxury goods" such as designer clothes, bags and cars. A 2012 survey suggested, for instance, that 44% of all luxury goods sold in Britain are bought by Chinese visitors. <sup>64</sup> Perhaps this explains the *Goldfish* man's ambiguous and rueful smile, avoiding eye contact with the viewer. It is the smile of the artist who recognizes that, whatever high-minded principles might motivate his work, when it reaches the market it, too, must meet the requirements of this niche luxury goods territory. And the smile indicates that the creative artist's supposed singularity and unrepeatability is finally another advertising and branding gambit, as internally contradictory as the idea of the Citroen "Picasso" signature range of mass-produced cars. <sup>65</sup>

Sotheby's business is also an industrial concern, setting aside the rhetoric of its luxury art and antique sector interests. It has a global workforce of about 1500 staff, two-thirds based in New York and London. It sells shares in itself on the New York stock exchange, under the symbol "BID," and April 2017 had a market capitalization of US\$2.4 billion. In Great Britain, Christie's, Sotheby's, Bonhams and the Fine Art Auction Group were the four biggest auction companies in 2010, while leading world dealers and dealing galleries include New York and London-based Anthony D'Offay, Richard Green, Pace-Wildenstein, White Cube and Gagosian—the so-called "powerhouse" companies. 66

But while there will continue to be a rise in the number of mainland China-based buyers, the arts infrastructure there, as noted earlier, started out and remains comparatively weak. Because of this, the intervention of Western companies, expertise and resources was required in order to enable the internationalization and "marketization" of Chinese art to occur in the first place. However, it may be the case by 2020 that effective Western dominance of the Chinese art market could stifle and restrict the further emergence of autonomous private organizations on the mainland. The behavior of the Chinese Communist Party's national and regional government, its legitimacy in China, and changing sensitivities to the power of Western economic and sociocultural interests acting in or on Chinese territory, will also have important effects. The global art world system as a whole, then, and global Asia's sector of it, is always bordering on precariousness—as are globalization processes as a whole.

Globalization, however, as well as comprising a set of interconnected economic and social relations and institutions, is also a set of discursive claims, some amounting to a kind of intellectual "system." It is a conceptual construct and set of working hypotheses concerning how human activities across cities, countries, regions and continents have become "mutually coexistent." Ideas of "the contemporary" and "the contemporary in art" are now closely tied to this question of how people's lives, activities, meanings, products, societies and beliefs have acquired this globalized status: the appearance of what might be called timely "mutual coexistence" and "mutual coexistence" in time. These are certainly ideas fraught with analytic complexities. <sup>67</sup> But this mutual coexistence

(which may be both socially beneficial and catastrophic, as worldwide infections such as the 2015-2016 Zika virus contagion suggest) in the case of the global art world system is still fundamentally a question of the asymmetries that condition the exercise of power and resources held by those individuals, groups and organizations located differentially in the power network. The power and resources remain held overwhelmingly by Western art world interests, although they must to some degree be prepared to share some of that power and wealth in order to extend and develop markets, in China, India and elsewhere, for contemporary art.<sup>68</sup>

The idea of "contemporary," did not, of course, come into existence only in the last two decades when theories of globalization became popularized and thought to be essential to an understanding of the changed nature of the world. The contrast between "modern" and "contemporary" had become significant in much earlier accounts of the history of twentieth-century art. By the early 1950s a difference, and then a partial antagonism began to emerge between modern and contemporary art and culture. "Modern" became increasingly associated with a tradition in art traceable back to the mid nineteenth century—to a set of canonical paintings and critical ideas—that had come to seem either "dated" (even clearly "out of date") or that had actually ended, as part of a concluded past.69

This much bigger question of "the modern" bears directly and very importantly on the world, and on the global art world, that emerged after the end of the Second World War in 1945. The end of that war unleashed a surge of "decolonization" in the still Western-controlled or dominated societies across the globe—a process that occurred in both relatively peaceful and very violent ways, especially in Asia. The creation of the nation-states India and the People's Republic of China in the first few years after the end of the Second World War instance this global transformation. 70 But this process occurred at the same time that the United States and NATO on one hand, and the Soviet Union and the Warsaw Pact countries on the other, attempted to create the totality of a single world system based on two Cold War camps. China's anti-colonial revolution, its social development and recurrent disasters and crises under Mao and the Communist Party was also conditioned by that world system's power network as it extended across the Pacific and Indian oceans, as well as by the PRC's complex and mutating relationship with the Soviet Union and Soviet Communism.

The heralding of its "Open Door" policy began when Mao famously met President Nixon in 1974—an entente designed to create a powerful ally, or potential ally, for China and the United States against the Soviet Union. Since then, China's political and economic relations with the Western powers have been under permanent redefinition and reconfiguration, and their future form remains hard to anticipate.<sup>71</sup> The PRC's internal political and legal structures, still permanently managed by the Communist Party attempting to dominate

the global capitalist forces it has unleashed and welcomed, remain a key source of potential destabilization. In relation to the growth of the indigenous art market in particular, discussed in Chapter 1, China suffers from a highly complex tax structure restricting the export of artworks—even to Hong Kong. This structure works to undermine the confidence and ability of Western companies wishing and trying to do business in China. The ambivalence of the Chinese government authorities on the question of the meaning and value of contemporary Chinese art—its place in China, for the Chinese people, and in the world—is mirrored arguably by the ambivalence Chinese artists and critics themselves feel about the meaning of their own labors and products in a global context.

Weiwei's sculpture/installation *Fountain of Light*, exhibited in the dock at Tate Liverpool, England, in 2007 as part of its exhibition "The Real Thing: Contemporary Art from China," reconstructed one of the great utopian modern monuments of the early twentieth century.<sup>73</sup> This was Vladimir Tatlin's 1919 "Model for a Monument to the Third International," planned to be a colossal combined sculpture and building that was to house and symbolize the institutions fighting for global communism based in Russia, the center of world revolution in 1917. A 7-meter chandelier of light, Weiwei's work was a kind of late "political pop" item from China—a satire, a joke, even a celebration, perhaps, of a revolutionary and egalitarian spirit that was supposed to have also infused the peasants' uprising in China.

This work might itself be read as a fetish, or as a critique of a fetish, of revolutionary art in our deeply counter-revolutionary times. Its "pop" credentials might be underlined in its own evident, ambiguous processing of an image. Tatlin's model was lost in the 1920s and survives only in reproduced grainy black and white photographs. But Weiwei's *Fountain of Light* was itself rendered into image. By placing the sculpture at some distance on the water, meters from the quay and away from close contact or touch, this tangible object lost most of its three-dimensionality and was experienced by viewers in Liverpool as an ethereal sign or beacon, a shining on of a light that represents a socialist transformation unaccomplished in Russia and China.

Fountain of Light might be interpreted to offer a sardonic critique of the lack of freedoms in China that are assumed to be normal in the West, for artists, and everyone else, under capitalist-democratic systems. But Chinese "political pop" crudely but clearly indicates that what were thought of or claimed to be irreconcilable systems can and have, in fact, often been reconciled and normalized everywhere: neoliberal global capitalism equals consumerism shackled to an eroding or absent democratic accountability. The globalized art world, and the place within it for global Asian countries and artists, encapsulates these reconciled if permanently unstable combinations, but perhaps also represents aspects of the likely future for all of us, everywhere or not at all.

The next and final chapter considers the status of contemporary art on global Asia's far western periphery, in the Palestinian Authority or State of Palestine. Could anything reasonably describable as an art world system exist here, given the extremities of the societal and political antagonisms active in this territory since the State of Israel came into existence, along with China and India, in the late 1940s? Or might the specific crises in Palestine-Israel relations, often fought out across the myriad borders that divide and connect their peoples, actually reveal some of the deepest structural characteristics of the global world order/disorder?

- representation of social activities and relationships—a very loaded way of perceiving the order and purpose of human relations and interests. The claim, for instance, that health or education provision is essentially a matter of "markets" indicates this clearly. But what if anything is truly outside of "the market," once the full range of factors influencing or conditioning "buyers" and "sellers" is truly taken into account? "Market" has thus become the preferred name—a reductive, partial abstraction generated within neoliberal discourse—for what used to be called "society" or "social formation," holistic and imaginative terms drawn from Enlightenment sociology and social theory. See Jonathan Harris, *The Myth of the Art Market* (IB Tauris: London, forthcoming).
