5

## Contemporary Art and Post-National Identities in the State of Palestine

We fly into Ben Gurion airport, a few miles east of the suburbs of Tel Aviv, on the same day that the pope arrives in Bethlehem from Jordan, the first time a pope hasn't entered the West Bank from the State of Israel. The Jerusalem Post article I've just been reading insinuates that the pope had been effectively hijacked by Palestinian Authority officials and made to "pay an unscheduled visit to the security barrier just outside Bethlehem" – the matter of propriety is a pervasive and corrosive preoccupation here. Travelling with a Palestinian who also has Israeli and British citizenship can be disorienting. Bashir Makhoul, an artist who relocated to England in 1989, never really gets to arrive "home" wherever he lands up. We fiddle with the seat belts and I think ahead to our talks about contemporary Palestinian video art in Jerusalem and Ramallah. "Welcome to Israel" the air stewardess pontificates over the intercom, as if it was her country. But the real Pope, praying at the "security barrier," has just blessed what he called the "State of Palestine." No place like home?

#### "Palestine is Open for Business"!1

The Palestinian Authority was constituted following the signing of the Oslo Peace Accords of 1993 and 1995 by representatives of the State of Israel and the PLO (Palestine Liberation Organization), in a deal brokered by the United States and the then Russian Federation. While it was generally assumed to be agreed that the objective of these accords was to enable "self-determination" of the Palestinian people, senior Israeli politicians did not believe, and said so at the time, that this would or should require the creation of a Palestinian state. Out of these accords, however, did develop a process of attempted "normalization" of relations between Palestinians, their official representatives and the State of Israel. For "normalization" understand absorption of Palestinians—their fragmented economies, lands and resources—into the neoliberal global

capitalist world order/disorder (the world's "normal" now, as my earlier chapters have shown). The global contemporary art world in Palestine could only emerge, if at all, as a creature intelligible within this broader system as it took root in the local conditions.<sup>2</sup>

This process was and is facilitated primarily via three determining conduits: (1) Israel's political-economic security state; (2) international aid provided mostly through "non-government organizations" (NGOs); and (3) resources of the shatat, the diaspora of Palestinians, along with others, who've wished to invest in the "era of peace" putatively ushered in by the Oslo agreements. In November 2012 the United Nations organization officially recognized the PA as a non-voting "observer member" state of the UN General Assembly. By September 2015, 136 of the 193 member states of the United Nations had recognized the existence of an entity called the State of Palestine. I shall use this name when referring to events after November 2012. Pope Francis incurred the annoyance of the Israeli government because he recognized this new status. explicitly using the term "State of Palestine" in his visit in the summer of 2014.<sup>3</sup>

NGOs and the dissemination of aid for which they are responsible worldwide now constitute a variant of the globalizing development business. At the same time, most of these agencies, which are US- or Europe-based and get their funding from governments and private charitable sources, provide a means through which the donor states and private donor organizations are able to pursue their political interests overseas. The "aid" and "development" help offered is a mixture of money, material resources and training for local people in establishing and running public services and commercial activities.<sup>4</sup> The United States, for instance, has given US\$400m in aid to the PA/State of Palestine since the death of Yasser Arafat, Leader of the PLO, in 2004. Total external financial support to the PA/State of Palestine since 1994 has been in the order of US\$18 billion.

In 2006 the PA was ranked the 11th "most aided" territory in the world, with over 60% of the money it received going to security, health and education areas. More than 130 NGOs work in the State of Palestine on "civil development" areas, including some specifically dedicated to the inculcation of democracy, culture and the arts. Khalil Nakhleh, a Palestinian commentator who previously ran an NGO, has observed that since the end of the Second Intifada—the Palestinian popular uprising against Israeli occupation in 1999-2001-an "almost total dependency on external (primarily Western) sources of aid" has come about. Nakhleh describes the Palestinian aid regime as a form of "cultural re-engineering of a new crop of depoliticized and managerial NGOs." These work closely, he notes, with their counterparts in the new private commercial sectors hatched since the Oslo agreements were signed.<sup>5</sup>

The Israeli company "Better Place Israel," for instance, was engaged in 2011 by the PA to provide electric cars for the territory. Its investors include USbased multinational financial services corporation Morgan Stanley and the former World Bank president James Wolfensohn. Its CEO is the former Israel Defense Forces (IDF) commander Moshe Kaplinksi, who was in charge of repulsing the Second Intifada and oversaw the Israel Defense Force's re-invasion of the West Bank. The Portland Trust, another private sector corporate developer active in Israel and the State of Palestine includes as one of its executives Lord Freud—a former vice-chairman of the Swiss UBS Investment Banking organization, one of the main sponsors, as I discussed in Chapter 1, of Art Basel's fairs and now also a provider of mortgage loans for Palestinians.<sup>6</sup>

Aid supplied by Western nation-states through NGOs essentially constitutes a form of reward handed out in the State of Palestine to those who are deemed to continue to acquiesce to the terms of the Oslo peace agreement. Conversely, aid has been withdrawn—for instance, from school-building and other projects in Gaza—as soon as armed resistance recurs, or when, for example, the US government designates certain organizations in receipt of aid, directly or indirectly, to be "terrorist" in nature. In one sense, civil society in the State of Palestine is this network of activities and social relations that the NGOs have largely created and maintain. The system has produced a hybrid class system, with distinct political and bureaucratic layers overseen by the State of Palestine's "Development Center." This is formally known as The Palestinian Coalition for Integrity and Accountability, or AMAN (which is itself an NGO).

In another sense, the NGO system has effectively supplanted the Palestinian organized mass movement against the illegal Israeli occupation of Palestinian territory, as set out in UN resolutions 242 and 338 (following the regional wars in 1967 and 1973). There are now 170 000 people working for NGOs in the State of Palestine and their salaries go to support 1.5 million dependents out of a population of 4.5 million. These jobs do not belong to a substantive productive local economy; according to Nakhleh they could actually be seen as a kind of "hidden unemployment." Though many of the NGOs do conduct important work on issues to do with human rights and the empowerment of women, jobs and activities in others are allocated, Nakhleh claims, on the basis of factional loyalties tied to the Fatah government administration. The gross domestic product of NGO aid is largely what he calls a "non-productive industry" of conferences, workshops, seminars and forums that proliferate towards the end of each fiscal year by which the annual budgets have to be spent. 8

Through the NGO system the State of Palestine has been allowed to become part of the global corporate capitalist political economy. As such, this system has become the substitute for any genuine liberation that the Oslo agreement might have been thought to promise the Palestinians—although its critics were numerous, fierce, articulate and comprehensive long before the deal was signed. From their perspective, the PA/State of Palestine has done nothing more than conform as well as it can—given the chronic history of violence and occupation in the region—to the development of global capitalism everywhere since the early 1990s. This has involved, as previous chapters have noted,

a combination of targeted state interventions, the privatization of public assets and extension of "free markets."

Both complementing and contrasting with the NGO system in the State of Palestine has been the emergence of a "public sector" economy, especially in and around the commercial center of Ramallah. This "public sector" consists of state monopolies in, for example, telecoms, electricity, cement, petroleum, cigarettes and flour. Although these industries and services appear to be run by the Fatah government they are basically state holdings in commercial companies that distribute goods overwhelmingly made in Israel. The State of Palestine has also been opened up to regional and global corporate penetration. This is based on open and sometimes disguised Israeli and shatat capital investment. It has led to the eruption of the same kinds of private debt-based consumerism that fueled the US and European banks' collapse in 2008. Conversely, there has been virtually no development of an indigenous agricultural sector or strong local industry for the Palestinians.<sup>10</sup>

The PA's economy since 1995 has come to constitute a part of what the historian Carroll Quigley in 1968 called the world capitalist system. This was built by the dominant Western nation-states and its key transnational financial institutions, such as the Bank of International Settlements, originally formed in 1919 and based in Basel (the home of Art Basel since 1970). This entity was, and remains, as Quigley characterized it then:

a world system of financial control in private hands able to dominate the political system of each country and the economy of the world as a whole [with] central banks acting in concert by secret agreements arrived at in frequent private meetings and conferences. The apex of the system was [...] the Bank of International Settlements [...] a private bank owned and controlled by the world's central banks which were themselves private corporations.

The Palestinian capitalist class, as Nakhleh terms it, operates through a network linking Egypt, often-blitzed Gaza and Israel itself. The Arab Bank, run from Jordan, is the other main player channeling and controlling the *shatat* stake in the State of Palestine 11

#### **Grounding the Culture Industry in Palestine**

Remember the qualifying plurals I inserted at the beginning of this chapter: Palestine's lands and economies. These are necessary because the geospatial and sociopolitical reality on the ground is that the Palestinian territories are chronically fragmented across the complex and interwoven "Security Areas" designated A, B, and C created as part of the Oslo agreement. These areas have dictated which military, police and civil forces control territory, resources and development of all kinds inside Israel "proper," the PA/State of Palestine, and the West Bank zone. The latter includes Jerusalem, Israel's self-declared "eternal" capital, occupied illegally, according to UN resolutions, since the war in 1967. Gaza, meanwhile, is a wholly geographically non-contiguous Palestinian territory—run by Fatah's rival, Hamas, until 2014 when the two organizations entered into what was termed a "unity government."

Israel withdrew all its citizens from Gaza in 2005 but the territory's borders, communications, and in/outflow of resources remain controlled entirely by Israel, except for secret tunnels dug under Gaza's southern border with Egypt, a country which has signed numerous security and other agreements with Israel over several decades. <sup>12</sup> The Oslo agreement naming conventions and security responsibility allocations loosely disguise the basic reality that the State of Palestine, despite the latter's self-declarations of autonomy, remains a "dependent state" tied to Israel for virtually all basic amenities and services, including access to water and energy, TV, radio and phone communications signals, airspace, all border controls and its currency. Even most of its tax revenues are collected directly by Israel from the Palestinians who live and work in State of Palestine territories. <sup>13</sup>

Attempts at societal "normalization," despite and yet dependent on these conditions, have enabled the erection of a façade of Palestine's entry into the neoliberal global world order/disorder. Quite literally, in that this is what, for example, the Grand Park Hotel in Ramallah—with its counterpart in Bethlehem, proclaiming that it is "proud to be the first Palestinian hotel chain"—really stands for. From the open-air restaurant and swimming pool area high above Ramallah's burgeoning skyline of office buildings and apartment blocks, there is a very good view of the extent of the Israeli "security fence." This is what the Israelis call what is in fact a towering concrete wall that has been erected on the Palestinian side of the border, annexing massive areas of land for reasons of "security" that always favor Israeli settlements, businesses and communications. Nine kilometers to the north of Ramallah is the site for the proposed new town of Al-Rawabi, a risky corporate investment development plan entirely dependent on Israeli permission to build an artery road between it and Ramallah, over "Area C" land which the State of Israel controls. 14

The Grand Park Hotel is part-owned by a government company of the State of Palestine. It recommends itself as a "5 star" luxury venue to its clients, many of whom are Arab and Gulf states businessmen doing deals in Ramallah. The hotel is another link in the apparently secure chain of high-quality hotels now stretching across greater, globalized Asia. *Layalina Palestine*, a glossy magazine provided free in all the rooms at the hotel, visualizes the aspirations and character of the "5 star occupation" that the State of Palestine is able to offer its wealthier citizens, investors and visitors. 

15 Layalina Palestine is intensely Westernized and secular—full of sexy Arab women dressed in couture, with big hair, big nails,

cosmetics and bling. The adverts and info-ads exemplify the commodification and spectacle that the neoliberal image-economy promises and which this State of Palestine attempts to emulate.

Layalina Palestine has adverts for Hublot watches and "Big Bang Ferrari," but also for the "Palestine Motors Co Ltd.," offering a "New Space Star Economic but Quality Compact." This might be read as a kind of unintended satire on the Oslo agreement that left the PA with little actual land but a lot of hot air above it (only 2.7 % of the total land mass was given over to sole PA control). Almost every page features Palestinians represented as carefree consumers, enjoying their new wealth and freedoms at weddings, parties and business events. There are ads for whitening cosmetics, Italian food, baby milk powder and breast cancer awareness. Photo-stories feature groups of friends dancing and singing, camping holidays (no refugee camps, but "Refutrees"—a feel-good story about tree planting to improve the urban environment), tourism experiences at the Dead Sea, in Jordan and sepia photos of Old Palestine and old Palestinians. "New Revolution no more frustration—just Wash and Go," says a shampoo advert, and a kind of symmetrical consciousness washing seems to be at work in the pages here too. <sup>16</sup>

Lavalina Palestine doesn't omit direct discussion of the political situation entirely. This edition included an essay on the Israeli separation wall along with documentary photographs and reports on a State of Palestine Ministry of Tourism and Antiquities "Universal Peace Day." At this event, as the photographs demonstrate, volunteers wore T-shirts emblazoned with the ambiguous slogan "Palestine Volunteers." But if the magazine washes or bleaches out the historical knowledge and symbols of Palestinian political struggle from contemporary consciousness in favor of spectacular consumerism, body narcissism and a standard neoliberal worship of material affluence—achieved or desired—then the deeper, repressed realities seem to emerge in the language and imagery of spectacle itself. Fantasies of space, power, control, luxury, corruption and potency spout copiously forth in the ads already mentioned. These seem to be lurking and implicit somehow even in the contingent names of the Hollywood films selected for Palestinian movie multiplex consumption: Planes, Gangster Squad, Jobs. The first multiplex cinema in Ramallah, the Palestine Tower Cinemas, which opened in 2011, is located at the "Palestine Trade Tower" in Al Ersal Street, part of the city's growing business district.<sup>17</sup>

Across the separation wall, on the eastern side, now "Palestinian side," of Jerusalem in the West Bank, where Israel continues to encourage Jews to settle and to appropriate land, against repeated UN petitions, there is Palestinian Art Court—al Hoash. "Al Hoash" is Arabic for courtyard. This is a visual arts gallery and cultural center dedicated to the history and future development of Palestinian art and society. <sup>18</sup>Formed originally in order to work to provide a museum home which could become the focus for appreciation and study of the Qupty Art Collection of over 250 works by Palestinian artists, Al Hoash has

since radically reconceived and expanded its objectives. This has partly occurred through its supportive dialogue with European institutions such as the Herning Art Museum in Denmark and Tate Liverpool in England.

Al Hoash has defined its mission as seeking to explore the historical and contemporary relationship between Palestinian art and the globe, utilizing its collection, but also organizing talks, symposia, new art commissions and publications in order "to connect to the world." Given the history of British mandate rule in Palestine after 1919, the creation of the State of Israel in 1948, the subsequent Nahkbah (the Arabic term for "catastrophe") when about one million Palestinians were displaced and dispossessed by Israeli occupation and settlement, and the continuous wars, invasions and collective violence up to and since the signing of the Oslo Peace Accords in 1993, it might be reasonable to assume that Al Hoash's local conditions and perspective bears little comparison to the work of most of the other art galleries, museums, art fairs and biennials in greater Asia discussed in earlier chapters.

But, to the contrary, the evidence is actually that the history of this part of the "Middle East"—far western global Asia—has significant parallels with the sociopolitical circumstances out of which contemporary art worlds have developed in Hong Kong, South Korea, India and the PRC. In all these states or substate territories, Western imperial regimes had been imposed and were all finally ended through popular struggle against the colonizers. Liberation came about through a mixture of organized armed mass resistance to imperial power by "proto-state" forces, as in, for instance, the PRC and Korea; decolonization process agreements, as in, for example, India (although the history of 1948 Partition with the co-creation of the state of Pakistan (and later Bangladesh, in 1971) was extremely violent); and through "terrorist" insurgency, in, for example, British Mandate Palestine, as various then-illegal Jewish paramilitary organizations sought to destabilize and end British rule. The situation in contemporary Israel/State of Palestine in many ways constitutes a radical continuity in this long history, with some anachronistic novelty lying rather in the persistence of the occupation of one colonized people (the Palestinians) and territory by another (Israeli Iews, including some Iews also colonized alongside the Palestinians under Ottoman and then British mandate rule). The State of Israel continues to refuse to negotiate a final settlement with the Palestinians it has colonized since 1948.

Given the continued military and settler occupations in Israel/State of Palestine territories, in the West Bank and in Gaza, the broad questions Al Hoash has asked about the context, meaning and purpose of historic, modern and contemporary art made by Palestinians are intrinsically and inveterately political. These include what was described in one statement as:

The question of Public (social) space, what is it? [...] How to act in the public space? Is it a (manifestation of) Power over the space and over the groups? A question of representation and memory of landscape? A place for the community? [...] We seek the development and elevation of art in recognition of its role in development, free will and expression of people and also as a constituent and representative of national and cultural identity through innovative approaches.<sup>20</sup>

Al Hoash, in a manner similar to Seoul Museum of Art (discussed in Chapter 2), has had to subject its own character and role to such critical, and self-critical, considerations. What, for example, should be the function of a contemporary visual arts institution that supports the struggle for the achievement of an autonomous Palestinian state within the State of Palestine as it present exists. following the Oslo Accords and the neoliberalization of Palestinian territories under the tutelage of Israel?

Al Hoash's recent answers to this question sound in many ways like the ambitious claims made on behalf of the organizers of Kochi's first biennial. Al Hoash will, one of its position papers explained, undertake a range of activities—curatorial, educational, research, community-centered—that will create what are called "synergies between visual culture, economy and the [Palestinian] society." These activities, defined as "research-based." "critically driven, community linked, educational, collaborative and open to the public" aim "to engage and integrate cultural work with the community as a vector for urban development, economic growth and social enhancement."21 But Al Hoash's stress on the relationship between Palestinians and the whole world—on, that is, "Palestinian" understood as a now globalized condition and experience—marks a fundamental departure from longstanding Fatah policies based on an assumed PA "nationhood" and "sovereignty" which remains conditional on the Oslo agreements, and therefore on a State of Palestine dependent on Israel for its existence and future development.

#### Palestinian Museums-of-art-in-waiting

Al Hoash, in fact, asks the radical questions that now face all nation-states and peoples living under the conditions of our globalized world order/disorder though the specificity of the Palestinian situation adds some extremities of definitional complexity and existential danger. The key questions include the following.

(1) What does it mean and what is it to be Palestinian, or to want to be Palestinian, if you happen to live in Jerusalem, or Ramallah, or in Gaza, or in Tel Aviv? In each case quite different politico-legal and geospatial relations to the State of Israel exist.

- (2) What constitutes "Palestinian culture" or "Palestinian art" in each of these places, if anything, and what might or should? In the latter case, the situation is perhaps the most problematic. This is because within the State of Israel "Palestinian" is not recognized as a legally possible identity—Palestinians living in Israel are dis/identified generically by that state as "Arabs."
- (3) How have these questions of identity, origin and meaning been answered historically, while the unending crises of colonial occupations, wars, dispossessions, settlements and intifada have continually reformed and transformed and deformed the lives and lands of Palestinians since 1948?<sup>22</sup>

Michael Hardt and Antonio Negri, influential commentators on the processes and consequences of globalization, believe that the whole world has become newly precarious because of the impact of those forces that have undermined the autonomy, identity and powers of nation-states and their citizens. Nevertheless, for Hardt and Negri, it is only through more globalization that a future possible liberation of all peoples, those they call the "multitudes," may occur, in their final freeing from both the still powerful nation-state system and the global capitalist world order. <sup>23</sup>In particular relation to Palestinian struggles for independence, Hardt and Negri laid down a kind of gauntlet.

"As soon as the nation begins to form as a sovereign state," they claim,

its progressive functions all but vanish. Jean Genet was enchanted by the revolutionary desire of the Black Panthers and the Palestinians, but he recognized that becoming a sovereign nation would be the end of their revolutionary qualities. "The day when the Palestinians are institutionalized," he said, "I will no longer be at their side. The day the Palestinians become a nation like the other nations, I will no longer be there." With national "liberation" and the construction of the nation state, all of the oppressive functions of modern sovereignty inevitably blossom in full force.<sup>24</sup>

While it's not entirely clear if Hardt and Negri concur completely with Genet here, it is certainly productive to consider the latter's comments in relation to the specific Palestinian form of "institutionalized [...] nation like the other nations" which the Oslo agreements, in the circumstances of global neoliberal capitalism, have produced. Likewise, all the case-studies in this book have raised diverse questions germane to what Genet calls "the oppressive functions of modern sovereignty"—in Hong Kong, South Korea, India and the PRC—which the neoliberalized, NGO-dependent State of Palestine shackled to Israel has now taken on. This is true especially given precisely how "modern sovereignty" has been reconfigured in these disputed territories through the processes of state capitalist-led globalization since the 1990s.

Formed in 2004, Al Hoash initially embodied what appears to have been a conjoined "conservationist" cultural and sociopolitical objective. In art-museological terms, the organization was created in order to secure an appropriate physical home for the Mazen and Yvette Qupty collection of over 200 works (mainly paintings) that constituted what was seen by many as the basis for a Palestinian "national collection" of art. The Qupty collection was described, for instance, in a statement published by Al Hoash entitled The Palestinian Art Museum Ierusalem as containing artworks embodying "the human cultural image of a nation with roots engraved in history."<sup>25</sup>This museum-in-waiting was presented as a kind of evidence for the authentic national statehood that the Palestinian people both already embodied in spirit and to which they also aspired in reality.

This incipient State of Palestine aspired to emulate the way that authentic nation-state building, from the nineteenth century onwards, had included Western countries (e.g., Great Britain, Germany, the United States) building and filling national museums of culture and national galleries of art, housed in capital-city based, purpose-built neoclassical structures designed to suggest favorable continuities with their image of Roman and Greek civilizations.<sup>26</sup> Decolonization in the late 1940s had led to the newly emerged nation-states of India and Israel itself (along with several others) moving to create their own versions of such state museums and galleries—precisely in order, their statist cultural elites believed, to entrench and validate their position in the world of formally autonomous nation states that the United Nations organization, itself established in 1945, explicitly sought to recognize and bring together.<sup>27</sup>

In sociopolitical terms, The Palestinian Art Museum Jerusalem project was intended to entrench and validate the assumed proto-nation state created by. and as, the PA, following the Oslo agreements. The Al Hoash document goes on to outline key aspects of the intended museum. This building was to be located in Jerusalem (the Palestinian nation's de facto capital, irrespective of Israeli occupation) and would be what was called "the medium through which the Palestinian national cultural memory is preserved and maintained, thus allowing it to take its rightful place within the global and historical narrative of national museums."28 This definitive statement was followed by another explaining that the museum-in-waiting would serve all Palestinians, including those living in the State of Israel created in 1948, as part of what was called "the creative endeavours of Palestinian artists in the West Bank, Gaza and Jerusalem, as well as Diaspora artists."

This suggests that the deliberately disaggregated areas created by the Oslo accords comprising "Palestine" were nevertheless being ratified by some Palestinians as a kind of normal, or at least "normalized," condition of contemporary Palestinian life. These fragments were not seen, that is, as the chaotic and contingent historical product of all the wars, occupations, resistance, subsequent reprisals and further colonization visited upon Palestinians across the territories that, since 1995, had come to constitute the Security Areas designated A, B and C. An actual Palestinian Museum under construction on land in Birzeit, sixteen miles north of Jerusalem and within Security Area A, built on land controlled wholly by Palestinians, was due to open in May 2016. Statements on the web site explaining the genesis of this institution suggest a critical understanding of the purpose of museums similar to the intellectual perspective of Al Hoash. This Palestinian Museum is funded by the Welfare Association, an amalgam of wealthy *shatat* Palestinian individuals and groups founded in 1983.<sup>29</sup>

But given the "Israelification" of Palestinians living in the State of Israel, the effective sequestration of Gaza by the IDF when its people voted for a Hamas government in 2007, and continued Israeli occupation and extension of settlements in the West Bank, including in east Jerusalem, the material substance of the State of Palestine has melted into air, to paraphrase Marx. With Oslo agreement Area A territories wholly controlled by the PNA constituting only about 3% of the land, Area B territories operated with mixed Israeli/PNA control comprising about 27% and Area C, where Israel operates all civil and security control save that over Palestinians at 70%, it is not surprising that the demographic integrity of the nascent State of Palestine—in political and social, as well as cultural and artistic, terms—comes to seem almost wholly impracticable, if not simply a product of wishful thinking.<sup>30</sup>

Conversely, the global population of Palestinians—that is, all those peoples and their descendants living in the rest of the world outside of Security Areas A, B and C, including all those living or born in Israel proper, as well as in sequestered Gaza—comprises an overwhelming majority, thus vitiating both the idea, and the ideal, of a "State of Palestine" constituting a specific, actual single national place and time of being. Remember, too, that in the State of Israel a law was passed in 2011 preventing any government funding of activities organized to mark the *Nahkbah* (the catastrophe of dispossession of Palestinians during and after the Israel—Palestinian war of 1947-49). The State of Israel, indeed, celebrates what Palestinians call the *Nahkbah* as its triumphant "War of Independence" from British rule. The "Present-Absentee" law passed in 1950 entitles the State of Israel to continue to accumulate the property and assets of Palestinians who remained in the country after 1948.

In one sense Al Hoash's plan in its *Palestinian Art Museum Jerusalem* statement seems to envisage the role of Palestinian artists to be that of imagining and depicting this integral nation-that-does-not-yet-exist and which really could not, given the conditions set by the Oslo agreements that effectively ratified the Israeli occupation and division of the West Bank and Gaza territories. In that "conservatory" sense, then, as the Al Hoash statement notes, "Palestinian artists play an essential role in the preservation of their heritage, culture, and historical past." In Jerusalem, since its occupation in 1967, it notes, "Israel has been implementing a systemized policy against the eastern part of the city and

its residents, aiming at eliminating its Palestinian Arab identity." Given these conditions, Palestinian Jerusalem has only been able to develop a poor "underdeveloped cultural infrastructure" and one under increasing threat as Israel attempts to take over more and more of its land.<sup>33</sup>

So, as Israel expands its settlements into eastern Jerusalem and areas in the West Bank, it might be assumed that the potential for the creation of a material, real, viable State of Palestine continues to contract. Nevertheless, as I've noted, the Welfare Association inaugurated the building of the "Palestinian Museum" in 2013. This institution might be thought to constitute a kind of cultural "Higgs boson particle" of teleological statist historicization—though its financial, political and administrative relationship with the Fatah government is undoubtedly complex. The museum's theoretical, though soon to be actual, existence is intended, that is, to offer a "proof" of the reality and authenticity of the State of Palestine, in the face of all the facts on the ground. <sup>34</sup> (I do not intend to suggest that this museum should not be built or will not be a useful thing to have been built; I am interested here rather in its symbolic significance.)

Al Hoash had become aware of the mutating meanings and purposes of museums under globalized conditions. The organization had sent its staff to study new "mission statements" and experimental practices at the Whitworth Art Gallery in Manchester, at Tate Liverpool and other influential institutions in Europe and elsewhere.<sup>35</sup>Al Hoash noted what it called "the continuous changes in the meaning of the museum and its transformation from a static space into a more interactive space that serves both local and international audiences." In the case of the Palestinian diaspora, this meant that the vast majority of Palestinians themselves were already part of these "international audiences," already effectively part of the global multitudes.

But if the State of Palestine had originally been constituted after the Oslo agreements with financial support from the troika of Israeli state and commercial backers, shatat capitalists and external NGOs, then Al Hoash, too, had been offered and accepted funding from a range of partisan international donors. These included the Welfare Association, the United Nations Development Programme of Assistance to the Palestinian People, the EU, some European states and private supporters including Price Water House Coopers, which describes itself as a "multinational professional services network" company.<sup>37</sup> This accountancy and audit global combine, operating in over 150 countries, is the fifth-largest privately owned company in the world and works to support the privatization of state-owned and state-run organizations including industries, hospitals and universities. In 2012 it was fined £1.4m by the British government for falsely reporting that the J. P. Morgan investment bank had complied with rules regarding the protection of its clients' own funds. Five years earlier, before the financial Crash, Price Water House Coopers was criticized by the UK Treasury Select Committee for malpractice in its dealings with the British building society Northern Rock, just before it went bust.<sup>38</sup>

## The "NGO-ization" of the Contemporary Art World in the State of Palestine

The State of Palestine's Grand Park Hotel chain is another expression of this neoliberal globalized world, with its promise of seamlessly available "luxury with pride"—providing the "modern standards" and "superior facilities" that the weary world business traveler now expects to find at the end of his or her busy day, be it one that ends in London, Delhi, Beijing, Tel Aviv and now also Bethlehem or Ramallah. The global contemporary art world has a minor but constitutive role in this totality too. It provides the possibility of more luxury in the form of high-quality cultural experiences (via the biennial exhibition and the art fair), a potential means of financial investment for High Net and Ultra-High Net Worth individuals and corporations (via the art market and dealing galleries), or simply an aesthetic means for the suitably cultivated or broad-minded to relax and enjoy some precious recreational time away from their business meetings appreciating contemporary art.

Or, as the Grand Park Hotel brochure puts it, this businessman, when he can, should enjoy his moment of "glory, success, and creativity." And Ramallah, the State of Palestine's burgeoning commercial and government center, now offers what one commentator has called its own highly "localized cosmopolitanism" (also known as "the Ramallah syndrome"). The Grand Park's sister hotel, meanwhile, is located in "downtown Bethlehem"—like Ramallah, in Security Area A, where it is illegal for Israeli citizens to enter, although the Israeli army sometimes operates there in order to arrest suspected militants. The Bethlehem Grand Park provides what the brochure describes as "outstanding service in the spirit of the city of peace [...] where Magis and Kings from the East have followed the star to where Jesus was born." Surely this is the epitome of the State of Palestine's "Five Star Occupation" experience: what Susaina Maira calls the surviving "colonial present" within neoliberal capitalism.<sup>39</sup>

How have other visual arts and cultural organizations operating in this State of Palestine been shaped by the Oslo agreements and their consequences? Sounding disconcertingly like President George W. Bush when he infamously declared that victory had been achieved after the US invasion of Saddam Hussain's Iraq in 2003, the then Palestinian prime minister, Salam Fayyad, announced in Ramallah in 2011 that another "mission had been accomplished." The Palestinians, he declared, had succeeded in creating the conditions necessary for the State of Palestine to exist. <sup>40</sup> It was no coincidence that Fayyad had previously worked for the World Bank and that this State of Palestine would be constructed along global neoliberal principles tailored to the specific conditions of continued occupation by Israel, Oslo agreement security and control protocols, and Palestine's consequent "dependent state" position. Fayyad's state building project derived from the 2008 "Palestinian Reform and Development Plan," an apparent blueprint for "free market" neoliberalism.

But while capital, industrial and commercial developments led by Israeli state and private business sectors along with *shatat* interests began to flow into the PA during the mid-1990s and continued in the new millennium, what might be called the sociopolitical gestation of the Palestinian territories has effectively been managed through "NGO-ization." This is the work done by a veritable "industrial-complex" of international aid and development agencies, linked to key donor states such as the US and Norway, and to organizations of states such as the United Nations and the EU that fund, channel, direct, monitor and review the activities of the Palestinian state apparatuses and other civil society institutions.<sup>41</sup>

NGOs have also been created by, and work for, corporate transnational agencies, such as the World Bank and the Ford Foundation, as well as for Arab/ Islamic corporate and transnational agencies, and for UK charities. The Welfare Association operates its own group of limited companies which have been established as monopoly businesses in the PNA, and are effectively run as tax-exempt charities following the American model developed by corporations such as Ford, Rockefeller and the George Soros Open Society Foundations. What might be described as a too-happy symmetry of business and "sociocultural development" interests has characterized the Welfare Association's activities, according to Khalil Nakhleh. For example: given the continued Israeli building of settlements in eastern Jerusalem, the Welfare Association valuably raised US\$28m from the Arab Fund, the EU and UNESCO ostensibly in order to purchase land and fund building renovation specifically for Palestinian projects. But it contributed no money itself and instead channeled the sums it had raised into the investment funds of the Welfare Association's own commercial corporate backers.42

To take another example, Nakhleh shows that the Welfare Association's 1997–2011 NGO project organized directly with the World Bank—focused on the West Bank, Jerusalem and Gaza—converted what had been radical political objectives centered on land reclamation, opposition to Israeli land confiscation, housing and income generation into depoliticized healthcare, education and legal services provision. <sup>43</sup> The NGO run by the EU's European Commission Technical Assistance Office in Jerusalem funds all EU education in the State of Palestine, part of what Nakhleh calls an "absolute dependence" culture in which the Fatah leadership has entered: an alliance of regional politico-financial interests superintended by the NGO's managerial culture, all "under Israeli occupation and the overall consent of the USA."

Cultural NGOs working in the State of Palestine include the Sharek Youth Forum, based in Ramallah but also active—erratically, given the intermittent high levels of violence—in Gaza, funded by USAID. My focus here, however, is on visual arts organizations rather than on these more socially influential NGOs concerned with the lives of younger generations of Palestinians. Sunaina Maira has compellingly written about these in her study of music, performance

and youth subcultures, *Jil Oslo* ("jil" is Arabic for "generation").<sup>45</sup> The language of NGOs operating in the State of Palestine is highly significant and embodies the international governmental effort above all to manage the Palestinians living in Security Areas A, B and C into a form of administered peace and cohabitation, under a neoliberal capitalist social order superintended by Israel. This is the apparently benign argot of "development needs," "human rights enhancement," "democratization" and "conflict resolution." Palestinian curator and critic Rawan Sharaf has noted, however, that the social system encouraged by such foreign nation-based aid has replaced the building of indigenous livelihoods and autonomous political capacity by Palestinians (highly limited by Israeli occupation and military power) with the imperative of what she calls the "vicious circle of securing funding."

The intended, if still only partial, result of NGO-ization has thus been a radical depoliticization of Palestinian society under a Fatah-dominated state apparatus subservient to Israel and its key international allies. Nevertheless, the work of the NGOs, in helping to "professionalize" artists and other groups of cultural workers has had, Sharaf observes, positive as well as negative effects. In the mid-1990s, immediately following the Oslo agreements, the upbeat "peace," "tolerance," "coexistence" and "reconciliation" agenda led to some Palestinian artists and groups working with Israeli cultural organizations. New, and hybrid, Palestinian arts organizations emerged. These included Al Wasiti, the first art archive (closed in 2000), the Al Mamal Foundation for Contemporary Art, founded in 1998 and the still extant Sakakini Cultural Center in Ramallah, connected to the *shatat* A. M. Qattan Foundation (registered as a UK and Palestinian charitable organization).

Post-Oslo, these organizations tried to begin to foster a kind of indigenous Palestinian Authority "contemporary art"—based in performance, installation, video and multimedia. These are the forms of global art, discussed in previous chapters, popular with biennial exhibition curators around the world though not, according to Sharaf, with most local Palestinians, with whom the new visual arts agencies in the main failed to engage. Along with and utilizing the globalized idioms of contemporary art production, a number of Palestinian artists living both inside and outside of the State of Palestine have become renowned on the global biennial and museum circuit, joining the pantheon of Asia's global contemporary artists. It is not my purpose here, however, to enhance their already considerable fame and significance.<sup>47</sup>

Some NGO-funded art colleges opened after the Oslo agreements, such as the International Academy of Art-Palestine in Ramallah, which, along with Al Najah and Al Quds universities, are the only higher education institutions currently offering formal qualifications in art practice in the State of Palestine. Other organizations for visual arts and culture have been inaugurated since the 1990s, including Al Hoash, Art School Palestine (a residential/arts exchange project, not an education institution), the Yabous Cultural Center, Dar Annadwa

and the Peace Centre based in Bethlehem. 48 But no art market could be sustained across the Palestinian territories during the 1990s as there were no commercial galleries—with the exception of Anadil, founded in Jerusalem in 1995, which later morphed into the Al Mamal Foundation. The A. M. Qattan Foundation, run with shatat capital from Kuwait and Lebanon, had been founded in 1993 to support Palestinian art and culture, and it continues to maintain centers in Ramallah, Gaza and London. The Foundation also organizes an annual Young Artists Award.

Gallery One, located in Ramallah and the first commercial gallery to open for many years, was started in 2014 by the director of the not-for-profit Art School Palestine. 49 Development of the Palestinian Museum has been financed, as mentioned earlier, by the Welfare Association. Some sales of contemporary artworks have been conducted by the United Nations Development Programme to support artists living in Gaza, and Al Hoash has worked to organize annual art auctions in order to raise money to support local artists. There are some avid and wealthy private collectors of Palestinian art active in the global markets across western Asia, as well as in Europe and the United States. 50

The NGO-ization of civil society in the State of Palestine has fundamentally reformed the definition and character of the workers (of virtually all kinds, including artists, curators and administrators) who access such funding. In one direction. Sharaf notes that since the Oslo agreements their identities have been reshaped into that of "clients" rather than autonomous, self-directed producers. In another, Maira concludes that successful Palestinian artists, including musicians and dancers, have been refashioned by what she calls "neoliberal models of individualism that have detached artists from the collective spaces of cultural production" and links to popular struggle. The most promising were groomed for connection to a global arts market, its publics and the international funding structures available via participation in events such as biennials and the patronage of powerful museums. This is clearly a political and ideological as well as a cultural and social "reframing" process. The League of Palestinian Artists, established in 1974 and connected closely to the Palestinian Liberation Organization (PLO) during the 1970s and 1980s, progressively lost influence following the Oslo agreement when the Palestinian Authority took over from the PLO, became the local "ruling politico-bourgeois class" and the process of depoliticization by "NGO-ization" began.<sup>51</sup>

Through a combination of the ostensibly humanitarian work of NGOs and capital directed from both Israel and shatat interests into the State of Palestine the territories have been prepared for and penetrated by neoliberal global capitalism. "Industrial Zones," for example that at Beit Hanoun on the Gaza-Israel border, supported by NGOs and international banks, have enabled Israeli businesses to source low-paid Palestinian labor employed without reference to the labor laws of either states in order to manufacture goods for export, not to develop the State of Palestine's own societal livelihood. These workers are forbidden to join

unions and they labor under military control, with security arrangements subcontracted out to the PA's own soldiers and police.

Industrial Zones have become a common feature of the global Asian neoliberal capitalist economy, operating sporadically, as noted in Chapter 2, between North and South Korea (as well as in many other countries in the region, including the PRC and the Philippines). The NGOs involved across Asia operate what Naomi Klein has called a form of "philanthropic imperialism," often also providing intelligence to the US military. Colin Powell, US secretary of state for defense during much of the 2000s, described NGOs as "an important part of our combat team," while Klein has observed that they act as "the charity wing of the military silently mopping up after wars."

In the State of Palestine the NGO-led "development" environment, symbolized by the Industrial Zone, has normalized and legitimized the continued Israeli occupation and domination of Palestinian lands, resources and its people. They have become dependent upon it, as well as exploited by local and absentee Palestinian capitalists (there was actually an increase in urban poverty in the PA between the years 1993 and 2008). The result, according to Nakhleh, has created:

exploitation, monopolies, misuse and corruption of available funds [which has secured] dealerships for non-productive consumer goods and services from regional and international companies, conducting business deals with Israeli capitalists to expand the profit base for both sides, with the support of the entire gamut of the dominant colonial structures, the control and possession of economic assets that PA bureaucrats managed and regulated earlier, through coalitions, often fabricated, by their capitalist allies.<sup>53</sup>

The separation wall's construction, for instance, along with that of checkpoints, terminals, settlements and roads inside the occupation, was the work of the Israeli Nesher company which has been given an effective monopoly in the State of Palestine (as well as being responsible for producing 85% of all concrete used in Israel). The Palestinian Authority government pays "rent" for the concession to dispense these Israeli goods through its holding companies directly into Israel-based banks. <sup>54</sup>

Concrete, however, had no role in the construction of two of the most ostentatious mansions built by wealthy Palestinians that exemplify gross inequalities within the political economy of the State of Palestine. Rashad Al-Shawwa's so-called "Pyramid," officially titled The Shawwa Cultural Center, was built in 1985, nearly a decade before the Oslo agreements. It is located in Rimal, Gaza, the town of which Al-Shawwa was mayor for eleven years, appointed then under effective Israeli tutelage. The mansion was built with Welfare Association funding disbursed from Geneva and included an extensive library facility for

public use which remained empty and unused for five years. Ignoring the skills of local craftspeople and indigenous materials, the mansion was made entirely from materials imported from Europe.

Munib Al Masri's "House of Palestine" mansion was built in Nablus, a Palestinian town 30 miles north of Jerusalem. In 1994 Israel allowed Al Masri, along with PLO leader Yasser Arafat, to return to Palestine. Al Masri worked as a liaison agent between the Fatah and Hamas parties, and also acted as a messenger for the Israeli government. Al Masri's "House of Palestine"—a Palladian-style mansion overlooking the Balatta refugee camp built in 1950 which is now home to 30 000 people—was also built entirely with European materials during the Second Intifada. A kind of pseudo-monument to religious and ethnic tolerance, the mansion includes a central structure, the "Dome of Tolerance," a tribute to Islam, Christianity and Judaism. Al Masri, dubbed the "King of the West Bank," is reputedly the world's richest Palestinian, with personal holdings—including a collection of European artworks—worth, it has been estimated, a third of the value of the State of Palestine. "

#### **Concrete into Dust**

The Gaza-born artist Tayseer Barakat runs a café in Ramallah. Barakat's catalogue of images and texts, published as *Dust, Dialogue, Iron*, was exhibited at Ramallah's Al Mahatta Gallery Station in 2009. This composite work presents a series of imagined/imaged voices and people coupled with some photographs and artworks. The latter are a mixture of drawings and shallow-relief prints. <sup>56</sup> In virtually all cases the images suggest the substantially obliterated remains of people killed violently and subsequently recognizable only through abstracted silhouettes. They resemble the disturbing image-like trace outlines of incinerated bodies of those killed by the atomic explosions at Hiroshima and Nagasaki in 1945. <sup>57</sup>

The people depicted in Barakat's images seem wholly anonymous as well as abstract. They have themselves, in death, become abstractions, apparently twice over. The actual individuals killed have been represented, abstractly, only as facial, head or partial body silhouettes. Then, within the context of the catalogue, they have been rendered (I construe) into abstractly representing all Palestinians killed in the conflict in the Middle East. Children are often depicted in Barakat's prose, but again, although sometimes given names, these appear to be meant as abstracted representations or "typifications." One text, set alongside such images, reads "At night, I cry my mother [...] My name is Shahd the girl of fifteen years" ("I am Shahd from Palestine")". It is not clear if these short pieces of prose are based on actual individuals whom Barakat knew personally or came to know about through other sources. <sup>58</sup>

These images in some ways resemble ancient Egyptian painted wall depictions—abstracted profiles of heads and shoulders, like the hieroglyphics

one sees on the walls of pharaohs' tombs or on coins. No identifying detail remains—the images are flat and flattening, mostly in outline, with the quality of what looks like rough sand constituting the figure with the background in roughened black, although this form also evokes a kind of sand texture. In another image (none have specific titles and there is no pagination in the catalogue) a schematic face in frontal view looks out at us—with just the dark slit of one eye, part of a nose and half a dark lip identifiable. It is as if the whole human being's form has been sucked out of the image, leaving only a kind of residual sediment of shape and former animation: "I am 12 year old Amer from Deir El-Balah. My father cried me since the deafening sound until my new colours." The latter reference might make sense in relation to the face with its life sucked out. A kind of peacefulness, or pacific release, characterizes this image.

The text "Mariam from Izbet Abd-Rabbuh in Gaza" is accompanied by a beautifully rendered profile of a woman, or young girl, almost, or apparently, in motion—flattened, reduced to schematic outline, but also seemingly at peace. Other, ordinary, color photographs interspersed with these drawn and relief images show exploding bombs, or an actual child carrying loaves of bread on his head. Others—whitened and overexposed to an almost X-ray appearance show parts of what look like several dead bodies of children neatly lined up for inspection or burial. But no potentially identifying faces are to be seen—their individuality is withheld. A focus on limbs instead shows the bodies as if in sleep rather than, or as, death. "Are you in Somalia or do you live in a faraway Indian village?" another text asks. Some of the references suggest historical as well as contemporary events. One reads "I am Maria, the daughter of one of the Taifa Kings [...] Born in Malaga in 1218." The silhouette drawings are also like relief maps—schemata of territories, the end of land, the end of territory, its insides and outsides. A final X-ray type photograph appears to show three small children lying dead in a line or trench—again as if asleep, in a state of peacefulness, in this case their bodies and clothes apparently unblemished. An adult kneels behind them, again faceless, reduced to lower limbs.

Dust, Dialogue, Iron shows and rejects death, and the worlds that have created it. The people imaged as dead seem to live again, to transcend their deaths through "their" words or through the words of someone else—speaking a kind of testimony to the fact of their deaths and the situations in which these deaths occurred. The Gaza situation, the abject "Philistine"/Palestine view, is close by, present and depicted graphically. "The basic levels of experience that motivate art are related to those of the objective world, Theodor Adorno noted, "from which they recoil. The unsolved antagonisms of reality return in artworks as immanent problems of form."59 Barakat struggles to show the life form of specific humans from which life has been removed but not extinguished, nor forgotten.

I am very grateful to Rawan Sharaf for her help and advice in preparing this chapter.
