## Conclusion: Motifs of Global Fracture in the Art Of Bashir Makhoul and Wang Guofeng

7.30 a.m.... In the taxi again, back from Manchester Airport: gentle drizzle and grey skies of the English northwest. The global cities of Hong Kong, Beijing, Seoul, Delhi and Tel Aviv fade as we proceed through the small towns of Cheshire to my home in Congleton: diminutive market and commercial center in farming countryside. Medieval and now modern, relatively un-gentrified; only verging on the "contemporary" as far as the ads in the windows of the real estate agents are concerned. Safe in England?... Congleton had been a focus for Oliver Cromwell and the republicans in the Civil War—the regicide John Bradwell kept a house here: "modernization" centuries ago, English style, with its share of violent death, social chaos and rearranging the world order/disorder... The key in the door, the door closing, time to unpack.

#### Re-Entering the Maze

Step outside this global contemporary art world.

Back inside Makhoul's installation *Enter Ghost, Exit Ghost* I might have felt lost, but I knew that I wasn't really. For this construction was, from its beginning to its end, an artwork, an installation, in a gallery space—all familiar and quite safe things. But the work-as-maze was asking me to consider the possibility of entertaining radical doubt regarding my "disposition," although that doubt was also supposed to be entertaining, a form of diversion. Consider some of the historical and current meanings of disposition: "The action of getting rid of or making over"... "power of disposing, control" ... "The action of ordering or regulating by (esp. divine) right or power" ... "The stationing of troops ready for attack or defense" ... "A frame of mind; a mood" ... "The fact of being set in order; spatial arrangement; relative position, esp. of constituent parts". *Enter Ghost, Exit Ghost* sought to divert me, but not only for the purpose of entertainment. It led me to consider my position.

![](_page_184_Picture_2.jpeg)

![](_page_184_Picture_3.jpeg)

Cover image: Bashir Makhoul *Enter Ghost, Exit Ghost* (2012, mixed media installation). *Source*: © Bashir Makhoul.

The term maze in Middle English usage referred to a mental state of "delirium, delusion, disappointment"—only later did it come to mean a physical structure of paths and passages "arranged in bewildering complexity." A "maze of streets," therefore—such as an Arab Kasbah or traditional Japanese *machizukuri* warren

of urban passageways—might be an unintended outcome of human construction practices, rather than one conceived from the outset as a form of diversion. The psychological condition of "amazement" meant "loss of one's wits or self-possession; mental stupefaction; bewilderment, perplexity." Later it accrued the exacerbated senses of "overwhelming fear or apprehension" and, later still, "overwhelming wonder, extreme astonishment." Note these extremities of human reactions: overwhelming wonder, astonishment, fear and apprehension—suggesting a consciousness, a position, turned upside down; a world order disordered.

Human consciousness and self-consciousness is inherently situated, or seated, in material existence. Though scientific and philosophical debate on the question of the definition and location of "mind" remains intractable, it does not and cannot exist outside of the physical body and brain. Without the physically situated organs of sight, touch, hearing, taste and smell, consciousness can exist—sense-deprived or sense-bereft patients are sometimes kept alive through intensive care procedures—but individual humans could not survive independently in the world. Perception abilities and consciousness, therefore, are bound up together in practical life. The information gained through these senses informs, and helps to form, consciousness and selfconsciousness: our sense of who, what, where and why we are in the world. Practical in this sense does not only mean "useful in everyday life tasks" and should not be opposed to notions of theoretical or speculative activity. Our senses of why we exist, in certain places at certain times—for instance, what the value and meaning of our lives are—are inherently bound up with the nature of those places and times; that is, with their "conjunctural" significance.4

The sequences of "lenticular" panels which made up the walls of the maze that was Enter Ghost, Exit Ghost created the sense of a consciousness, a position turned upside down. Though I knew I remained in an installation, an artwork, in a gallery, these panels—projecting a series of photographic images as I walked pass them—exhibited images that appeared, then disappeared, and sometimes seem eerily to coexist in my perception of them. Because the images changed as I moved past each panel, I became highly aware that perception, and acts of consciousness and self-consciousness sequentially related to these perceptions, are inseparable from my own physical and bodily disposition. The term "lenticular," as I explained in the Introduction, also has its origins in late Middle English usage, where it referred to a lens-like shape. The root of this term, lens, lies in the Latin for "lentil"—a small rounded seed cultivated for food in Mediterranean countries. A lens was a transparent substance, such as a piece of glass, with one or more surfaces "curved or otherwise shaped to cause regular convergence or divergence of light passing through it."

Enter Ghost, Exit Ghost, even before I began to interrogate the kinds of imagery projected through its panels, was asking me to consider the nature and

"situatedness" of human perception and its relation to consciousness and self-consciousness. How do we see the world and why do we see it that way? These are two of the most basic questions ever to have been asked by philosophers. To the duality and dialectic of human mind "perception-consciousness" a third term, and problem, should be added: perspectival understanding. Having a "perspective" means to see something from a particular place or position, and implies a recognition that, from that place or position, some things will be/can be seen and other things will not/cannot be seen. Place or position in this sense, it should be clear, means not simply a physical or spatial location affording a view (thought it necessarily is that), but also a correctible hypothesis, or, alternatively, a principle or belief that is held to be axiomatically true—such as that, for example, "all humans are equally precious", or that "the Land of Israel belongs to the Jews because God gave it to them for all eternity."

In contrast, an example of a "correctible hypothesis" would be that if you moved to the left of your current position you would see something that is currently not visible, or that, for instance, lack of clean water can create the conditions for disease. These two propositions are what philosophers have called heuristic (from the Greek "to find"), meaning their claims are subject to trial and error revision based on the accumulation of further evidence which can lead to a change in perspective. Scientific research is generally thought to proceed through such empirical procedure or testing. In contrast, statements of belief are sometimes also called ineffable, from Latin, meaning "too great to be expressed in words." Note that there is a sense and motor link between human consciousness, expression through language and discovery in the world. One might have begun to find a way through the maze of *Enter Ghost*. Exit Ghost by making heuristic claims about where one is and has been—to avoid, for instance, endlessly revisiting the same place. The claims could have been tested through movement and then further reconsideration. Of course, the purpose of a maze built to confuse is precisely to confound such rational and heuristic action—to defeat consciousness and self-consciousness. The other course of action, however—movement based on ineffable principle suggests lack of communication between those trapped in the maze and a belief that divine will is guiding them.

Seeing images mutate in *Enter Ghost, Exit Ghost* as I moved through the maze, correcting or confirming my hypotheses of where I thought I was, or had been, or wanted to be, was constantly a matter of changing perspective. Whether to turn "left" or "right"—but left or right in relation to what fixed or achieved position? Whether to turn, or to return; or to hold this position? Would a "road map" perhaps have helped with the direction I wanted to go in? We enter here the geometry of radical doubt. Have some of my thinking and moves so far seemed "obtuse"? In this maze what images could I have seen, or

remembered, or thought I remembered seeing, from a plane angle greater than 90 degrees and less than 180 degrees? Would a 90-degree or less change in direction have been a good idea? Would that have been to the right or the left? The ghost of an image I glimpsed once in the maze suggested I had returned, unwittingly, to a position I'd occupied before, suggesting I hadn't found a real way forward. The route was obscured, the light was bad, although the lenticular panels projected more images, more ghosts of a past, present or even future moment in movement

Enter Ghost, Exit Ghost alluded to Shakespeare's play Hamlet and the dramatic-symbolic vehicle the spectral image in the play constitutes, but it also brought to mind another of the metaphors of the eponymous character's own bewilderment. In a conversation with Polonius, Ophelia's father and servant to King Claudius—the man who Hamlet suspects has killed his own father and married his mother—Hamlet facetiously debates the shape of a cloud, which seems to resemble, they agree, a camel, then perhaps a weasel, and, yet again, rather a whale. 5 But Hamlet becomes as disoriented by this exchange as he had hoped Polonius would be; his own state of mind is fearful, full of apprehension, and possibly delirious. There is, in meteorology, the genus of the "lenticular cloud": lens-shaped, occasionally iridescent, and glittering or flashing with an array of colors which changes as the observer moves.

#### In Girum Imus Nocte et Consumimur Igni

The maze that was *Enter Ghost*, *Exit Ghost* stood some comparison with the architecture of checkpoints and border crossings that both link together and divide Israel and the State of Palestine. This shift in focus makes sense because the shifting photographic images in this artwork, this installation in the gallery space, depicted past and present moments in the cities and towns of Palestine and in the nation-state of Israel. They represented the presence, within these imbricated territories, of another people whose own home and place. Palestine. once existed, but whose images and symbols are also now a matter of ellipses, shifting shapes, places, meanings, peoples and plans. Take the Qalandiya crossing between Jerusalem and Ramallah in the West Bank. This particular maze operates five stages in the circulation of its temporary occupants. These channels link iron turnstiles, X-ray gates, more turnstiles, inspection booths, and then X-ray machines for bags. Palestinians negotiating this structure are hailed by the Israeli guards and other security personnel by loudspeaker and move through its locks controlled by remote controlled gates. The glass panels enabling Israeli surveillance and operation of the crossing are so thick that they prevent the Palestinians seeing the humans responsible for their transit through or detention in it. The architecture of this structure instructively embodies the hieratic architecture of visibilities and invisibilities that the Israeli state has created, maintains and constantly refines in its dealings with the Palestinians. Enter Ghost, Exit Ghost was both a meditation upon, and a simulation of, this structure, or "logic of the visible" in Israel, and its connections to power and Palestine.

Ariel Sharon, Israeli soldier-hero from the 1967 war, later minister for new settlements and then Likud prime minister, once indicated the conditions and relations that should pertain, he believed, between visibility and Israeli power. "Arabs," he declared, "should see Jewish lights every night from 500 meters." The settlements he conceived and planned were designed to institute a visual dominance of and over the "Land of Israel"—they were to be an inveterate reminder of Jewish predominance ("dominance over") and Palestinian subordination, "under the gaze" of their rulers. This architecture of the Israeli state, its physical and legal structures, is essentially asymmetrical: rules of engagement issued to the occupying forces at the end of 2003, for instance, in the wake of the Second Intifada, or *up*rising, stipulate that soldiers may shoot to kill any Palestinian caught observing settlements with binoculars or in any other "suspicious manner." The act of looking, with the implication of the licit or illicitness of what can or might be seen by a particular individual or group, has itself been deemed a crime punishable by summary death.

Looking, seeing and representing are interactive processes—in legal, political and artistic practices. Enter Ghost, Exit Ghost as an artefact itself articulated all these processes through its serial lenticular imagery and its bewildering maze of paths leading to the seeming opacities of the cardboard city at its termination. What can be seen or not seen is dependent on where you are, but also on who you are and how your own perspective has been formed. There are many antecedents in art's history. John Constable, the English landscape painter, depicted the working people of East Anglia as only marginally visible in his great paintings of Suffolk—their represented size, such as the mother nursing a baby in *Dedham Vale* (1828) reflected his sense of their background relative insignificance within the countryside he both loved and owned. 10 Visibility is a part-synonym for the notion of transparency: the clarity associated with candor in social and democratic political life. The glass cupola built above and into the debating chamber at the Reichstag building in Berlin after it once again became the seat of the unified German parliament after 1994 was designed literally to embody and metaphorically figure this supposed transparency to democratic discourse—as if democracy could, or should, actually be seen to be happening.

Maya Ying Lin's *Vietnam Veterans Memorial*, erected in Washington, DC in 1982 turned the rhetoric of visibility in another direction. Consisting of a long rising black marble wall containing the names of all the American soldiers killed in that conflict, this structure actually throws back at the viewer the

visibility of their own image looking at the Wall, as much as they simultaneously trace the names of their loved ones carved in the stone in front of them. The wall functions as a kind of "anti-memorial" to a war of invasion and occupation that was lost in 1975, and against which a majority in the United States eventually emerged. Enter Ghost, Exit Ghost, though focused on Israel and the Palestinians, actually connected to this longer and broader history of regional and global conflict within which the Cold War and the Vietnam War are both implicated. The maze of these interconnections is political and ideological, but also cultural and aesthetic. I have demonstrated in this book that the development of the global contemporary art world since the 1990s is not intelligible without understanding the still active legacies of the Cold War-across the Middle East (western Asia) into the subcontinent, to "Southeast Asia," and on to China, the Korean Peninsula and Japan.

In Girum Imus Nocte et Consumimur Igni/"We Go Round and Round in the Night and are Consumed by Fire." This palindromic, maze-like, sentence—in Latin the letters from both beginning to end and from end to beginning spell the same words—was a favorite of Guy Debord's, the author of Society of the Spectacle. 12 His book sought to describe and explain the direction society had taken in the world since the mid-1920s, when forms of spectacular diversion (in cinema—with the advent of synchronized sound-imagery—advertising, urban development, and in culture and the arts generally) began to become integral to the social orders of both the Western democratic capitalist societies and the Soviet eastern European state-communist countries. For Debord, the stand-off between these two power blocs had, by the 1950s, generated a form of deadly coexistence and mutual interdependency. Some of these Cold War dead ends and impermeable borders that were products of this interdependency have featured in my analyses here of five postcolonial societies, territories and nation-states that emerged since that conjuncture.

Though certainly in conflict and competition with each other through the alliances established by NATO and the Warsaw Pact states, these two power blocs had effectively carved up the world and its resources of people and land into competing and coexisting "spheres of interest." The Berlin Wall, erected in 1961 to prevent citizens from East Germany crossing into the zones of the city controlled by the Americans and their allies, expressed and deepened the Cold War division of Berlin and Germany within the superpower conflict. Bulgarian artist Christo erected his own Iron Curtain-Wall of Oil Barrels, with official permission, in the Rue Visconti in Paris a year later, in what was an early artistic commentary on the aesthetics and politics of the Berlin Wall. Seemingly ambivalent or "neutral," this practical, temporary, division of a street by a high stack of oil barrels both roughly imitated and yet might be thought to have offered a critique of the Berlin Wall itself—though the meaning of the action in blocking the Paris street for a number of hours overnight still resists any simple or singular reading. The structure the artist built, however, focused upon an act and situation elsewhere—the construction and maintenance of the Berlin Wall—which had both reflected and produced extremely powerful practical, ideological, social and emotive effects, within that city and its population, divided Germany, Western Europe and the Cold War world beyond.

Christo's Iron Curtain worked through a "distancing" device which both literally separated—physically dividing the street in Paris—and drew attention, metaphorically, to the action of the Berlin Wall, using its different formal materials "poetically," via a verfremdungeffekt ("making strange") device. 13 Enter Ghost, Exit Ghost performed a congruent action, suggesting a reconsideration of the sociopolitical meanings of quotidian terms and preconceptions such as "walls," "doors," "roads," "barriers," "partitions," "beginnings" and "endings"—all, in fact, different kinds and extents of borders. It did so both to entertain and to prompt critical thought, referencing and utilizing—like the "affect" (sense or mood) devices of movie spectaculars and CGI technologies—the evolved machinery of a now global spectacle to achieve its own effect. The socalled "making strange" devices associated with twentieth-century avant-garde artistic practice included juxtaposition and dissonance in compositional (ordering) strategies. The lenticular panels central to the experience of *Enter* Ghost, Exit Ghost materialized this principle, combining as they did historically disparate and opposed images—pasts and presents of Palestinian life held visually together within acts of vision which themselves mutated as the spectator moved along the walls that channeled their progression through the maze. The "maze" here, however, refers as much to the fate of the Middle East within the logic of the Cold War and to the history of the Palestinians since their own 1948 Nahkbah, as it does to the actual warrens of paths in their towns and their playful-vet-serious simulation in Makhoul's diverting installation. We go round and round in the night and are consumed by fire. The maze is global—that is clear now to all.

#### **Utopian Photo-Mythologies**

I saw Wang Guofeng's "North Korea 2011" digital photographs *Number 1, Part 1* and *Number 1, Part 2* in his studio in Beijing, displayed and manipulated on the artist's computer screens. Other very large-scale photographic prints had been attached to the walls. *Number 1, Part 1* shows an unidentified public building, perhaps in Pyongyang, its truncated façade directly front to the camera lens—in the style in which Walker Evans photographed sharecroppers' shacks in the United States in the 1930s. (The master print of *Number 1*, showing the building in its entirety, has a very large print size of 905 cm  $\times$  450 cm.) This view eliminates any building recession detail and has the effect of flattening—in physical but also metaphorical ways—our sense of this substantial material structure. It appears to be "all front", all façade, with no "depth".

The large portrait of a somber man in a blue suit (one "Dear Father" or another, I assume) hanging from the second floor, directly in the middle of the facade, reinforces this flatness. The frontality of the portrait sitter's pose further emphasizes the whole composition's enigmatic "out-of-time-ness": the building and people in front of it, those working digging the road and a band playing music, appear oddly "frozen" too, although this quality is hard to articulate intellectually. It's more of an amorphous feeling, or saturating tone, than anything else. The vacuous blue sky and sense of physical, environmental wintry coldness (a roadie to the right of the band has his hands dug into his pockets) underlines this.

Because Guofeng was able to increase focus within images such as this on screens in his studio—digitally enhancing detail down to extraordinary levels of sharpness apparently anywhere on the photographic image as a whole—my sense developed that he had produced not really separate, or even separable, pictures, but rather a system or ecology of visual forms: from the building shape to the workers' body shape, as those two elements enter into a symbiosis of meaning and inter-reference. Clicking back and forwards from a wider shot to a detail produces this effect/affect in this viewer. Other groups of photographs by Guofeng also produce such a system, such as his *Ideality* (2006–2007) and *Utopia* (2007) series, consisting of 12 images of public building facades in Beijing and Moscow respectively. They have the look of typology sequences. meaningful only in categorical relation, oddly frozen from other references outside the system. This quality of "frozenness"—perhaps Guofeng's chief signature in formal terms—is a kind of visual correlate of Roland Barthes's myth "syntagm," the structure of linguistic elements, drawn from his use of Saussurian linguistics theory. 14 Guofeng's photo-mythologies seem to enact a parallel formal, analytic suspension of other codes and references, and of the referent, "the world out there". Yet his photographs do seek to explain and understand the world—this situation.

But step back now from Guofeng's photographs and consider briefly the historical relationship between Western capitalism and Eastern communism. as this developed during the twentieth century. I have been concerned in this book as much with the kinds of stabilities and interdependencies that have formed within globalization in the post-1945 era as with the instabilities and aggression that threatened the future of the world during the Cold War that supposedly ended in 1991. The early 1930s saw, on the one hand, the beginnings of Western states' official recognition of the Soviet Union (by the United States in 1933, for example) and, on the other, emergence of the global geopolitical division which persisted, in varying forms and at varying levels of antagonism and danger before and after the Second World War, up until the end of the Soviet Union. 15

Debord's account of the reduction of all social life to what he calls spectacular domination by the economy (whether this is identified ideologically as "capitalist" or "communist") is certainly bleak. Perhaps this is more understandable given his own biographical proximity both to the rise of the Nazis in Germany and the dictatorial state controlling the Soviet Union during the 1930s and 1940s that claimed to be authentically "communist". In succession to that, the era of the Cold War embodied, for Debord, the domination of two interrelated forms of spectacle enshrining the global victory of modern capitalism, prior, however, to the emergence of neoliberalism in the 1980s. These were what he called the "concentrated" variants, centered on cults of authoritarian personality, that developed in the Soviet Union and later in China and North Korea, which represented a victory over the revolutionary workers' movement, while the "diffuse" forms of spectacle in the Western societies extended capitalism's "culture industry" powers that had been honed in the fascist period in central Europe.

Capitalism's actual worldwide predominance since the 1950s—in either its Soviet/PRC/North Korean "state-bureaucratic," or in its Western "democratic," forms, in the United States and Western Europe—had been founded, according to Debord, on a practical "integration of state and economy," a trend that could certainly be traced further back in the twentieth century, but which had become "at the very least the motor of all recent economic developments." Their actual institutional combination, in whatever varieties of empirical, historical modes that Debord believed stretched from the United States to China, was, he said, in all cases a union "highly favourable to the development of spectacular domination." <sup>16</sup>

Two factors in particular, however, isolate recent globalization from earlier nation-state systems and related internationalization processes—though not from the history of imperialist wars and incursions instigated after 1945. including those conducted by US, European and Soviet governments in, for example, Korea, Hungary, Vietnam, Czechoslovakia, Afghanistan, Kuwait, Iraq and Libya. Firstly, the emergence of truly global, as distinct from merely regional, ambitions within the superpower political, military and corporate forces involved. Strategists from these increasingly interlocked "power-elite" groups in Western Europe and the United States have operated, since the early 1950s, within supra-national alliances (dominated by the latter, now sole though weakened, superpower) with systematic global aspirations and capacity for action.<sup>17</sup> Though the onset of the Cold War between the Western capitalist democracies and the Soviet Union and its allied "Eastern bloc" states might in some ways reasonably be regarded as having operated a break on globalization, from another perspective it actually created two power blocs which together, in an effectively single system of rule, were even more motivated to attempt to carve up the world and manipulate all its peoples and resources. 18 Consideration of the global system must include the status of the PRC, and that of North Korea's relation to China and the world beyond. How might Guofeng's photographs critically inform analysis of both these

social orders—to a degree interdependent, because of their geophysical proximity, though not symbiotic, given China's size and power?

## Aesthetic and Social Implications of Digital Photography

Guofeng's most literal "theater of the frozen" comprises images of the megamonumental sculptures of Kim Jong-un and his father, stood together, "cheesy" smiles in raincoats, acknowledging their people's adoration. The crowd appears like giant Gulliver's lilliputian antagonists beneath their leaders' monumental trouser-legged shoes. This "North Korea 2012" series No.3 (close-up) is a detail of No.3 ( $750~cm \times 220~cm$  print size) which pulls back to reveal father and son flanked by two enormous sculpted red flags and representatives of the people and army.

Many in the crowd—just in front of Guofeng's lens—point their own cameras toward the pair, now in the middle distance, cultish dark snowmen in front of a snowy mountain back-drop mounted on the wall of a windowless building. An actual white, featureless sky surrounds the whole event. Guofeng's photographs picture, then, semblances of North Korean "community" and human

![](_page_193_Picture_6.jpeg)

**Figure 12** Wang Guofeng *Number 3 (close-up) North Korea 2012* (still from video, 2012). *Source*: © Wang Guofeng, Courtesy de Sarthe Gallery.

interrelations; they nearly all show a "coming together" of people, and of the people with, or coming near to, the Leader. They offer, in short, a visual form to this society. The question arises: what binds this society?

Though the Cold War had entrenched and sharpened antagonistic and competing economic systems, in political, ideological and military terms (think of the Korean War), those systems were nevertheless both driven by a common logic of accumulative materialism. Contemporary North Korean public propaganda painting continues to celebrate, in "Socialist Realist" mode, this drive to industrialize.<sup>19</sup> Any society's "mode of production" is actually comprised of its primary organization of human social relationships and activities within the physical environment. Western libertarian socialists, ecological activists and many other movements and groups from the 1960s onwards came to reject in principle, and as a basis for the practical organization of a future truly alternative society, a rapacious triumphalism in the exploitative transformation of nature that official Marxism, in China and North Korea, effectively shared with capitalism.<sup>20</sup> In contrast, they believed, any desirable and actually obtainable (not simply "utopian") radically alternative future would have to be, in economic, social and political terms, self-managing and self-renewing, a livelihood lived on the basis of people primarily caring for each other. Such a society would be "need-oriented," not "market-driven," offering a form of life sustained, not determined, by its material-productive capacities, and one crucially that recognized the limits of the environment and its ecological systems.

Guofeng's images of both North Korea and the PRC offer visual analogues of the totality of these societies' contemporary modes of production. Notions of "space," "image" and "representation" are as indispensable in understanding these photographs as they are, for example, to understanding capitalism's global market as a "unified space," or of the spectacles of production and consumption, or community and devotion, understood as a sphere of "image" production. In art and society, therefore, questions of the relations between "representation" (visual/cognitive) and "reality" are posed, both explicitly and tacitly. Guofeng's photographs *No.2* and *No.2* (close-up), also from the "North Korea 2012" series, suggest Leni Riefenstahl's well-known images of the Berlin Olympic Games in 1936, which have also been understood as an image of the Nazi social order in Germany at the time.<sup>21</sup> In these two photographs by Guofeng, however, the masses seem to entirely comprise soldiers.

Once again, Guofeng is able to focus in on individual heads and military hats (belonging to men and some women) seemingly anywhere in the composition when projecting these images on his computer screens. The effect is bizarre. The "social architecture" of the nation and the army is matched by the brick-by-brick construction effect in the photograph, whose print version measures in at an enormous 2039 cm  $\times$  208 cm. Detail reveals many differentiations, despite the overall appearance of uniformity and "massification." The two photographs seem "artless" in their mere quantification, or registering of bodies,

but this is an effect of certain authorial decisions allied to the technical capacities of his digital camera and software. Everything, everyone, seems to be "in focus" simultaneously—which becomes a kind of metaphor of the ideological projection of equality and communal interconnection the regime claims to embody, literally, in these bodies. The sheer breadth of Guofeng's giant "landscape" picture—assembled necessarily in strips of photographic print paper for exhibition—manifests a kind of anti-compositional force as it has no compelling center or dynamic of forms, offering no particular place for the eye to settle, as it may as well settle anywhere. (Similar groups of shots show other sections of massed crowds, massed performers and stadiums, such as North Korea 2013No.1, 658 cm × 150 cm, which depicts an event celebrating the sixtieth anniversary of the founding of the state—Riefenstahl comparisons are obvious here too.)

Guofeng's "North Korea 2012" series No. 20 (Traffic Policewoman) is a sequence of five full length portraits, with the subjects placed on the road, appearing perhaps to half-smile to the camera. The code here is naturalistic. when compared with Guofeng's views of the massed ranks. These individuated women seem relaxed, even enjoying the attention of the camera, although the actual blankness of their expressions suggests this is yet another "reading in" made by this particular viewer. Barthes's famous discussion in Mythologies of the black colonial soldier saluting the French tricolor flag comes to mind.<sup>22</sup> These policewomen simply "stand for", show, the rightness of the North Korean social order: one sees immediately past their facades into their patriotic souls. Barthes's interest is in the spontaneous process, or appearing spontaneous process, wherein an image may produce this type of ideological effect. The image seems innocent of the semantic work it actually does—creating the connotation immediately jumping from a set of material signs to a naturalized meaning. Yet we know, given Guofeng's serial productivity, that he manufactures this visual syntagm while at the same time the photographs seem merely to reflect a pre-existing state of affairs. This is the constitutive contradiction embedded both in the photograph and in our understanding of it.

What are they watching? (close-up) is a detail of the broader landscape image (260 cm × 346 cm) with the same title, also part of the "North Korea 2012" series. Massed men in blue suits turn their backs to the camera, Magritte-style, while the center of the composition is taken up by a digital video camera, with a second to the right side of the image. They are both held aloft, like trophies, the first pointing down and the second up, towards the action off to the right. A third, a TV camera on a tripod, cuts through the top of the image. Guofeng's photograph pays homage to the technics of spectacular public visibility mobilized and projected by the regime and its media apparatus—a world where every gesture can and will be read as a sign of your loyalty and attentiveness towards the Leader. A group of geriatric generals stumble into view top right of the picture, their medals caught in the fading sunlight and spotlights of the cameras.

## The One and the Multitude in the World Order/Disorder

Guofeng's "North Korea 2012" photographs include No 1, Part 1 and No. 1, Part 2, two images that take us to the heart of this concentrated spectacular regime. No 1, Part 1, shows us a crowd reacting, animatedly, to an event taking place in the upper left of the composition (as if Christ might be descending to the people or revealing His stigmata). Hands and arms are raised high in joyous clapping, as if the mere sound of clapping would not be enough—the welcome and applause must somehow appear visible for it to be, or become, somehow authentic, verifiable. The flags above the people, yellow sickle and hammer in one, seem, improbably, to be almost clapping too, streaming and billowing dynamically towards the upper left. Another empty blue sky grounds the image. In No.1, Part 2 we see the source of their animation. Kim Jong-un is on the edge of the concrete podium, his own arms and hands raised clapping, while he appears to look—disconcertingly—directly into Guofeng's camera lens, and so at us. Two photographers closely flank him to the right, the ubiquitous public visibility of the Supreme Leader guaranteed by their image-making, while lesser dignitaries on the podium stand back at a respectable, and safer, distance to the left, again clapping and looking at Jong-un.

![](_page_196_Picture_4.jpeg)

**Figure 13** Wang Guofeng *Number 1, Part 1 North Korea 2012* (still from video, 2012). *Source:* © Wang Guofeng, Courtesy de Sarthe Gallery.

Jong-un's stare at the camera is disconcerting. His look, however, is fairly blank, and it is impossible to know what he might be thinking. He will no doubt turn his gaze slightly away from us in a second. But apparent contact with his furrowed brow—his eyes appear to be in darkness—is alarming: we have a reservoir of ideas, or rumors, about this man and what he has done and can do. This knowledge, or assumed knowledge, always accompanies our attention to Guofeng's photographs, but it is, in a fundamental sense, "blank" too. That is why these photographs, strangely, seem to produce whatever meaning one (wants to) brings to them, as though the images themselves are neutral, or neutralizing. There is a relativism to them which is also "freezing", leading nowhere except to another image and then—as they always belong to a system—back again. In Guofeng's broader "landscape" shot of the stadium (size given as 643 cm × 300 cm for the print) where long-un's appearance occurs, he attaches a slogan, reminiscent of those in Barbara Kruger's photo-text images, a kind of commentary or meta-commentary: "Abandon what you always believe; and let your brains, eyes, and mind focus on the moment of reality!" Is this Guofeng's own reading of the image, or a synoptic imagining of what Jong-un might be saying or meaning? It could be both, but neither actually carries much force. The image itself seems to me to "create" this message (or an approximation of it) automatically, somehow, between its form and my attention to it. This is Barthes's contradiction, of course. His reading of myth is clearly subjective, yet also claims truthfulness or something of the scientist's objectivity, not simply the status of one man's opinion. Likewise, Guofeng's photographs connote both, simultaneously, subjective and objective; opinion and truth.

A wider view may help, again, in how we might assess this state, this situation, of North Korea (with implications for the PRC, too). Independent, or "nonaligned," socialists and Marxists critical of the Soviet Union, both those who in some cases had had highly significant roles in the Bolshevik uprising, such as Leon Trotsky, as well as others from Russia and elsewhere, had for many decades objected, in principle, to the regime's self-designation as "communist" and, instead, characterized the Soviet Union through a variety of other names linked to particular critiques. These included "state-capitalist." "deformed workers' state," and (this sometimes intended as a condemnation, sometimes as more of a neutral description) "state-socialist." The Soviet Union was certainly identified by the nonaligned left within these critiques as, in varying degrees, anti-democratic, authoritarian or totalitarian—though its parallel and usually more hostile analysis of capitalist power and the state in the Western democratic societies often operated a damaging break on both their understanding, and public statement of this critique, of "actually existing communism" in the Soviet Union and of that in the PRC.<sup>24</sup>

Later commentators towards the end of the Cold War stressed the interlock between the state, corporate communications technologies and the "militaryindustrial complex," but stressed that there were significant variations in these combinations across different countries in the Western and Eastern blocs—questioning Debord's formulations that sometimes appear to eliminate any genuine conflict or possibility of real change within these societies. It would be reckless, for instance, to claim that the end of the Soviet Union in 1991 brought about only superficial or trivial changes for people living in Eastern bloc states, or in the Germany that reunited soon afterwards. However, all the changes that did occur in the mid-1990s and since in these countries have certainly not been experienced as good or necessary, despite hubristic claims that an abstract and total Western capitalist "freedom" had finally been brought to these societies. And for the PRC now, never mind the situation in North Korea?

Guofeng's most recent photographs, taken in North Korea in 2014, pose different sized groups of workers, children, students and others in group portraits. One assumes that in each of these seventeen pictures (though this may not be the full extent of the series) the twin portraits of Jong-un and his father hang on the wall, although we don't actually see them in every picture—they are presumably sometimes located behind Guofeng as he takes the shot. So, for example, members of the *English Class at Pyongyong International Football School*—whose faces mostly look dazed and blank—themselves presumably stare at the twin Dear Fathers facing them on the front wall of the classroom. Their faces and body demeanors seem to betray a not yet fully trained sense of how they should be—how they must correctly visibly embody, themselves, the social order of which they seem to know they are a part. The room is antiseptically white and bare, save for one or two notice boards—a blankness matched by the sitters' white shirts. The picture radiates discomfort.

In *Children's Palace No.1* seventeen younger children sit at traditional musical instruments, frontally place, while Jong-un and his pater look down, or across, at us (and them) from the back wall. The children here appear not yet socialized sufficiently to worry too much about their physical dispositions and some smile unselfconsciously at the camera. *Doctors No.1*, by contrast, shows a police identification-type line-up of nine suspects, white-clad, shifting uncomfortably in a lobby area—fully aware they are participating in a group mug shot. In *Family of a People's Scientist* (though it's not entirely clear if one of these four is the People's Scientist) the back wall contains Father and Son as well as two other photographs of the massed ranks, pictures that look not dissimilar from Guofeng's own. We see what we want to see?

Five "Space" shots in the series show rooms empty of actual people but containing the disembodied images of Supreme Leaders B and C. These images are eerie: "gloomy" and "strange," shifting towards frightening. This is because the hieratic social order seems present without any actual bodies in these compositions to embody it—the representation works mechanistically, somehow, parasitically drawing on, and in, the few objects (a teddy bear, a TV aerial) that "people" these rooms in the absence of actual people. When the people return, en masse, to the same spaces and to others (remember that all these images are

![](_page_199_Picture_2.jpeg)

**Figure 14** Wang Guofeng *Space No.1* (still from video, 2014). *Source*: © Wang Guofeng, Courtesy de Sarthe Gallery.

![](_page_199_Picture_4.jpeg)

**Figure 15** Wang Guofeng *Students of Kim Chaek University of Technology* (still from video, 2014). *Source*: © Wang Guofeng, Courtesy de Sarthe Gallery.

part of the system, the ecology of representation Guofeng has created) the effect remains strange. There is a surrealist "fort-da" game of throwing and taking back going on here, based on some warped Freudian image-economy trading with the cult of personality aspects of the regime. <sup>25</sup> Especially consider Space No.1—a room of computers emptied of bodies and University Students of Kim Chaek University of Technology, the same room filled with men at the terminals. These all look troubled—no smiles, caught metaphorically in the headlights, wondering, though apparently with no time to, which way to look or sit, what face, or facade, to project. They also look baffled, somehow aware of but not able to articulate something of the weight of the contradictions that Barthes remarked are at the heart of living. Some seem to be clenching their fists to get through this tricky situation of public and recorded visibility.

How will they seem to all the others?
